Refining the Three Significations: The Sun Objection
Objection that the three definitions are contradicted by the word 'shams' and the solution adding 'by way of conventional designation'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the sun example contradicts the definitions of the three significations and apply the condition ‘by way of conventional designation’ to preserve their distinction.

Core Idea
The three significations collapse without the restriction of original conventional designation.

The logicians’ initial definitions of correspondence, inclusion, and implication seem clear, but a single word can accidentally fit all three. The word ‘shams’ (sun) provides a classic counterexample that forced logicians to add a critical condition. Understanding this refinement is essential for correctly analysing how words convey meanings in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three definitions
The three significations are al-mutabaqa (correspondence of the whole meaning), al-tadammun (inclusion of a part), and al-iltizam (implication of an external concomitant). The original definitions did not specify that the signification must be based on the original coinage (wad') of the word.
2
Examine the word 'shams'
Assume 'shams' is coined for three things: the disk (jirm), the light (daw'), and the combination of both. The word then signifies the disk by correspondence, the light by implication (if light is considered a concomitant of the disk), and each part of the combination by inclusion. Thus one word demonstrates all three types, which should be mutually exclusive by the original definitions.
This shows an overlap that defeats the purpose of distinguishing the types.
3
Identify the flaw in the original definitions
The definitions lacked a tie to how the word was originally assigned. Without that tie, any meaning that can be mentally associated with a word could be classified arbitrarily. The logicians realized that the type must be determined by the original wad' (conventional designation).
4
Apply the refining condition
Add 'bi-tawassut al-wad' (by way of conventional designation) to each definition. Now correspondence means the word was coined for that exact meaning; inclusion means it was coined for a whole and then used for a part; implication means it was coined for something else but a necessary external concomitant follows. In the sun example, if the word was coined only for the disk, then its use for the light is implication, not correspondence or inclusion.
This condition saves the definitions from counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إيرادات على هذه التعريفات وفي هذا المقام أسئلة: الأول: إن حدود الدلالات الثلاث ينتقض كل منها بالأخريين في مثل ما إذا فرضنا أن الشمس موضوعة للجرم والضوء والمجموع. فإن الدلالة على الضوء مثلا يمكن أن تكون مطابقة وتضمنا والتزاما. فلا بد من قيد [بتوسط الوضع] في كل منها، كما فعلوا. (أي: قرص الشمس المدور، فإن لفظ شمس يطلق عليه مطابقة. (أي: المنتشر على الأرض، فإن لفظ شمس يطلق عليه التزاما. (فإن لفظ شمس يطلق على القرص والضوء معا، فدلالته على واحد منهما تضمنا. (إذن لفظ واحد دل مطابقة وتضمنا والتزاما مع وجود فرق بين الدلالات الثلاث، فانتقضت تعاريفها السابقة بهذا المثال. (فلا بد من زيادة قيد ضمن تعاريفها ليتميز بعضها عن البعض والقيد هو لفظ بتوسط الوضع). (أي: ما فعله جمهور المناطقة بإضافة هذا القيد.
Objections to these definitions. In this regard there are questions: The first: The definitions of the three significations are contradicted by each other in a case like if we assume that 'sun' is designated for the disk, the light, and the combined whole. For the signification of light, for example, can be correspondence, inclusion, and implication. So a restriction [by way of conventional designation] is necessary in each of them, as they did. (i.e., the round disk of the sun, for the word 'sun' is applied to it by correspondence. (i.e., the light spread on the earth, for the word 'sun' is applied to it by implication. (For the word 'sun' is applied to the disk and light together, so its signification of each one individually is by inclusion. (Thus one word signifies by correspondence, inclusion, and implication while there is a difference among the three significations, so the previous definitions are contradicted by this example. (So it is necessary to add a restriction in their definitions to distinguish them, and the restriction is the phrase 'by way of conventional designation'. (i.e., what the majority of logicians did by adding this restriction.)
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the three definitions of signification given earlier. The objection uses the word 'shams' (sun) as an example: if we suppose the word is conventionally used for the disk, the light, and the combination, then in the case of the light, it could be considered correspondence (if the light is the whole meaning), inclusion (if part of the combination), or implication (if it is external to the disk). Thus one word fits all three types, contradicting the definitions. The solution, proposed by the majority of logicians, is to add the condition 'by way of conventional designation' (bi-tawassut al-wad') to each definition, meaning that the signification is based on the original conventional assignment of the word. This ensures that correspondence is only when the word was originally coined for that meaning, inclusion when it was coined for a whole and then used for a part, and implication when it was coined for something else but an external concomitant is mentally associated. Without this condition, the definitions fail to distinguish the types.

بتوسط الوضع
حد

B) The signification must be by way of conventional designation
C) The signification must be external
D) The signification must be of a universal

Key Takeaway
The three types of signification are only properly distinguished when the condition of conventional designation (wad') is added to their definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: The word 'salah' (prayer) is originally coined for the entire ritual. When a jurist says 'salah is wajib', which type of signification applies to the word itself? Now, if we consider the physical actions (ruku', sujud) as parts, would that be inclusion? Explain how the condition 'by way of conventional designation' clarifies this.
?
Task: Find another example from fiqh where a single word has multiple potential meanings that could fall under different signification types. Apply the condition to determine the correct type.

The Definition of Proposition: Genus and Differentia
Author defines qadiyya and explains its genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of a proposition (qadiyya) and distinguish propositions from performative utterances.

Core Idea
A proposition is a composite expression that can be true or false; performatives are not.

In this passage, the author defines the fundamental unit of logic: the proposition (qadiyya). Understanding its definition is crucial because all reasoning, whether in kalam, fiqh, or tafsir, depends on propositions that can be judged true or false. We will parse the definition into its genus and differentia, and see how this excludes non-truth-apt utterances like commands and wishes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. It is 'القضيّة' (al-qaḍiyya). This is the focus of the definition.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The genus is 'قول' (qawl) described as 'مركب' (murakkab), meaning a composite utterance or concept. Notice the author splits it into verbal and conceptual composites. This is the broader category that includes propositions.
In Arabic, 'qawl murakkab' covers both spoken phrases and mental combinations.
3
Identify the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is 'يصح أن يقال لقائله: إنه صادق فيه أو كاذب' (it is valid to say of its speaker that he is truthful in it or false). This condition makes the expression truth-apt, distinguishing propositions from other composites.
The differentia excludes all performative utterances (inshā') because they cannot be true or false.
4
Apply to classify examples
Take an Islamic example: 'Allah is one' is truth-apt, so it is a proposition. 'Pray!' is a command, not truth-apt, so it is not a proposition. This step tests the definition.
Performatives include commands, wishes, oaths, and questions—they are not propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث) في مبادئ التصديقاتِ وهي (القضَايَا) وأحكامها. القضيّةُ ): قول يصحُ أنْ يُقالَ لقائِلِهِ: إِنَّهُ صادِقٌ فِيْهِ أَوْ كَاذِبٌ فالقولُ: -وهو المركَّب - مَلْفُوظَا جِنْسُ للقضيّة الملْفُوظَةِ، ومعقولاً جنس للقضيّة المَعْقُولَةِ). وباقي القيُودِ فصل يُخرِجُ المركَّبات الإنشائيَّة طلبيَّة) كانت وغيرها).
(The third chapter) On the principles of affirmations (taṣdīqāt), and they are the propositions (qaḍāyā) and their rulings. The proposition (al-qaḍiyya): an utterance such that it is valid to say of its speaker that he is truthful in it or false. So the utterance—which is the composite—as a verbal composite is the genus for the spoken proposition, and as a conceptual composite is the genus for the conceptual proposition. And the remaining restrictions are a differentia that exclude the performative composites (inshā'iyya), whether they are requestive or otherwise.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter on the principles of assents (taṣdīqāt), i.e., propositions (qaḍāyā) and their properties. The author defines proposition as a composite expression (qawl) that can be judged as true or false when attributed to its speaker. This definition has two parts: genus and differentia. The genus is 'composite expression' (qawl murakkab), which can be either verbal (lafẓī) or conceptual (ma'qūlī). The differentia is the condition that the statement be truth-apt, i.e., one can say the speaker is truthful or lying. This differentia excludes performative utterances (inshā') such as commands, questions, wishes, and oaths, which are not truth-evaluable because they do not correspond to an external reality that can be true or false. For example, 'The sky is above us' can be true or false, while 'Pray!' cannot.

قول مركب
إنشاء

Key Takeaway
A proposition is defined by being a composite expression that is truth-apt, which excludes all performative utterances.

Definition of the Proposition (al-Qadiyya)
Definition of proposition and exclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a proposition according to Islamic logicians, and classify utterances as propositions or non-propositions based on the definition.

Core Idea
A proposition is any utterance that can be true or false; commands and descriptions are not.

Every logical argument rests on propositions. But what exactly is a proposition? The author gives a precise definition: an utterance of which truth or falsehood can be predicated. This lesson walks you through that definition and shows how it excludes non-informative speech and restrictive constructions, which are not subject to logical evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: al-qadiyya (القضية). This is the subject of the definition.
The definition appears in the first line of the Arabic.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The genus is al-qawl (القول) – utterance. This includes both spoken words and mental concepts. Note that the author explicitly says 'as uttered is a genus for the spoken proposition, and as conceived is a genus for the mental proposition.'
Mental propositions are judgments in the mind, not yet expressed.
3
Identify the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is 'it can be said of its speaker that he is truthful or false in it.' This capacity for truth-value sets propositions apart from other utterances.
Truth and falsehood are properties of the proposition itself, not of the speaker's intention.
4
Understand what is excluded
The differentia excludes two types of compounds: insha'iyyat (non-informative speech, like commands, questions, oaths) and taqyidiyyat (restrictive constructions, like possessive phrases and adjective-noun pairs). These lack a judgment that can correspond to reality.
Insha'iyyat are acts, not reports; taqyidiyyat are parts of propositions, not complete judgments.
5
Test the definition with examples
Apply the definition to examples from the text: 'The mosque is spacious' is a proposition because it can be true or false. 'The boy's pen' is not a proposition because it does not assert anything that can be true or false.
Also test with 'Pray!' – it is a command, not truth-apt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث في مبادئ التصديقات وهي القضايا وأحكامها. القضية: قول يصح أن يقال لقائله: إنه صادق فيه أو كاذب. فالقول - وهو المركب - لفظا جنس للقضية الملفوظة، ومعقولا جنس للقضية المعقولة. وباقي القيود فصل يخرج المركبات الإنشائية طلبية كانت وغيرها (١). أو والتقيدية; لأن صدق القول وكذبه مطابقة حكمه للواقع، للاعتقاد، أو لهما معا (٤) وعدمها. ولا حكم للإنشائيات والتقييديات; لأن الحكم أداء للواقع في نفس الأمر.

(١) سبق أن قسم المنطق بعد المقدمة إلى أربعة أبواب، فهذا الباب الثالث منها.
(٢) القضية هي ما يسميه علماء البلاغة (الخبر) ويقابله الإنشاء.
(٣) إذا نطقت بقولك: الجامع واسع.
(٤) إذا خطر بذهنك الجامع واسع.
(٥) الإنشاء الطلبي الأمر، النهي، الاستفهام، التمني، التحضيض، العرض، الترجي.
(٦) غير الطلبية هي إنشائية من غير ما سبق مثل المدح "نعم الرجل خالد" والذم "بئس المرافق الفاسق"؛ ومثل القسم "والله أنت قائم" والعقود مثل "بعتك هذا وقبلته". هذه لا يقال لقائلها صدقت أو كذبت; لأن معناها غير موجود قبل النطق، بل يحصل بعد أن تنطق بها، لذا لا واقع لها قبل النطق; لأن النطق إن وافق الواقع فالقضية صادقة مثل: السماء فوقنا، وإن خالفته فهي كاذبة مثل: السماء تحتنا.

(١) النسبة التقييدية: هي المضاف والمضاف إليه، مثل: قلم الولد، والنعت والمنعوت مثل رجل عالم، فإن النطق بها لا يكون معه حكم، وهو إثبات أمر لأمر أو نفيه عنه.
(٢) الخبر الصادق هو ما يطابق الواقع ولو خالف الاعتقاد، مثل قول الكافر المشرك: الله واحد مع أنه يعتقد تعدده وهذا عند الجمهور، وكذبه مخالفته للواقع.
(٣) أما عند النظام فهو الصدق للخبر: مطابقته للاعتقاد، وإن خالف الواقع، مثل قول المشرك: الله ثالث ثلاثة والكاذب عدم مطابقته للاعتقاد.
(٤) وعند الجاحظ صدقه مطابقته للواقع والاعتقاد، مثل قول الموحد: الله واحد، أو الإسلام حق. والكذب عدم مطابقته للواقع مثل قول الفيلسوف العالم قديم، فإنه مطابق لاعتقاده، وليس مطابقا للواقع، فهو كذب. وكذا إذا قال: العالم حادث، فإنه مطابق للواقع وغير مطابق لاعتقاده، فهو كذب.
(٥) أي: عدم المطابقة للواقع عند الجمهور، وللاعتقاد عند النظام، وعدم مطابقته للواقع والاعتقاد عند الجاحظ، كل ذلك كاذب كما سبق أن مثلنا.
The third chapter: On the principles of assents, which are propositions and their rulings. The proposition: an utterance of which it is valid to say to its speaker that he is truthful or false in it. The utterance — which is the compound — as uttered is a genus for the spoken proposition, and as conceived is a genus for the mental proposition. And the remaining conditions are a differentia that exclude the non-informative compounds, whether they are imperative or otherwise (1). Or the restrictive constructions; because the truth and falsehood of an utterance is the correspondence of its judgment to reality, to belief, or to both (4) and its lack. And there is no judgment for the non-informative and restrictive constructions; because the judgment is an expression of reality in itself.

(1) He previously divided logic after the introduction into four chapters, so this is the third chapter.
(2) The proposition is what the scholars of balaghah call informative, and its opposite is non-informative.
(3) If you utter your statement: "The mosque is spacious."
(4) If it occurs in your mind: "The mosque is spacious."
(5) The imperative non-informative: command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, urging, presenting, hoping.
(6) The non-imperative ones are non-informative from other categories such as praise: "What an excellent man Khalid is!" and blame: "What a bad companion the corrupt person is!"; and like oath: "By Allah, you are standing" and contracts like: "I sold you this and you accepted it." These are not said of their speaker that he is truthful or false; because their meaning does not exist before the utterance, but rather occurs after you utter them, hence they have no reality before the utterance; because if the utterance corresponds to reality, the proposition is true, like "The sky is above us", and if it contradicts it, it is false, like "The sky is below us."

(1) The restrictive construction: it is the possessive like "the boy's pen" and the adjective and described like "a learned man", for uttering them does not involve a judgment, which is affirming something of something or negating it.
(2) The truthful report is what corresponds to reality even if it contradicts belief, like the statement of the polytheist: "Allah is one" while he believes in multiplicity, and this is according to the majority, and its falsehood is its contradiction to reality.
(3) As for al-Nazzam, truth for a report is its correspondence to belief, even if it contradicts reality, like the polytheist's statement: "Allah is the third of three", and falsehood is its lack of correspondence to belief.
(4) According to al-Jahiz, its truth is its correspondence to both reality and belief, like the monotheist's statement: "Allah is one" or "Islam is true". And falsehood is its lack of correspondence to reality, like the philosopher's statement: "The world is pre-eternal", for it corresponds to his belief but does not correspond to reality, so it is false. Likewise, if he says: "The world is originated", it corresponds to reality but not to his belief, so it is false.
(5) That is: lack of correspondence to reality according to the majority, to belief according to al-Nazzam, and lack of correspondence to both according to al-Jahiz, all of that is false as we have already exemplified.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn definition of the proposition (qadiyya) and its truth conditions. The author begins by stating that the third chapter deals with the principles of assents (tasdiqat), which are propositions and their rulings. He then gives the definition: a proposition is an utterance (qawl) of which it can be said that the speaker is truthful or false. The genus is 'utterance', which includes both spoken and mental utterances. The differentia is the capacity for truth or falsehood, which excludes all non-informative utterances (insha'iyyat) such as commands, prohibitions, questions, etc., and also excludes restrictive constructions (taqyidiyyat) like possessive phrases and adjective-noun combinations, because these do not express a judgment that can correspond or not correspond to reality. The sharh then discusses truth and falsehood: truth is the correspondence of the judgment to something. The text mentions three positions: the majority (Jumhur) say truth is correspondence to reality regardless of belief; al-Nazzam says it is correspondence to belief regardless of reality; and al-Jahiz requires both. The footnotes provide detailed examples and explanations for each view, using examples from creed and philosophy. The author also notes that non-informative and restrictive sentences have no judgment, so they are not subject to truth-value. This clarifies the scope of the proposition as the unit of logical analysis.

القول
الإنشاء
التقييدية

Key Takeaway
A proposition is defined by its capacity for truth or falsehood; non-informative and restrictive utterances fall outside the scope of logic.

Three Theories of Truth in Islamic Logic
Explaining three theories of truth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three theories of truth (majority, al-Nazzam, al-Jahiz) and apply each criterion to determine the truth value of a given statement.

Core Idea
Truth is correspondence — but the standard of correspondence (reality, belief, or both) varies among scholars.

After defining the proposition, the author explains that its truth or falsehood is determined by whether its judgment corresponds to something. But to what? Three different views are recorded: correspondence to reality (majority), to belief (al-Nazzam), or to both (al-Jahiz). This lesson explores each view and its implications for evaluating statements in Islamic theology and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three theories in the text
The passage mentions three positions: the majority (al-Jumhur), al-Nazzam, and al-Jahiz. Find the sentences that describe each.
The footnote numbers help identify them.
2
Extract the criterion for each theory
For the majority: truth = correspondence to reality (al-waqi'). For al-Nazzam: truth = correspondence to belief (al-i'tiqad). For al-Jahiz: truth = correspondence to both reality and belief.
Write down the Arabic phrases: مطابقة حكمه للواقع, مطابقته للاعتقاد, مطابقته للواقع والاعتقاد.
3
Apply theories to a provided example
The text gives the example of a polytheist who says 'Allah is one.' According to the majority, this is true (matches reality). According to al-Nazzam, it is false (does not match his belief). According to al-Jahiz, it is false (does not match both).
Trace each reasoning in the text.
4
Create your own example
Think of a statement where the speaker's belief differs from reality. For instance, a sincere but mistaken believer says 'The world is pre-eternal.' Apply each theory.
Use the structure from the text's examples.
5
Consider the implications for logical analysis
Which theory makes truth objective? Which introduces subjectivity? The majority view is standard in logic because it focuses on the proposition's relation to reality, not the speaker's psychology.
This is a critical reflection step.

الباب الثالث في مبادئ التصديقات وهي القضايا وأحكامها. القضية: قول يصح أن يقال لقائله: إنه صادق فيه أو كاذب. فالقول - وهو المركب - لفظا جنس للقضية الملفوظة، ومعقولا جنس للقضية المعقولة. وباقي القيود فصل يخرج المركبات الإنشائية طلبية كانت وغيرها (١). أو والتقيدية; لأن صدق القول وكذبه مطابقة حكمه للواقع، للاعتقاد، أو لهما معا (٤) وعدمها. ولا حكم للإنشائيات والتقييديات; لأن الحكم أداء للواقع في نفس الأمر.

(١) سبق أن قسم المنطق بعد المقدمة إلى أربعة أبواب، فهذا الباب الثالث منها.
(٢) القضية هي ما يسميه علماء البلاغة (الخبر) ويقابله الإنشاء.
(٣) إذا نطقت بقولك: الجامع واسع.
(٤) إذا خطر بذهنك الجامع واسع.
(٥) الإنشاء الطلبي الأمر، النهي، الاستفهام، التمني، التحضيض، العرض، الترجي.
(٦) غير الطلبية هي إنشائية من غير ما سبق مثل المدح "نعم الرجل خالد" والذم "بئس المرافق الفاسق"؛ ومثل القسم "والله أنت قائم" والعقود مثل "بعتك هذا وقبلته". هذه لا يقال لقائلها صدقت أو كذبت; لأن معناها غير موجود قبل النطق، بل يحصل بعد أن تنطق بها، لذا لا واقع لها قبل النطق; لأن النطق إن وافق الواقع فالقضية صادقة مثل: السماء فوقنا، وإن خالفته فهي كاذبة مثل: السماء تحتنا.

(١) النسبة التقييدية: هي المضاف والمضاف إليه، مثل: قلم الولد، والنعت والمنعوت مثل رجل عالم، فإن النطق بها لا يكون معه حكم، وهو إثبات أمر لأمر أو نفيه عنه.
(٢) الخبر الصادق هو ما يطابق الواقع ولو خالف الاعتقاد، مثل قول الكافر المشرك: الله واحد مع أنه يعتقد تعدده وهذا عند الجمهور، وكذبه مخالفته للواقع.
(٣) أما عند النظام فهو الصدق للخبر: مطابقته للاعتقاد، وإن خالف الواقع، مثل قول المشرك: الله ثالث ثلاثة والكاذب عدم مطابقته للاعتقاد.
(٤) وعند الجاحظ صدقه مطابقته للواقع والاعتقاد، مثل قول الموحد: الله واحد، أو الإسلام حق. والكذب عدم مطابقته للواقع مثل قول الفيلسوف العالم قديم، فإنه مطابق لاعتقاده، وليس مطابقا للواقع، فهو كذب. وكذا إذا قال: العالم حادث، فإنه مطابق للواقع وغير مطابق لاعتقاده، فهو كذب.
(٥) أي: عدم المطابقة للواقع عند الجمهور، وللاعتقاد عند النظام، وعدم مطابقته للواقع والاعتقاد عند الجاحظ، كل ذلك كاذب كما سبق أن مثلنا.
The third chapter: On the principles of assents, which are propositions and their rulings. The proposition: an utterance of which it is valid to say to its speaker that he is truthful or false in it. The utterance — which is the compound — as uttered is a genus for the spoken proposition, and as conceived is a genus for the mental proposition. And the remaining conditions are a differentia that exclude the non-informative compounds, whether they are imperative or otherwise (1). Or the restrictive constructions; because the truth and falsehood of an utterance is the correspondence of its judgment to reality, to belief, or to both (4) and its lack. And there is no judgment for the non-informative and restrictive constructions; because the judgment is an expression of reality in itself.

(1) He previously divided logic after the introduction into four chapters, so this is the third chapter.
(2) The proposition is what the scholars of balaghah call informative, and its opposite is non-informative.
(3) If you utter your statement: "The mosque is spacious."
(4) If it occurs in your mind: "The mosque is spacious."
(5) The imperative non-informative: command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, urging, presenting, hoping.
(6) The non-imperative ones are non-informative from other categories such as praise: "What an excellent man Khalid is!" and blame: "What a bad companion the corrupt person is!"; and like oath: "By Allah, you are standing" and contracts like: "I sold you this and you accepted it." These are not said of their speaker that he is truthful or false; because their meaning does not exist before the utterance, but rather occurs after you utter them, hence they have no reality before the utterance; because if the utterance corresponds to reality, the proposition is true, like "The sky is above us", and if it contradicts it, it is false, like "The sky is below us."

(1) The restrictive construction: it is the possessive like "the boy's pen" and the adjective and described like "a learned man", for uttering them does not involve a judgment, which is affirming something of something or negating it.
(2) The truthful report is what corresponds to reality even if it contradicts belief, like the statement of the polytheist: "Allah is one" while he believes in multiplicity, and this is according to the majority, and its falsehood is its contradiction to reality.
(3) As for al-Nazzam, truth for a report is its correspondence to belief, even if it contradicts reality, like the polytheist's statement: "Allah is the third of three", and falsehood is its lack of correspondence to belief.
(4) According to al-Jahiz, its truth is its correspondence to both reality and belief, like the monotheist's statement: "Allah is one" or "Islam is true". And falsehood is its lack of correspondence to reality, like the philosopher's statement: "The world is pre-eternal", for it corresponds to his belief but does not correspond to reality, so it is false. Likewise, if he says: "The world is originated", it corresponds to reality but not to his belief, so it is false.
(5) That is: lack of correspondence to reality according to the majority, to belief according to al-Nazzam, and lack of correspondence to both according to al-Jahiz, all of that is false as we have already exemplified.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn definition of the proposition (qadiyya) and its truth conditions. The author begins by stating that the third chapter deals with the principles of assents (tasdiqat), which are propositions and their rulings. He then gives the definition: a proposition is an utterance (qawl) of which it can be said that the speaker is truthful or false. The genus is 'utterance', which includes both spoken and mental utterances. The differentia is the capacity for truth or falsehood, which excludes all non-informative utterances (insha'iyyat) such as commands, prohibitions, questions, etc., and also excludes restrictive constructions (taqyidiyyat) like possessive phrases and adjective-noun combinations, because these do not express a judgment that can correspond or not correspond to reality. The sharh then discusses truth and falsehood: truth is the correspondence of the judgment to something. The text mentions three positions: the majority (Jumhur) say truth is correspondence to reality regardless of belief; al-Nazzam says it is correspondence to belief regardless of reality; and al-Jahiz requires both. The footnotes provide detailed examples and explanations for each view, using examples from creed and philosophy. The author also notes that non-informative and restrictive sentences have no judgment, so they are not subject to truth-value. This clarifies the scope of the proposition as the unit of logical analysis.

Key Takeaway
Truth is defined by correspondence, but the standard varies: majority (reality), al-Nazzam (belief), al-Jahiz (both); each yields different truth values for the same statement.

Truth, Falsehood, and the Absence of Judgment in Performatives
Defines truth/falsehood and excludes performatives from judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between informative propositions (subject to truth/falsehood) and performative utterances (lacking judgment), and identify the three positions on truth: correspondence to reality (al-Jumhur), to belief (al-Nazzam), or to both (al-Jahiz).

Core Idea
Truth is correspondence of judgment to reality; performatives have no judgment to correspond.

This passage from Fanari's commentary lays the foundation for what makes a statement true or false. It clarifies that not all utterances can be evaluated as true or false—only informative statements (akhbar) carry the judgment necessary for truth conditions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for anyone working with Islamic texts, whether in fiqh, kalam, or tafsir, because it determines how we treat different types of speech acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions of truth and falsehood
The passage states that truth (sidq) and falsehood (kidhb) are defined as correspondence of the judgment (hukm) to reality, or to belief, or to both. Find these three positions and note who held each: the majority (al-Jumhur) said reality, al-Nazzam said belief, and al-Jahiz required both.
Falsehood (kidhb) is simply the lack of that correspondence.
2
Locate the key condition for truth-bearing utterances
The passage then asserts that performatives (insha'iyyat) and restrictives (taqyidiyyat) have no judgment. Highlight the phrase: 'لا حكم للإنشائيات والتقييديات'—no judgment for these types.
This is a categorical exclusion.
3
Understand why performatives lack judgment
The reason given is that judgment is a rendering of reality from both sides of the relation (subject and predicate) with respect to a specific time (past, present, future). Performatives and restrictives do not perform such a rendering—they lack this temporally anchored assertion.
For example, a command like 'Pray!' does not assert a state of affairs; it brings one about.
4
Connect to the distinction between khabar and insha'
This is the core logical distinction in Islamic logic between informative statements (khabar) and performative utterances (insha'). Only khabar can be true or false; insha' is evaluated differently (e.g., as valid/invalid, sincere/insincere).
This distinction impacts how we interpret revelation: verses that are commands (amr) are not true/false but binding or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتقيدية؛ لأنَّ صِدْقَ القَوْلِ وَكَذِبَهُ مطابَقَةُ حُكْمِهِ للوَاقِع أو لِلَاعْتِقادِ، أو لَهُما مَعا وعَدَمُهَا. ولا حُكْمَ للإنشائِيَاتِ والتقييدياتِ؛ لأنَّ الحُكْمَ أَدَاء للواقع في نفس الأمرِ مِنْ طَرفَي النسبة، ماضياً أو حالاً، أو استقبالاً ولا أَداء في الإنشائياتِ وَالتقَيدياتِ.
And the restrictive [proposition]: because truth of a statement and its falsehood is correspondence of its judgment to reality, or to belief, or to both, and its lack. And there is no judgment for performatives and restrictives because judgment is a rendering of reality in the mind from both sides of the relation, past, present, or future, and there is no rendering in performatives and restrictives.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of truth and falsehood in informative statements (akhbar). It first mentions multiple positions: the majority (al-Jumhur) holds that truth is correspondence of the judgment to external reality, Nazzam holds it is correspondence to the speaker's belief, and Jahiz requires both. Falsehood is the lack of such correspondence. Then the passage turns to utterances that do not carry judgment: performatives (insha'iyyat) like commands or questions, and restrictive phrases (taqidiyyat) like possessive constructions or adjective-noun combinations. The reason given is that judgment involves asserting a relation between two terms that reflects reality at a specific time (past, present, future). Since performatives and restrictives lack this temporally anchored assertion, they are not subject to truth or falsehood. This is a key distinction in Islamic logic between informative and non-informative speech.

Key Takeaway

Dividing Propositions into Categorical and Conditional
Author divides propositions into categorical and conditional with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given proposition as categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya) based on the type of relational judgment it expresses.

Core Idea
Propositions are divided by the relation: attribute (categorical) or condition (conditional).

The author of the Fawa'id al-Fanari divides propositions into two fundamental types: categorical and conditional. This division is the first step in analyzing arguments because the form of the proposition determines how it functions in a syllogism. In this lesson, you will learn to identify each type and its subtypes using the criteria given in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
The author states that every proposition is either hamliyya (categorical) or shartiyya (conditional). Note that the division is exhaustive: there are no other types.
2
Understand the criterion: the relational judgment
The division is based on the type of nisba hukmiyya (relational judgment). If the relation is affirmation/negation of a concept about another concept (subject-predicate), it is categorical. If the relation involves a condition (if-then) or a separation (either-or), it is conditional.
The author explains: 'النسبة: إن كانت ثبوت مفهوم لمفهوم... حملية' and 'وإن كانت ثبوت مفهوم عند ثبوت مفهوم آخر... شرطية'.
3
Examine the examples provided
The author gives concrete examples: 'الجامع واسع' (categorical), 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود' (connective conditional), 'العدد إما زوج أو فرد' (disjunctive conditional). Notice that the conditional includes both connective (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) subtypes.
4
Recognize negation in both types
The author also shows negation: for categorical, 'الجامع ليس واسعا'; for conditional, 'ليس إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود'. Negation applies to the whole proposition, not just the parts.
The term 'انتزاع' (withdrawal) is used for negation of the relational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي: أقسام القضية. إما حملية كقولنا: زيد كاتب أو ليس بكاتب. وإما شرطية؛ لأن القضية لا بد فيها من إيقاع النسبة الحكمية أو انتزاعها. فالنسبة: إن كانت ثبوت مفهوم لمفهوم، فالقضية القائلة بإيقاعها أو سلبها - حملية. وإن كانت ثبوت مفهوم عند ثبوت مفهوم آخر، أو ثبوت مباينة مفهوم عن مفهوم آخر، فالقضية القائلة بإيقاع ذلك أو سلبها - شرطية. (۱) مثل: الجامع واسع فالجامع مفهوم، وهو المبنى الذي يصلى به، وواسع مفهوم أي عدم الضيق فإذا أثبتنا السعة للجامع أي حملنا السعة عليه، فهذه تسمى حملية، وكذا إذا سلبنا السعة عنه وقلنا: الجامع ليس واسعا، والمراد بالانتزاع السلب. (۲) مثل إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فهنا أثبتنا وجود النهار عند طلوع الشمس. (٣) مثل العدد إما زوج أو فرد هنا أثبتنا مخالفة فردية العدد لزوجيته. فكلاهما يسميان شرطية. مثال السلب للشرطية الأولى : ليس إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود.
And they are: Divisions of the proposition. Either categorical, like our saying: Zayd is a writer or is not a writer. Or conditional; because the proposition necessarily involves the occurrence of the relational judgment or its withdrawal. The relation: if it is the affirmation of a concept to a concept, then the proposition affirming its occurrence or its negation is categorical. And if it is the affirmation of a concept upon the affirmation of another concept, or the affirmation of the difference of a concept from another concept, then the proposition affirming that occurrence or its negation is conditional. (1) Example: The mosque is spacious: 'mosque' is a concept, and it is the building in which one prays, and 'spacious' is a concept meaning absence of constriction. So if we affirm spaciousness to the mosque, i.e., we attribute spaciousness to it, this is called categorical. Likewise if we negate spaciousness from it and say: The mosque is not spacious. And by 'withdrawal' is meant negation. (2) Example: If the sun is rising, then daytime exists. Here we have affirmed the existence of daytime at the rising of the sun. (3) Example: The number is either even or odd. Here we have affirmed the opposition of the oddness of the number to its evenness. Both are called conditional. Example of the negation of the first conditional: Not: If the sun is rising, then night exists.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions (qadaya) into two main types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The author begins by stating the division: a proposition is either hamliyya, like 'Zayd is a writer' or 'Zayd is not a writer', or shartiyya. The reason is that every proposition must involve either the occurrence (iqa') of a relational judgment (nisba hukmiyya) or its withdrawal/intizar (here explained as negation). The relation (nisba) is then analyzed: If the relation is the affirmation (thubut) of one concept (mafhum) to another concept (i.e., subject-predicate attribution), then the proposition that affirms that occurrence or its negation is hamliyya. This is the simple categorical proposition. If the relation is the affirmation of a concept upon the condition of the affirmation of another concept (i.e., a conditional connection, like 'if...then'), or the affirmation of the separation (mubayana) of a concept from another concept (i.e., disjunction, like 'either...or'), then the proposition affirming that occurrence or its negation is shartiyya. The author then provides examples: For hamliyya: 'The mosque is spacious' — analyzing the concepts. For shartiyya: the muttasila (connective) example: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'; and the munfasila (disjunctive) example: 'The number is either even or odd.' Both are called conditional. Also, the negation of the first conditional: 'Not: if the sun is rising, then night exists.' This passage is fundamental for understanding the two pillars of propositional logic in Islamic tradition. Note that the conditional proposition includes both the connective (shartiyya muttasila) and the disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila). The student should memorize the definitions and be able to identify examples from fiqh and kalam.

Key Terms
حمليَّة (hamliyya): A categorical proposition that predicates a concept (mahmul) of a subject (mawdu') by affirmation or negation, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer'.
شرطية (shartiyya): A conditional proposition that links two propositions; either connective (mutasila: if...then) or disjunctive (munfasila: either...or).
النسبة الحكمية (al-nisba al-hukmiyya): The relational judgment that connects the subject and predicate (in hamliyya) or two propositions (in shartiyya). It is either affirmed (iqa') or negated (intiza').
انتزاع (intiza'): Withdrawal or negation of the relational judgment; in this context it means the negation of the relation (salb) as opposed to its affirmation (iqa').

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) العدد زوج (The number is even)

Key Takeaway
The division of propositions into categorical and conditional is based on the type of relational judgment affirmed or negated: subject-predicate for categorical, condition or disjunction for conditional.

The Two Fundamental Types of Propositions: Categorical and Conditional
Author divides propositions into categorical and conditional with examples and negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given proposition as categorical or conditional by examining the nature of its judgmental relation.

Core Idea
The type of proposition depends on whether the relation is direct attribution or conditional dependence.

In logic, every proposition (qadhiyya) affirms or denies a relation. To think clearly in Islamic sciences, you must first recognize what kind of relation is being asserted. This lesson introduces the two primary types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The distinction is based on whether the relation is a direct attribution of one concept to another, or a relation that depends on a condition or mutual exclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's core relation
Read the proposition and ask: what is being affirmed or denied? Is it a direct attribution (e.g., 'Zayd is a writer') or a conditional link (e.g., 'If... then...')?
The judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya) is the key.
2
Check if the relation is subject-predicate
If the relation affirms or denies one concept of another directly, it is categorical. Example: 'The mosque is spacious' – 'spacious' is predicated directly of 'mosque'.
Negation of a categorical qualifies as categorical too.
3
Check if the relation involves a condition
If the relation depends on another statement (e.g., 'If A then B' or 'Either A or B'), it is conditional. The truth of the whole depends on the relation between its parts.
Conditionals include connected (mutassil) and disjunctive (munfasil).
4
Apply the test to mixed examples
Take an Islamic statement like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' – this is categorical. Compare with 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden' – this is conditional. The shift in relation type changes the logical properties.
This skill is essential for analyzing arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضية: إما حمليَّةٌ كقولنا: زيد كاتب أو ليس بكاتبٍ. وإما شرطية)؛ لأنَّ القضيَّة لا بد فيها من إيقاع النسبة الحكمية) أو انتزاعها. فالنسبةُ: إِنْ كَانَتْ ثبوت مفهوم لمفهوم، فالقضيَّةُ القائلة بإيقاعها أو سلبها -حملية. وإن كانت ثبوت مفهوم عند ثبوتِ مفهوم آخر". أو ثبوت مباينة مفهوم عن مفهوم آخر)، فالقضية القائلة (۱) مثل: الجامع واسع فالجامع مفهوم، وهو المبنى الذي يصلى به، وواسع مفهوم أي عدم الضيق فإذا أثبتنا السعة للجامع أي حملنا السعة عليه، فهذه تسمى حملية، وكذا إذا سلبنا السعة عنه وقلنا: الجامع ليس واسعا، والمراد بالانتزاع السلب. (۲) مثل إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود فهنا أثبتنا وجود النهار عند طلوع الشمس. (۳) مثل العدد إما زوج أو فرد هنا أثبتنا مخالفة فردية العدد لزوجيته. فكلاهما يسميان شرطية.
مثال السلب للشرطية الأولى : ليس إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود.
ومثال السالبة للشرطية الثانية : ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو منقسماً إلى متساويين.
والسلب يتسلط على العناد أي لا عناد بين القضيتين، وفي الإيجاب إثبات العناد بين القضيتين، والعناد معناه التنافر والتباين وعدم الاجتماع، وفي السالبة عدم التنافر والتباين.
The divisions of the proposition: either categorical, like our saying: Zayd is a writer or is not a writer. Or conditional; because the proposition necessarily involves the placement of the judgmental relation or its withdrawal. So the relation: if it is the affirmation of a concept for a concept, then the proposition stating its placement or its negation is categorical. And if it is the affirmation of a concept upon the affirmation of another concept, or the affirmation of the dissimilarity of a concept from another concept, then the proposition stating its placement or its withdrawal is conditional. (1) Example: The mosque is spacious – so 'mosque' is a concept, and it is the building in which prayer is performed, and 'spacious' is a concept meaning absence of narrowness; so if we affirm spaciousness for the mosque, i.e., we predicate spaciousness upon it, then this is called categorical, and likewise if we negate spaciousness from it and say: the mosque is not spacious, and by 'withdrawal' is meant negation. (2) Example: If the sun is rising, then daytime exists – here we affirm the existence of daytime when the sun rises. (3) Example: The number is either even or odd – here we affirm the opposition of the number's oddness to its evenness. Both are called conditional.
Example of the negation of the first conditional: Not: If the sun is rising, then nighttime exists.
And example of the negative of the second conditional: Not: Either the number is even or divisible into two equal parts.
And negation applies to opposition, i.e., there is no opposition between the two propositions; and in affirmation there is the establishment of opposition between the two propositions. Opposition means discordance, dissimilarity, and lack of conjunction; and in the negative, there is no discordance or dissimilarity.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of propositions (qadhiyya) into two main types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The author begins by stating the division with examples: a categorical proposition is like 'Zayd is a writer' or 'Zayd is not a writer'. A conditional proposition is introduced with a brief rationale: every proposition necessarily involves either the placement (i'qa') of a judgmental relation or its withdrawal (intiza'), i.e., affirmation or negation. The type of relation determines the proposition type. If the relation is the affirmation of one concept for another (subject-predicate), the proposition is categorical. If the relation is the affirmation of a concept upon the condition of another concept (as in conditional conjunctions) or the affirmation of mutual exclusion between concepts (as in disjunctions), then the proposition is conditional. The author provides three illustrative footnotes. The first footnote expands on the categorical example: 'The mosque is spacious' – identifying the concepts and what predication entails. The second footnote gives a typical conditional example: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists', which is a connected conditional (mutassil). The third footnote gives a disjunctive conditional (munfasil): 'The number is either even or odd', where the two parts are mutually exclusive. Both types are called conditional. The passage then gives examples of negations of conditionals: the negation of the first type as 'Not: If the sun is rising then nighttime exists' (which would be false if the sun rises and night exists, but it is a negation of the whole conditional), and the negation of the disjunctive as 'Not: Either the number is even or divisible into two equal parts' (since being divisible into equal parts is equivalent to evenness, so the disjunction excludes that). Finally, the author discusses the concept of 'inad (opposition). Negation applies to 'inad, meaning there is no opposition between the two propositions in the negative, while affirmation establishes opposition. 'Inad is defined as discordance, dissimilarity, and lack of conjunction. This clarifies how negation and affirmation relate to the conditional relations.

Key Terms
قضية
حمليّة
شرطيّة
نسبة حكمية
انتزاع
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The imam is knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental division of propositions is into categorical (direct subject-predicate attribution) and conditional (relation dependent on a condition or mutual exclusion).

Meaning of 'Inaad in Disjunctive Conditionals
Author explains the meaning of 'inad' in disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'inad' (contradiction) in the context of disjunctive conditionals and explain how negation affects it.

Core Idea
The negation of a disjunctive denies mutual exclusivity, not the disjuncts.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive conditionals involve a relation called 'inad' (contradiction) between the two parts. This passage unpacks what 'inad' means and how it behaves under affirmation and negation. Grasping this is key to correctly analyzing disjunctive arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Example
Find the example of a negative disjunctive in the Arabic text: "ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو منقسماً إلى متساويين".
2
Identify the Two Disjuncts
The two disjuncts are 'the number is even' and 'the number is divisible into two equal parts'.
3
Understand 'Inad'
The author defines 'inad' as mutual exclusivity: the two propositions cannot be true together.
4
See the Effect of Negation
The negation ("ليس") applies to the entire disjunctive, denying the mutual exclusivity between the two disjuncts.
Affirmation of a disjunctive asserts mutual exclusivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال السالبة للشرطية الثانية : ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو منقسماً إلى متساويين. والسلب يتسلط على العناد أي لا عناد بين القضيتين، وفي الإيجاب إثبات العناد بين القضيتين، والعناد معناه التنافر والتباين وعدم الاجتماع، وفي السالبة عدم التنافر والتباين.
And an example of the negative of the second conditional: 'It is not the case that the number is either even or divisible into two equal parts.' And negation dominates over contradiction, i.e., there is no contradiction between the two propositions, and in affirmation there is affirmation of contradiction between the two propositions. Contradiction means opposition, difference, and impossibility of coexistence. In the negative, it is the absence of opposition and difference.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage explains the notion of 'inad' (contradiction) in the context of disjunctive conditionals. The author gives an example of a negative disjunctive: 'It is not the case that the number is either even or divisible into two equal parts.' This negation denies the mutual exclusivity (contradiction) between being even and being divisible into two equal parts. The author clarifies that in an affirmative disjunctive, we assert that the two parts are mutually exclusive; in a negative disjunctive, we deny that exclusivity. The term 'inad' is defined as opposition, difference, and non-coexistence. Thus, the positive disjunctive affirms such opposition, while the negative disjunctive denies it. This is crucial for understanding the logical behavior of disjunctive propositions.

Classification of Conditionals: Conjunctive and Disjunctive
Author classifies conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals and identify their affirmative and negative forms in Arabic logical texts.

Core Idea
Conditionals are either conjunctive (if-then) or disjunctive (either-or); each has affirmative and negative forms.

In this passage, the author builds on the previous discussion of 'inad' to classify conditional propositions. Conditionals are divided into two main types: conjunctive, which link propositions with 'if...then', and disjunctive, which separate them with 'either...or'. Each type can be affirmative or negative, and mastering this classification is essential for analyzing compound propositions in logic and Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Read the opening line 'أقسام الشرطية' and note the two categories: متصلة (conjunctive) and منفصلة (disjunctive). These are the fundamental divisions.
2
Understand the logical relation in each type
For conjunctive, the truth of the consequent is tied to the antecedent (if A then B). For disjunctive, the two disjuncts are mutually exclusive (either A or B, but not both).
3
Recognize affirmative and negative forms
Each type has an affirmative form (judging that the relation holds) and a negative form (judging that it does not hold). The Arabic examples show 'إن كانت..فـ' for conjunctive and 'إما..وإما' for disjunctive, with negation prefix 'ليس'.
4
Apply to given examples
Match each example in the passage to its category: 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود' is affirmative conjunctive; 'ليس إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود' is negative conjunctive; 'العدد إما زوج وإما مفرد' is affirmative disjunctive; 'ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو منقسما بمتساويين' is negative disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الشرطية. ومن هذا يُعرف أن الشرطية أيضاً: إما (متصلَة): كقولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، حكم فيها بأن وجود النهار عند طلوع الشمس واقع). وكقولنا : ليس إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود، حكم فيها بأن وجود الليل عند طلوع الشمس غير واقع. وإما شرطية منفصلة كقولنا : العدد إما زوج وإما مفرد) حكم فيها بأن مباينة فردية العدد لزوجيته واقعة). وكقولنا ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو منقسما بمتساويين)، حكم فيها بأن مباينة الانقسام بمتساويين للزوجية غير واقعة. (1) :أي من التقسيم الثاني الذي هو ثبوت مفهوم عند ثبوت شيء آخر، أو مباينة مفهوم لمفهوم. (2) المتصلة هي التي حكم فيها بصدق قضية أو لا صدقها على صدق قضية أخرى وهي فعل الشرط. (3) هذا مثال للموجبة الشرطية المتصلة. (4) هذا مثال للسالبة الشرطية المتصلة. (5) هذا مثال للشرطية المنفصلة الموجبة. (6) هذا مثال للشرطية المنفصلة السالبة.
Sections of the conditional. From this it is known that the conditional is either conjunctive: like our saying 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists' — a judgment that the existence of daytime when the sun is shining is actual; and like our saying 'It is not the case that if the sun is shining, then night exists' — a judgment that the existence of night when the sun is shining is not actual. Or it is a disjunctive conditional: like our saying 'The number is either even or odd' — a judgment that the difference of the oddness of the number from its evenness is actual; and like our saying 'It is not the case that the number is either even or divisible into two equal parts' — a judgment that the difference of divisibility into two equal parts from evenness is not actual. (1) i.e., from the second division which is the existence of a concept upon the existence of another thing, or the difference of a concept from a concept. (2) The conjunctive is that in which the truth or falsity of one proposition is judged upon the truth of another proposition, and it is the condition of the conditional. (3) This is an example of the affirmative conjunctive conditional. (4) This is an example of the negative conjunctive conditional. (5) This is an example of the affirmative disjunctive conditional. (6) This is an example of the negative disjunctive conditional.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of conditional propositions. It begins with a heading 'Sections of the conditional'. The author states that from the preceding discussion (about 'inad' on page 91) we understand that conditionals are of two types. The first is conjunctive (mutatsila), which connects two propositions with 'if...then'. Two examples are given: the affirmative 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists' affirms that the consequent follows; the negative 'It is not the case that if the sun is shining, then night exists' denies that connection. The second type is disjunctive (munfasila), which separates two propositions with 'either...or'. Again, an affirmative example: 'The number is either even or odd' asserts mutual exclusivity; a negative example: 'It is not the case that the number is either even or divisible into two equal parts' denies such exclusivity. The footnotes further clarify: the conjunctive judges the truth or falsity of one proposition based on the truth of another; the examples illustrate the four forms: affirmative conjunctive, negative conjunctive, affirmative disjunctive, negative disjunctive. This classification is foundational for understanding compound propositions in logic.

المنفصلة
الشرطية

Key Takeaway
Conditionals are either conjunctive (if-then) or disjunctive (either-or), each with affirmative and negative forms, forming the basis for analyzing compound propositions in logic and Islamic sciences.

Parts of a Categorical Proposition: Subject and Predicate
Author names the subject and predicate of categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul) in any categorical proposition from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
The subject is what we talk about; the predicate is what we say about it.

In this passage, al-Fanari defines the two parts of a categorical proposition: subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul). He also connects these to grammar and rhetoric terms. Let's learn how to spot these parts so you can analyse any simple Islamic statement logically.

Guided Path
1
Identify a categorical proposition
Find a sentence that makes a direct affirmation or denial about something, without any condition. For example, 'The world is temporally originated' or 'Allah is merciful.'
Categorical propositions have no 'if' or 'then'.
2
Separate the subject and predicate
Ask: What is being talked about? That is the subject (mawdu'). What is being said about it? That is the predicate (mahmul).
The subject is the thing you are describing; the predicate is the description.
3
Label them as mawdu' and mahmul
Use the Arabic technical terms: the subject is al-mawdu', the predicate is al-mahmul.
These terms come from the root w-d-‘ (to place) and h-m-l (to carry).
4
Map to grammar and rhetoric terms
Recognize that mawdu' corresponds to mubtada' in Arabic grammar and musnad ilayhi in rhetoric. Mahmul corresponds to khabar and musnad.
This connection helps bridge logic and Arabic language studies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجزء الأول من الحمليَّة يُسمى موضوعاً؛ لأنه وضع ليحمل عليه شيء، والثاني محمولاً لحمله على الأول. والجزء الأول من الشرطية أي شرطية كانت يُسمى مقدماً لتقدمه في الذكر طبعاً، وإن تأخر وضعاً، والثاني تاليا لتلوه لذلك. (١) وهو ما يسميه النحويون: مبتدأ، وأهل البلاغة: المسند إليه. (٢) وهذا ما يسميه النحويون خبراً، وأهل البلاغة: مسنداً. (٣) وهو ما يسميه النحاة: فعل الشرط. (٤) كأن يقال: النهار موجود إن كانت الشمس طالعة. (٥) وهو ما يسميه النحاة جواب الشرط وجزاءه.
And the first part of the categorical (hamliyya) is called subject (mawdu'); because it is placed for something to be predicated upon it. And the second is called predicate (mahmul) for its predication upon the first. And the first part of the conditional, whatever conditional it may be, is called antecedent (muqaddam) due to its precedence in mention naturally, even if it is delayed in position; and the second is called consequent (tali) due to its following that. (1) That is what grammarians call 'mubtada' and rhetoricians call 'musnad ilayhi'. (2) This is what grammarians call 'khabar' and rhetoricians call 'musnad'. (3) That is what grammarians call the 'fi'l al-shart' (verb of the condition). (4) As in the saying: 'Daytime exists if the sun is risen.' (5) That is what grammarians call the 'jawab al-shart' (answer of the condition) and 'jaza'uhu' (its result).
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the technical names for the parts of categorical and conditional propositions. For the categorical (hamliyya) proposition, which consists of a subject and predicate, the first part is called mawdu' (subject) because it is placed (wudi'a) for something to be carried (predicated) upon it. The second part is called mahmul (predicate) because it is carried upon the first. The author then notes that grammarians and rhetoricians have equivalent terms: mubtada' and musnad ilayhi for subject, khabar and musnad for predicate. For the conditional (shartiyya), the first part is called muqaddam (antecedent) due to its natural precedence in mention, even if it is delayed in actual phrasing (i.e., the order can be reversed, as in 'Daytime exists if the sun is risen' where the consequent comes first). The second part is called tali (consequent) because it follows the antecedent. The author adds that grammarians call the antecedent 'fi'l al-shart' (the verb of the condition) and the consequent 'jawab al-shart' (the answer of the condition) or 'jaza'uhu' (its recompense). This passage clarifies the terminology used in logic, connecting it to Arabic grammar and rhetoric.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu'): The subject of a categorical proposition, upon which something is predicated. [Note: In logic, stable; in grammar it corresponds to mubtada'.]
محمول (mahmul): The predicate of a categorical proposition, what is affirmed or denied about the subject. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Predicate (mahmul)
C) Antecedent (muqaddam)
D) Consequent (tali)

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions have a subject (mawdu') and a predicate (mahmul); the subject is what is talked about, the predicate is what is said about it.

Parts of a Conditional Proposition: Antecedent and Consequent
Author names the antecedent and consequent of conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in any conditional proposition, even when the order is reversed.

Core Idea
The antecedent is the condition, even if it appears later in the sentence.

In this passage, al-Fanari defines the two parts of a conditional proposition: antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). He emphasizes that the antecedent naturally precedes, even if it is delayed in wording. Let's learn to spot these parts so you can analyse conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find a conditional proposition
Look for words like 'in' (if) or 'idha' (when). For example, in fiqh: 'If the traveler shortens the prayer, then it is permitted.'
Conditionals express a dependency between two statements.
2
Identify the antecedent (muqaddam)
The antecedent is the condition, usually introduced by 'in' or 'idha'. It is what precedes naturally in the logical relation.
Even if the sentence starts with the result, the antecedent remains the condition.
3
Identify the consequent (tali)
The consequent is the result that follows from the antecedent. It is what is caused by the condition.
In Arabic grammar, it is called jawab al-shart.
4
Check for reversed order
The antecedent can be placed after the consequent in wording. Al-Fanari's example: 'Daytime exists if the sun is risen.' Here 'the sun is risen' is the antecedent even though it comes later.
The natural (intrinsic) precedence is logical, not necessarily textual.
5
Connect to grammar terms
Grammarians call the antecedent 'fi'l al-shart' (verb of the condition) and the consequent 'jawab al-shart' (answer of the condition) or 'jaza'uhu' (its recompense).
This helps integrate logic with Arabic syntax studies.

Source Text

مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent in a conditional proposition; the part that precedes naturally, even if placed later in wording. [Note: In Arabic grammar, it is fi'l al-shart; in logic it is the premise that implies.]
تالي (tali): The consequent in a conditional proposition; the part that follows the antecedent. [Note: In grammar, jawab al-shart.]

Affirmative Categorical Propositions
Author defines affirmative proposition and gives example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an affirmative categorical proposition and explain the act of īqā‘ (establishing the predicate).

Core Idea
An affirmative proposition establishes the predicate for the subject, free from any tool of negation.

In this passage, the author introduces the classification of propositions into affirmative and negative. We begin with the affirmative: a judgment that affirms the predicate to the subject. Understanding this operation is essential for analyzing the truth conditions of claims in Islamic disciplines such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Find the subject and predicate
Identify the thing being spoken about (subject) and what is said about it (predicate). For example, in 'The prayer is obligatory,' the subject is 'the prayer' and the predicate is 'obligatory'.
2
Check for the tool of negation
Look for words like 'laysa' (ليس), 'mā' (ما), or 'lā' (لا) that indicate negation. If none are present, the proposition is likely affirmative.
The tool of negation is the key differentiator between affirmative and negative.
3
Identify the act of īqā‘
In an affirmative proposition, the predicate is actively established (īqā‘) for the subject. For instance, in 'The prayer is obligatory,' the property of being obligatory is affirmed of the prayer.
Īqā‘ is the mental act of affirmation.
4
Classify the proposition
If the judgment involves īqā‘ and no negation tool, the proposition is affirmative (mūjiba).

أنواع القضية من حيث الإيجاب والسلب ومما مرَّ عُلِمَ أنْ (القضيَّة) حَمليةٌ كانت أو شرطيةً متصلةً أو منفصلة: ۱ (إِمَّا موجبةٌ)(١)(٢) أي: حكم فيها بالإيقاع (كقولنا): في الحملية (زيد كاتب)(٣) . وإما سالبة (٤) إن كان الحكم فيها بالانتزاع (كقولنا) فيها: (زيد ليس بكاتب). وأمثلة الشرطيات، قد تقدمت). (١) أي: مثبتةٌ خالية من آلة السلب وهي آلة النفي. (٢) أي: إثبات المحمول للموضوع (٣) فالكتابة ثابتة لزيد. (٤) أي منفية فيها آلة السلب -وهي آلة النفي. (٥) هو انتزاع الكتابة عن زيد. Types of proposition in terms of affirmation and negation. And from what has passed it is known that the proposition — whether categorical or conditional, conjunctive or disjunctive — is either affirmative (mūjiba), i.e., a judgment in it of affirmation (īqā‘), as we say in the categorical: 'Zaid is a writer'; or negative (sāliba), if the judgment in it is of withdrawal (intizā‘), as we say in it: 'Zaid is not a writer.' And examples of conditionals have preceded. (1) i.e., positive, devoid of the tool of negation which is the tool of denial. (2) i.e., establishing the predicate for the subject. (3) So writing is established for Zaid. (4) i.e., negative, in it is the tool of negation – which is the tool of denial. (5) It is the withdrawal of writing from Zaid. (6) Where he gave an example for the conjunctive conditional by saying: 'It is not that if the sun is shining then the night exists.' And for the disjunctive conditional by saying: 'It is not that either the number is even or divisible into two equal parts.' And negation is not applied to the antecedent nor to the consequent but to the mutual exclusion and contrast. i.e., there is no contrast between the antecedent and consequent.

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions by quality: affirmative (موجبة) and negative (سالبة). An affirmative proposition is one wherein the judgment affirms (إيقاع) the predicate to the subject; for example, in the categorical proposition 'Zaid is a writer,' writing is established for Zaid. A negative proposition is one wherein the judgment withdraws (انتزاع) the predicate from the subject; for example, 'Zaid is not a writer,' where writing is withdrawn from Zaid. The author clarifies that this applies to all proposition types: categorical, conjunctive conditional, and disjunctive conditional. Footnotes explain that the affirmative is free from the tool of negation, and the negative contains it. For conditionals, the author alludes to earlier examples: in the conjunctive conditional, negation applies to the entire connection, not just the antecedent or consequent. For example, 'It is not that if the sun is shining then the night exists' negates the implication. In the disjunctive conditional, 'It is not that either the number is even or divisible into two equal parts' negates the disjunction — meaning there is no actual contrast or mutual exclusion between the parts. The student should note that negation in conditionals operates on the relation between the parts, not on the parts individually.

Negative Categorical Propositions
Author defines negative proposition and gives example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a negative categorical proposition and explain the act of intizā‘ (withdrawing the predicate).

Core Idea
A negative proposition withdraws the predicate from the subject using the tool of negation.

Having studied affirmative propositions, we now turn to their counterpart: negative propositions. In a negative judgment, the predicate is withdrawn (intizā‘) from the subject via a tool of negation like 'laysa'. Recognizing this operation is crucial for evaluating contradictory claims in Islamic legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Find the subject and predicate
Identify the subject and predicate as before. For example, in 'Zakat is not due on the poor,' the subject is 'zakat' and the predicate is 'due on the poor'.
2
Locate the tool of negation
Look for negation particles such as 'laysa', 'mā', 'lā', or 'lam'. Their presence indicates the proposition is negative.
The tool of negation is the explicit sign of negation.
3
Identify the act of intizā‘
In a negative proposition, the predicate is withdrawn (intizā‘) from the subject. For instance, in 'Zakat is not due on the poor,' the property of being due is withdrawn from zakat relative to the poor.
Intizā‘ is the mental act of denial or withdrawal.
4
Classify the proposition
If the judgment involves intizā‘ with a tool of negation, the proposition is negative (sāliba).

Negation in Conditional Propositions
Author clarifies the scope of negation in conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how negation operates in conditional propositions (conjunctive and disjunctive) and identify what is being negated.

Core Idea
In conditionals, negation targets the relation between antecedent and consequent, not the individual parts.

The author reminds us that conditional propositions are also either affirmative or negative, but negation in conditionals is subtle. When a conditional is negated, the negation does not apply to the antecedent or consequent alone but to the entire conditional relationship—whether it is an implication (in conjunctive conditionals) or a disjunction (in disjunctive conditionals). This nuance is critical for correctly interpreting theological and legal conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the conditional is conjunctive (if...then) or disjunctive (either...or). For example, 'If the traveler is on a journey, then he may shorten the prayer' is conjunctive; 'Either the act is obligatory or forbidden' is disjunctive.
2
Locate the negation tool
Find the word 'not' or its equivalent that negates the entire conditional. In Arabic, it is often 'laysa' at the beginning, e.g., 'laysa in kānat...'
The negation typically precedes the entire conditional statement.
3
Determine the scope of negation
The negation applies to the relation between the parts, not to the parts themselves. For conjunctive, it negates the implication; for disjunctive, it negates the mutual exclusion or the disjunction itself.
The author explicitly states: 'negation is not applied to the antecedent nor to the consequent but to the mutual exclusion and contrast.'
4
Interpret the meaning
Explain what the negated conditional means. For 'It is not that if the sun is shining then the night exists', it means the implication 'if sun then night' is false (the sun can shine while night does not exist).
