Classification of Propositions by Quality and Quantity
Author classifies propositions by quality (affirmative/negative) and then by quantity (particular, quantified, absolute).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given categorical proposition as affirmative or negative, and then as particular, quantified, or absolute, and further distinguish universal from particular quantified propositions.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either affirmative or negative; every categorical proposition is either particular, quantified, or absolute.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational division of propositions by quality (affirmative/negative) and then by quantity (particular/quantified/absolute). These two axes produce eight basic types of categorical propositions. Understanding them is essential for analysing arguments in Islamic logic, especially in legal reasoning (usul al-fiqh) and theology (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Identify quality: affirmative or negative
Look for the presence or absence of a negation tool (like 'laysa' or 'ma'). If the proposition affirms the predicate of the subject without a negation tool, it is affirmative (mujiba). If it includes a negation tool, it is negative (saliba).
2
Determine if the subject is specific
If the subject refers to a specific individual (e.g., a proper name 'Zaid', a demonstrative 'this'), it is a particular proposition (makhsusa). If the subject is a general term, proceed to check for a quantifier.
Makhsusa subjects are one of the six definite nouns (al-ma'arif al-sitta).
3
Check for a quantifier (sawr)
If the subject is general and there is a quantifier word like 'kull' (all), 'ba'd' (some), or the comprehensive 'al', then it is quantified (mahsura). If no quantifier appears, it is absolute (muhmala).
4
For quantified, distinguish universal vs. particular
If the quantifier indicates 'all' (kull, jamī'), it is universal (kulliyya). If it indicates 'some' (ba'd) or an indefinite after negation, it is particular (juz'iyya).
5
Combine quality and quantity to get one of eight types
For categorical propositions, there are two qualities × four quantity types (makhsusa, muhmala, kulliyya, juz'iyya) = eight distinct forms. For example, 'Every man is animal' is universal affirmative; 'No stone is man' is universal negative.
The text enumerates two makhsusa, two muhmala, and four mahsura (two universal and two particular) as the eight types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع القضية من حيث الإيجاب والسلب
ومما مرَّ عُلِمَ أن القضية حملية كانت أو شرطية متصلة أو منفصلة: إما موجبة (أي: مثبتة خالية من آلة السلب وهي آلة النفي) أي: حكم فيها بالإيقاع كقولنا في الحملية: زيد كاتب. وإما سالبة (أي منفية فيها آلة السلب وهي آلة النفي) إن كان الحكم فيها بالانتزاع كقولنا فيها: زيد ليس بكاتب. وأمثلة الشرطيات قد تقدمت. فالكتابة ثابتة لزيد. هو انتزاع الكتابة عن زيد. حيث مثل للشرطية المتصلة بقوله: ليس إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود. وللشرطية المتصلة بقوله: ليس إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو منقسماً إلى متساويين، والسلب ليس مسلطاً على المقدم ولا على التالي بل على التنافي والتباين. أي: ليس بين المقدم والتالي تباين.

أنواع الحملية من تقييد الموضوع بالكمية وعدم تقييده
وكل واحد منهما (أي من الموجبة والسالبة): إما مخصوصة، أو محصورة، أو مهملة. والمحصورة: إما كلية أو جزئية. ففي القضايا مخصوصتان، ومهملتان، ومحصورات أربع. وذلك لأن الحكم في كل من الموجبة والسالبة: إما على موضوع مشخص وهي المخصوصة. وإما على غيره. فإن بين فيها كمية الأفراد كلاً كانت أو بعضاً بذكر السور (أي اللفظ الدال عليها) فمحصورة. وإلا فمهملة. 
(١) أي: موضوعها معين وهو ما يسميه النحاة المعارف الست مثل العلم واسم الإشارة واسم الموصول.
(٢) أي محصورة فيها أداة حصر تشمل الأفراد أو بعضها الكلية مثل كل وجميع وأل الاستغراقية أو نكرة بعد النفي، والجزئية مثل بعض.
(٣) هي موضوعها ليس مشخصاً وهي خالية من سور الكل أو البعض.
(٤) موجبة وسالبة مثل: خالد مجتهد، خالد ليس مهملاً.
(٥) مثل الإنسان حيوان، ليس الحجر بحيوان.
(٦) مثل: كل إنسان حيوان، بعض الحيوان إنسان. ومثل: لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس إنساناً.
Types of proposition in terms of affirmation and negation. From what has passed, it is known that the proposition, whether categorical or conditional conjunctive or disjunctive, is either affirmative (i.e., positive, devoid of the tool of negation which is the tool of denial), meaning a judgment therein of occurrence, as we say in the categorical: 'Zaid is a writer.' Or negative (i.e., negated, containing the tool of negation which is the tool of denial), if the judgment therein is of removal, as we say therein: 'Zaid is not a writer.' Examples of conditionals have preceded. So writing is affirmed for Zaid. It is the removal of writing from Zaid. Where he gave an example for the conjunctive conditional by his saying: 'It is not that if the sun is risen then the night exists.' And for the disjunctive conditional by his saying: 'It is not that either the number is even or divisible into two equals,' and the negation is not applied to the antecedent nor to the consequent but to the incompatibility and separation, meaning there is no incompatibility between the antecedent and consequent.

Types of categorical in terms of restricting the subject by quantity and not restricting it. And each of them (i.e., affirmative and negative) is either particular, quantified, or absolute. And the quantified is either universal or particular. So in the propositions there are two particulars, two absolutes, and four quantifieds. That is because the judgment in each of affirmative and negative is either upon a specified subject—which is the particular; or upon other than it. If the quantity of individuals is stated therein, whether all or some, by mentioning the quantifier (i.e., the word indicating it)—then it is quantified. Otherwise—it is absolute.
(1) i.e., its subject is definite, which the grammarians call the six knowns, like proper nouns, demonstrative pronouns, and relative pronouns.
(2) i.e., quantified therein a tool of quantification that includes the individuals or some of them—universal like 'every', 'all', the comprehensive 'al', or an indefinite after negation; and particular like 'some'.
(3) Its subject is not specified, and it is devoid of a quantifier of all or some.
(4) Affirmative and negative like: 'Khalid is hardworking', 'Khalid is not lazy'.
(5) Like 'Man is an animal', 'Stone is not an animal'.
(6) Like: 'Every man is an animal', 'Some animals are men'. And like: 'No stone is a man', 'Some animals are not men'.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of propositions. The author first distinguishes propositions by quality (affirmative/negative), then by quantity (particular/quantified/absolute). The passage opens by reminding that every proposition—whether categorical or conditional—is either affirmative (judging that the predicate holds for the subject) or negative (judging that it is removed). Examples are given for categoricals: 'Zaid is a writer' (affirmative) and 'Zaid is not a writer' (negative). For conditionals, the author notes that examples were given previously, and clarifies that negation in conditionals applies to the relation (e.g., incompatibility) not to the parts. Then the author transitions to categorical propositions specifically, classifying each (affirmative and negative) into three types based on the subject: 'makhsusa' (particular: subject is a specific individual like 'Zaid'), 'mahsura' (quantified: subject's quantity is stated with a quantifier), and 'muhmala' (absolute: no quantifier). The quantified is further divided into universal (e.g., 'every') and particular (e.g., 'some'). The author enumerates that there are two individuals (makhsusa saaliba and mujiba), two absolutes, and four quantified (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative)—totaling eight types. This classification is foundational for understanding the structure of categorical propositions in logic.

Classifying Categorical Propositions by Quantification
Author classifies categorical propositions into particular, quantified, and indefinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as particular (makhṣūṣa), quantified (maḥṣūra, specifying universal or particular), or indefinite (muhmala) by examining its subject and quantifier.

Core Idea
The three types of categorical propositions are distinguished by the quantification of the subject.

In Islamic logic, every categorical proposition carries a judgment about a subject. But the way that subject is quantified—whether it refers to a specific individual, a quantity expressed by a word like 'every' or 'some', or no quantity at all—determines the proposition's type. This classification is essential for understanding how propositions function in syllogisms, especially when determining mood and validity. In this lesson, you will learn to identify each type directly from the text of any Islamic argument.

Guided Path
1
Locate the subject of the proposition
Find the term in the proposition that is being judged upon. For example, in 'Every scholar is knowledgeable', the subject is 'scholar'.
Ignore modifiers like 'every' or 'some' for now; just identify the core subject term.
2
Check if the subject is a specific individual
If the subject is a proper name (e.g., Zayd), a demonstrative (e.g., this man), or a relative pronoun (e.g., who is standing), the proposition is makhṣūṣa (particular singular). Otherwise, proceed.
Grammarians call these the 'six known things'—proper nouns, demonstratives, relative pronouns, etc.
3
Look for a quantifier word (sūr)
If there is no specific individual, check for a word like 'every', 'all', 'some', 'none', or the definite article for generality. If present, the proposition is maḥṣūra (quantified). Then decide: if it covers all individuals (e.g., 'every'), it is universal (kulliyya); if only some (e.g., 'some'), it is particular (juz'iyya). If no quantifier word appears, the proposition is muhmala (indefinite).
Examples: 'Every man is mortal' (universal), 'Some man is just' (particular), 'Man is mortal' (indefinite).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفوائد الفنارية 

أنواع الحملية من تقييد الموضوع بالكمية وعدم تقييده 

وكُلّ واحدٍ منهما ) أي : من الموجبة والسالبة : إما مخصوصة)، أو محصورة)، أو مهملة (۳) 
والمحصورة: إما كلية أو جزئية. 
ففي القضايا مخصوصتان)، ومُهملتانِ، ومَحْصُورات أربع. وذلك لأنَّ الحكم في كُلّ من الموجبة والسالبة: 
١ - إما على موضوع مشخص - وهي المخصُوصَةً. 
وإما على غيره. 
فإن بين فيها كمية الأفرادِ كُلاً كانَتْ أو بعْضًا بذكرِ السُّورِ أَيْ ٢ - ٣ - اللفظ الدالّ عليها - فمحضورة. 
٤ - وإلا - فَمهملة.

(۲) أي محصورة فيها أداة حصر تشمل الأفراد أو بعضها الكلية مثل: كل وجميع، وأل الاستغراقية، أو نكرة بعد النفي، والجزئية: مثل: بعض. (۳) هي موضوعها ليس مشخصاً، وهي خالية من سور الكل أو البعض. (٤) موجبة وسالبة مثل : خالد مجتهد، خالد ليس مهملاً.

(٦) مثل : كل إنسان حيوان بعض الحيوان إنسان. 
ومثل : لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس إنساناً.
Al-Fawaid al-Fanariyya

Types of categorical propositions regarding the qualification of the subject with quantity and its absence.

And each one of them (i.e., affirmative and negative) is either particular, or quantified, or indefinite.
And the quantified: either universal or particular.
Thus, among the propositions there are two particulars, two indefinites, and four quantified ones. That is because the judgment in each of the affirmative and negative:
1. Either upon a specific subject – and that is the particular.
2. Or upon other than it.
Then if the quantity of individuals, whether all or some, is clarified by mentioning the determinative (sūr), i.e., the word indicating it – then it is quantified.
3. Otherwise – then it is indefinite.
(1) Meaning: its subject is determined, and it is what grammarians call the six known things, like: proper noun, demonstrative pronoun, relative pronoun.
(2) Meaning: quantified by an instrument of restriction that encompasses all or some individuals; universal like: 'every', 'all', the definite article for generality, or an indefinite after negation; and particular like: 'some'.
(3) Its subject is not specific, and it is devoid of a determinative of all or some.
(4) Affirmative and negative, like: Khālid is diligent, Khālid is not negligent.
(5) Like: Man is an animal, stone is not an animal.
(6) Like: Every man is an animal, some animal is man. And like: Nothing of stone is man, and some animal is not man.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of categorical (ḥamliyya) propositions according to the quantification of their subject. The author begins by stating that every affirmative or negative proposition is either particular (makhṣūṣa), quantified (maḥṣūra), or indefinite (muhmala). The quantified is further divided into universal (kulliyya) and particular (juz'iyya). The total number of proposition types is thus two particulars, two indefinites, and four quantified ones—eight in all, counting both affirmative and negative. The author then explains each type: (1) A particular proposition has a specific subject (e.g., proper name, demonstrative, or relative pronoun). (2) If the quantity of individuals is specified by a determinative (sūr) such as 'every' or 'some', it is quantified. (3) If not, it is indefinite. The footnotes give examples: a particular proposition like 'Khālid is diligent'; a quantified universal like 'Every man is an animal'; a quantified particular like 'Some animal is man'; an indefinite like 'Man is an animal' (without 'every'). The classification is foundational for analyzing categorical syllogisms, where the mood depends on the quantifiers.

Types of Conditional Propositions by Temporal Quantification
Author classifies conditionals by temporal quantification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional propositions as particular, quantified (universal or particular), or indefinite based on temporal quantification.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are quantified by time, not by subjects.

In categorical propositions, quantification is about the subject term. But when logicians turn to conditionals, a different dimension emerges: temporal quantification. This lesson shows you how to identify whether a conditional is tied to a specific moment, covers a temporal range, or is left indefinite. Mastering this classification is essential for working with conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three-way division
The author divides conditionals into particular (makhṣūṣa), quantified (maḥṣūra), and indefinite (muhmala). The basis is whether a specific time, a temporal quantifier (all or part of time), or no temporal information is indicated.
This mirrors the tripartite division of categorical propositions, but here quantification applies to time, not to individuals.
2
Identify the particular conditional (makhṣūṣa)
Look for an explicit time or moment, e.g., 'now' or 'at this instant'. The judgment of conjunction or disjunction holds only at that specific time. Example: 'If Zayd comes now, honor him now.'
The time may be indicated by a temporal adverb or context.
3
Identify the quantified conditional (maḥṣūra)
If the conditional uses a temporal quantifier like 'whenever' (kullamā) for universal, or 'sometimes' (qad) for particular, it is quantified. Universal covers all times, particular covers some times.
Example: 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists' (universal). 'Sometimes if something is an animal, it is human' (particular).
4
Identify the indefinite conditional (muhmala)
If there is no temporal specification or quantifier, the conditional is indefinite. Example: 'If the sun is rising, daytime exists.' The truth depends on the actual state, not on a temporal range.
Indefinite conditionals are the default when no quantifier appears.
5
Apply the same to disjunctive conditionals
The classification works identically for disjunctive conditionals (e.g., 'Always, a number is either even or odd' is universal quantified; 'Sometimes this thing is either an animal or a human' is particular quantified; 'Either the sun is rising or daytime does not exist' is indefinite).
Practice identifying the type from the examples given by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشَّرطِيَّاتِ فَإِنْ كَانَ الحكم فيها بالاتصال) والانفصال (۲) في زمانٍ معين فَمَخْصُوْصَةٌ. 
وإلا فإن بيِّنَ فيها كَميةُ الزمانِ جميعِهِ (۳) أو بعضه(٤) فَمحصورةٌ، 
وإلا فمهملة). 
(۱) مثل : إن جاء زيد الآن فأكرمه الآن. (۲) مثل: زيد في هذا الآن إما كاتب أو غير كاتب. (۳) مثل : كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود. (٤) مثل : قد يكون إذا كان الشيء حيوانا كان إنسانا. (٥) مثل : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود. هذه أمثلة الشرطية المتصلة. أما الشرطية المنفصلة فكالآتي: 
١. الشرطية المتصلة المخصوصة مثل: إن صاح الديك الآن فعبدي 
.٢ الشرطية المنفصلة الكلية مثل: دائما إما أن يكون العدد زوجا 
أو فرداً. 
.٣ الشرطية المنفصلة الجزئية مثل: قد يكون إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً أو إنساناً. ٤. الشرطية المنفصلة المهملة مثل: إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن لا يكون النهار موجوداً.
As for conditionals: if the judgment in them is about conjunction or disjunction in a specific time, then it is particular. Otherwise, if the quantity of all or some of the time is clarified, then it is quantified; otherwise, indefinite. (1) Example: if Zayd comes now, then honor him now. (2) Example: Zayd in this now is either a writer or not a writer. (3) Example: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists. (4) Example: Sometimes if something is an animal, it is a human. (5) Example: If the sun is rising, then daytime exists. These are examples of the conditional conjunctive. As for the disjunctive conditional, then as follows: 1. The particular conjunctive conditional: e.g., If the rooster crows now, then my slave is free. 2. The universal disjunctive conditional: e.g., Always, a number is either even or odd. 3. The particular disjunctive conditional: e.g., Sometimes, this thing is either an animal or a human. 4. The indefinite disjunctive conditional: e.g., Either the sun is rising, or daytime does not exist.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional (sharṭiyya) propositions based on temporal quantification. The author explains: if the judgment of conjunction (ittiṣāl) or disjunction (infiṣāl) is tied to a specific time, the conditional is particular (makhṣūṣa), like 'If Zayd comes now, honor him now'. If not, but the quantity of time (all or part) is clarified, it is quantified (maḥṣūra), either universal (kulliyya) like 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists', or particular (juz'iyya) like 'Sometimes if something is an animal, it is human'. If no temporal quantity is indicated, it is indefinite (muhmala), e.g., 'If the sun is rising, daytime exists'. The author then gives separate examples for conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals: particular conjunctive ('If the rooster crows now, my slave is free'), universal disjunctive ('Always, a number is either even or odd'), particular disjunctive ('Sometimes, this thing is either an animal or a human'), and indefinite disjunctive ('Either the sun is rising or daytime does not exist'). This classification is crucial for understanding conditional syllogisms, as the mood depends on whether the conditional is temporally quantified.

Conditional propositions in Islamic logic are classified by their temporal quantification: particular (tied to a specific time), quantified (by a temporal quantifier), or indefinite (no temporal specification).

The Exhaustiveness of Proposition Classification: The Natural Proposition Objection
Author addresses an objection that the division of propositions omits the natural proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the natural proposition (tabi'iyya) and explain why it is excluded from the standard classification of propositions used in logical productions.

Core Idea
Not every type of proposition belongs in the logician's classification—only those used in actual reasoning.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: the division of propositions is incomplete because it omits the 'natural proposition' (qadiyya tabi'iyya), like 'Man is a species.' The author responds by narrowing the scope to propositions used in sciences and logical productions (intajat), where judgments concern particulars, not natures. This lesson unpacks the dialectical exchange and clarifies when a proposition type belongs in a logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (naqḍ)
The objector claims the division of propositions (into makhsusa, mahsura, etc.) is not exhaustive because it leaves out the natural proposition. Understand what the natural proposition is: it predicates something on the nature or essence of the subject, not on its individuals, e.g., 'Man is a species.'
The natural proposition is not about particular individuals; it is about the universal nature.
2
Understand the natural proposition (qadiyya ṭabīʿiyya)
The author defines the natural proposition as one where judgment is on the nature (ṭabīʿa) of the subject. Examples: 'Man is a species' (al-insānu nawʿun), 'Animal is a genus' (al-ḥayawānu jinsun). The predicate applies to the essence, not to each individual.
This type of proposition is common in metaphysical discussions but not in productive reasoning.
3
Isolate the author's response (jawāb)
The author restricts the domain: the division applies only to propositions used in the sciences and logical productions (intājāt). In these contexts, judgments are made on the particulars (juzʾiyyāt) of the subject, not on its nature. Therefore, the natural proposition is irrelevant and rightly excluded.
The author's response depends on the purpose of the classification: it is for propositions that serve as premises in syllogisms.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
The objector's challenge fails because it misapplies the scope. The classification is exhaustive within its intended domain. This shows the importance of defining the subject matter precisely in logical discourse.
This is a common move in Islamic logic: clarifying the scope of a division to defend its exhaustiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: التقسيم غير حاصر؛ لعدم ذكر الطبيعية فيه. قُلتُ: موردُ القِسمةِ القضية المستعملة في العلوم والإنتاجات. وهي التي حُكِمَ فيها على جزئيات الموضوع لا على طبيعته كما بُيِّنَ فِي المُطولات. (١) أي: الحكم فيها ليس على الأفراد مثل: الإنسان نوع، والحيوان جنس، فالحكم بالنوعية ليس على أفراده بل على طبيعة الإنسان، وعدم ذكرها مع أنواع القضايا تكون القسمة غير مستوفية للأقسام. (۲) أي: في الإنتاجات لا حاجة إلى الطبيعية؛ لأن الحكم يكون على الأفراد لا على الطبيعة للأشياء.
If you say: The division is not exhaustive because it does not mention the natural [proposition]. I say: The subject of the division is the proposition used in the sciences and logical productions. It is that in which judgment is made on the particulars of the subject, not on its nature, as was explained in the lengthy works. (1) That is: the judgment is not on individuals; for example, 'Man is a species,' 'Animal is a genus'; the judgment of specieshood is not on its individuals but on the nature of man, and not mentioning it with the types of propositions makes the division incomplete. (2) That is: in productions, there is no need for the natural [proposition], because judgment is on individuals, not on the nature of things.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an objection-response (naqd wa jawab) regarding the classification of propositions. The objector claims the division of propositions (into makhsusa, mahsura, muhmala, etc.) is not exhaustive because it omits the 'natural proposition' (qadiyya tabi'iyya). The natural proposition is one where the predicate is affirmed of the nature of the subject, not of its individuals, e.g., 'Man is a species' — here 'species' is true of the essence of man, not of each individual man. The author responds by narrowing the scope: the division applies only to propositions used in the sciences and logical productions (intajat). In such productions, judgments are always about the particulars (individuals) of the subject, not about its nature. Therefore, the natural proposition is irrelevant to this division and is rightly excluded. The response clarifies that logical reasoning deals with individual instances, not abstract natures, so the classification remains adequate for its intended purpose. The footnotes (1) and (2) elaborate on the natural proposition and the reason for its exclusion.

Why Natural Propositions Are Excluded from the Division
Author addresses an objection that the division of propositions omits natural propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why natural propositions are excluded from the standard division of propositions in syllogistic logic.

Core Idea
Not all propositions are used in syllogisms; natural propositions are excluded because they judge on essences, not individuals.

In this brief dialectical exchange, an objector points out a potential gap in the logic textbook's division of propositions: it seems to omit 'natural propositions'. The author's response reveals an important principle: the division is not meant to cover every kind of proposition, only those that actually function in scientific and syllogistic reasoning. Let's examine how the author defends his classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims the division of propositions is not exhaustive (ghayr hasir) because it does not mention the natural proposition (al-tabi'iyya). This proposition affirms a predicate of the subject's nature, e.g., 'Human is a species.'
2
Analyze the author's response
The author restricts the domain of the division: it applies only to propositions 'used in the sciences and syllogisms' (al-musta'mala fi al-'ulum wa al-intajat). He states that in such propositions, judgment is made on the particulars of the subject (juz'iyyat al-mawdu'), not on its nature.
3
Understand the rationale
Syllogisms require universal conclusions derived from premises about individual instances. Natural propositions, which describe essences, do not serve this function. They are relevant for definitions but not for syllogistic inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: التقسيم غير حاصر؛ لعدم ذكر الطبيعية فيه. قلت: مورد القسمة القضية المستعملة في العلوم والإنتاجات. وهي التي حكم فيها على جزئيات الموضوع لا على طبيعته كما بين في المطولات.

This passage is a dialectical exchange (naqd wa jawab). The objector claims that the author's earlier division of propositions (into affirmative and negative, etc.) is not exhaustive because it leaves out the 'natural proposition' (al-qadiyya al-tabi'iyya), where the judgment is on the nature of the subject (e.g., 'Man is a species') rather than on individuals. The author replies that the division concerns only those propositions used in the sciences and syllogistic demonstrations (al-istighlal fi al-'ulum wa al-intajat), where judgments are always on individuals (juz'iyyat al-mawdu') to enable universal conclusions. Natural propositions are excluded because they are not employed in syllogisms; their role is in definitions and general concepts. The author references 'lengthy works' (al-mutawwalat) for further clarification.

Quantifiers in Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates the universal and particular quantifiers for categorical and conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct quantifier (sawr) for any given categorical or conditional proposition in Arabic.

Core Idea
Quantifiers determine the scope of a proposition; categoricals use 'kull', 'ba'd', etc., while conditionals use temporal terms like 'da'iman'.

This lesson introduces the key quantifiers (sawar) that logicians use to indicate whether a proposition is universal or particular. In Islamic logic, mastering these quantifiers is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh. The author enumerates them for both categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya) propositions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish proposition types
Recognize whether the proposition is categorical (subject-predicate) or conditional (if-then or either-or). The quantifier words differ.
Categoricals: 'كل إنسان كاتب'. Conditionals: 'دائماً إذا كان النهار فهو موجود'.
2
Learn categorical quantifiers
Memorize the four quantifier sets: universal affirmative (كل), particular affirmative (بعض, واحد), universal negative (لا شيء, لا واحد), particular negative (ليس كل, ليس بعض, بعض ليس).
In particular negative, the order can vary: 'ليس بعض' vs 'بعض ليس'.
3
Learn conditional quantifiers
For universal affirmative in conditionals, use temporal words: دائماً, كلما, متى, مهما. For particular affirmative: قد يكون.
The author implies that negative quantifiers for conditionals follow by analogy.
4
Test with examples
Given an Arabic proposition, classify it as categorical or conditional, then name its quantifier and quantity (universal/particular, affirmative/negative).
E.g., 'كل خمر حرام' → categorical, universal affirmative, quantifier: كل.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of quantifiers (sawar) used in quantified propositions. The author begins by restating that affirmative and negative propositions are either particular (makhsusa, i.e., indefinite without quantifier) or quantified. He gives examples of universal quantified ('Every human is a writer', 'No human is a writer') and particular quantified ('Some human is a writer', 'Not some human is a writer', etc.). Then he lists the specific quantifier words: for categoricals (hamliyya), 'kull' for universal affirmative, 'ba'd' and 'wahid' for particular affirmative, 'la shay'' and 'la wahid' for universal negative, and for particular negative: 'laysa kull', 'laysa ba'd', and 'ba'd laysa' (with the note that the order of negation and 'ba'd' may vary). For conditional propositions (shartiyya), the universal affirmative quantifiers are temporal: 'da'iman' (always), 'kullama' (whenever), 'mata' (when), 'mahma' (whatever), and their synonyms. The particular affirmative quantifier is 'qad yakun' (it may be). The passage ends abruptly, likely expecting the reader to know the negative quantifiers for conditionals by analogy.

Quantifiers in Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates quantifiers for universal and particular affirmative and negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct quantifier (sawr) for each type of categorical and conditional proposition (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) from a given list.

Core Idea
Quantifiers determine the logical scope of a proposition — 'all', 'some', 'none', 'not all'.

In logic, the quantifier (sawr) attached to the subject specifies whether the proposition applies to all members of a class or only some, and whether it affirms or denies. This lesson will walk you through the standard quantifiers for categorical and conditional propositions as enumerated by the author, so you can quickly identify the quantity and quality of any logical proposition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the four types of categorical propositions
Recall that categorical propositions can be universal affirmative (A), particular affirmative (I), universal negative (E), or particular negative (O). Note the corresponding quantifiers for each type in the passage.
2
Identify quantifiers for categoricals
From the passage, extract the quantifiers: for UA: 'كل' (kull), for PA: 'بعض' (ba‘ḍ) or 'واحد' (wāḥid), for UN: 'لا شيء' (lā shay’) or 'لا واحد' (lā wāḥida), and for PN: 'ليس كل' (laysa kull), 'ليس بعض' (laysa ba‘ḍ), 'بعض ليس' (ba‘ḍun laysa).
3
Extend to conditional propositions
Conditionals have their own quantifiers: UA: 'دائماً' (dā’iman), 'كلما' (kullamā), etc.; PA: 'قد يكون' (qad yakūnu); UN: 'ليس البتة' (laysa al-battata); PN: 'قد لا يكون' (qad lā yakūnu), 'ليس دائماً' (laysa dā’iman), etc.
4
Understand the muhmala (indefinite) proposition
A proposition without any quantifier is called muhmala. It has the logical force of a particular (juz’iyya) because judging all individuals implies judging some individuals, and vice versa.

And each of the affirmative and the negative is either particular as we mentioned from their examples, or universal quantified, like our saying: 'Every human is a writer' and 'No human is a writer' or 'Not one human is a writer'. Or particular quantified, like our saying: 'Some human is a writer' or 'Not one human is a writer', or 'Not some human is a writer', or 'Not every human is a writer'. And from this it is known that the quantifier in categoricals for universal affirmative is 'kull', for particular affirmative is 'ba‘ḍ' and 'wāḥid', for universal negative is 'lā shay'' and 'lā wāḥida', and for particular negative is 'laysa kull', 'laysa ba‘ḍ', 'ba‘ḍun laysa'. And let it also be known in conditionals: the quantifier for universal affirmative is 'dā’iman', 'kullamā', 'matā', 'mahmā' and what is in their meaning; for particular affirmative is 'qad yakūnu'; for universal negative is 'laysa al-battata'; and for particular negative is 'qad lā yakūnu', 'laysa dā’iman', 'laysa kullamā', and 'laysa mahmā'. The purpose of mentioning the quantifiers is to give examples that are well-known in usage, not to enumerate exhaustively. For 'qāṭibatan', 'kāffatan', and the 'lām' of inclusion are valid as quantifiers for the universal affirmative categorical, as the shaykh indicated in al-Shifā’. And they may not be [quantified]—i.e., particular or quantified—they are called 'muhmala' (indefinite) due to the omission of the quantifier in them, like our saying in categoricals: 'Man is rational', and in conditionals: 'If Zayd comes, or when Zayd comes, honor him.' And the indefinite has the force of the particular, because the judgment on the individuals of a thing in general and the judgment on some of its parts are mutually concomitant positively and conversely.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions (qadaya) according to quantity and defines their quantifiers (aswar). The author first distinguishes particular (makhsusa), universal quantified (kulliyya musawwara), and particular quantified (juz'iyya musawwara) for both affirmative and negative categorical propositions. Examples are given: 'Every human is a writer' (universal affirmative), 'No human is a writer' or 'Not one human is a writer' (universal negative), 'Some human is a writer' (particular affirmative), and 'Not some human is a writer' or 'Not every human is a writer' (particular negative). From these examples, it is concluded that the standard quantifier for universal affirmative is 'kull' (all), for particular affirmative is 'ba‘ḍ' (some) or 'wāḥid' (one), for universal negative is 'lā shay'' (nothing) or 'lā wāḥida' (not one), and for particular negative is 'laysa kull' (not all), 'laysa ba‘ḍ' (not some), or 'ba‘ḍun laysa' (some...not). The author then extends the discussion to conditional propositions (shartiyya): the universal affirmative quantifier is 'dā’iman' (always), 'kullamā' (whenever), 'matā' (when), 'mahmā' (whatever), and similar. The particular affirmative is 'qad yakūnu' (sometimes). The universal negative is 'laysa al-battata' (absolutely not). The particular negative is 'qad lā yakūnu' (sometimes not), 'laysa dā’iman' (not always), 'laysa kullamā' (not whenever), and 'laysa mahmā' (not whatever). The author clarifies that these lists are not exhaustive but rather cite the most common and well-known quantifiers; for example, 'qāṭibatan' (all together), 'kāffatan' (entirely), and the 'lām' of inclusion (like 'al-rijālu' meaning 'the men collectively') are also recognized as universal quantifiers for categoricals, as indicated by Ibn Sīnā in al-Shifā’. Propositions that lack any quantifier are called 'muhmala' (indefinite), e.g., the categorical 'Man is rational' and the conditional 'If Zayd comes, honor him'. The indefinite has the logical force of a particular proposition because judging all individuals in general entails judging some individuals, and vice versa.

Key Terms
سور (sawr): A linguistic particle that specifies the quantity (universality or particularity) of a proposition's subject, e.g., 'kull', 'ba‘ḍ'. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the logical tradition.]
مهملة (muhmala): A proposition lacking a quantifier (like 'insānun nāṭiq'), treated as particular in logical force. [Note: In some later texts, muhmala may be distinguished from mursala, but here it simply means unquantified.]

Why the Indefinite Proposition is Not Universal
Author explains why indefinite is not universal and presents a table of categorical proposition divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish indefinite propositions from universal and particular propositions, and classify any categorical proposition into its correct type using the table.

Core Idea
The indefinite attaches to the particular, not the universal, because it is realized when some individuals have the predicate.

In this passage, the author resolves a common confusion: why the indefinite proposition (e.g., 'Human is a writer') is treated as particular rather than universal. He then provides a complete table of categorical proposition types that you can use as a reference for any example you encounter. Let's work through his reasoning and build your classification skills.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three examples
Look at the three examples: 'Every human is an animal' (universal), 'Some human is a writer' (particular), 'Human is a writer' (indefinite). Notice how the indefinite lacks any quantifier ('every' or 'some').
2
Test why indefinite is not universal
If you try to treat 'Human is a writer' as universal, you would need every human to be a writer, which is false. But the sentence is often used when only some humans are writers. So it must be attached to the particular, where it is realized (true for some).
3
Learn the five categories from the table
The table lists: personal (specific subject like 'Khalid is a fighter'), specific (no benefit in syllogism), universal (quantifier indicating all, e.g., 'Every hardworking succeeds'), particular (quantifier indicating some, e.g., 'Some students are present'), indefinite (no quantifier, e.g., 'The army is ready for battle').
Note that 'personal' has a singular subject, while 'specific' is a different category.
4
Practice classifying propositions
Take any categorical proposition from Islamic texts. Determine its quantity (universal, particular, indefinite) and whether it is personal or specific. Use the table as a guide.

(1) 'al-muntashir': i.e., in some indefinite times. And it is better to make the reason for attachment that the indefinite is realized with the particular and is not realized with the universal. Clarification of that: 'Every human is an animal' universal. 'Some human is a writer' particular. 'Human is a writer' indefinite. Universal. If we attach it to the universal, then we must ensure that every individual of it is a writer, which is not realized. And 'human is a writer' may be used when some of them are writers, and it is not used for all because it is not realized; since it is necessary that all individuals be writers to attach it to the universal. Therefore, it is attached to what is realized (particular) and not attached to what is not realized (universal). And here is a table of the divisions of the categorical proposition: Personal or whose subject is specific: affirmative: Khalid is a fighter; negative: Khalid is not a coward. Particular: whose quantifier indicates some individuals: affirmative: some students are present; negative: some students are not present. Indefinite: which lacks both quantifiers: affirmative: the army is ready for battle; negative: the army is not cowardly.

Commentary
This passage clarifies why the indefinite proposition is attached to the particular, not the universal, and then presents a table categorizing categorical propositions. The author begins with a footnote explaining 'al-muntashir' (distributed time) and then asserts that the indefinite is realized with the particular and not with the universal. To illustrate: 'Every human is an animal' is universal, 'Some human is a writer' is particular, and 'Human is a writer' is indefinite. If we try to treat the indefinite as universal, we would need every individual human to be a writer, which is not the case; thus the indefinite is attached to the particular, which is realized when some individuals have the predicate. Four Types of Categorical Propositions by Quantity
Author details the four types of categorical propositions with quantifiers and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify categorical propositions as personal, universal, particular, or indefinite based on their quantifier and subject type.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has a quantity determined by its quantifier or lack thereof.

In this passage, the author breaks down categorical propositions into four types based on their quantity. Understanding these types is essential for building valid syllogisms in Islamic logic. Pay close attention to the quantifier signs and the special case of indefinite propositions, which have no quantifier but behave like particular ones in many contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier sign (sawr)
Look for words like 'كل' (every), 'لا شيء' (no), or 'بعض' (some) in the proposition. If a quantifier is present, the proposition is either universal or particular. If absent, it is indefinite.
The quantifier determines the quantity: universal covers all, particular covers some.
2
Distinguish universal from particular
Universal quantifiers ('every', 'no') encompass all individuals of the subject. Particular quantifiers ('some', 'not all') cover only a part. For example, 'Every striver succeeds' is universal; 'Some students are present' is particular.
Universal propositions are key in syllogisms; particular ones introduce exceptions.
3
Recognize personal propositions
A personal proposition has a specific individual as its subject, like 'Khalid is a fighter'. The author notes that these are not useful in syllogisms because they cannot be generalized.
Personal propositions are also called 'shakhsiyya' or 'makhsusa'.
4
Analyze the indefinite proposition
A proposition with no quantifier, like 'The army is ready for battle', is indefinite. The author discusses whether it is equivalent to the universal or particular, concluding that it behaves like the particular because it does not require all individuals.
Indefinite propositions are context-dependent; treat them as particular unless context demands universal.

And the ruling is likewise for a scattered time, with the absolute judgment they are mutually requisite. (1) Scattered: meaning in some unspecified times. It is better to make the cause of annexation that the indefinite is realized with the particular and not realized with the universal. Clarification: 'Every human is an animal' is universal. 'Some human is a writer' is particular. 'Human is a writer' is indefinite. The universal: if we annex it to the universal, it is necessary to ascertain that every individual of it is a writer, which is not realized. 'Human is a writer' may be uttered if some of them are writers, and is not uttered for the whole, because it is not realized, since it is necessary for all individuals to be writers for annexation to it. Therefore, it is annexed to the realized and not annexed to the non-realized. And here is a table of the divisions of the categorical proposition. The personal or whose subject is determined: affirmative: Khalid is a fighter; negative: Khalid is not a coward. The universal: what contains a quantifier indicating encompassing all individuals: affirmative: Every striver succeeds; negative: None of the lazy achieve success. The particular: what contains a quantifier indicating some individuals: affirmative: Some students are present; negative: Some students are not present. The indefinite: what is devoid of the two quantifiers: affirmative: [السابقين] ; negative: The army is ready for battle; The army is not cowardly.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines the division of categorical propositions by quantity. The author begins by discussing the indefinite (muhmala), explaining that it behaves like a particular proposition in being realized with some instances but not like a universal, which requires all instances. The indefinite has no quantifier. A table then lists four types: (1) Personal (shakhsiyya): subject is a specific individual, e.g., 'Khalid is a fighter'. (2) Universal (kulliyya): quantifier indicates all individuals, e.g., 'Every striver succeeds'. (3) Particular (juz'iyya): quantifier indicates some, e.g., 'Some students are present'. (4) Indefinite (muhmala): no quantifier, e.g., 'The army is ready for battle'. The author notes that personal propositions are not useful in syllogisms (قياس) because their subject is a single individual. This classification is fundamental for understanding how propositions function in syllogistic reasoning.

Categorical propositions are divided by quantity into personal, universal, particular, and indefinite, each distinguished by its quantifier or lack thereof.

Divisions of Conditional Conjunctive Propositions: Luzumiyya and Ittifaqiyya
Author divides conditional conjunctive propositions into implicative and coincidental, with subtypes of implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two main types of conditional conjunctive propositions (luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya) and identify the three subtypes of implication within luzumiyya.

Core Idea
Every conditional is either based on real implication or mere coincidence — only the first grounds sound reasoning.

In logic, not all 'if-then' statements are created equal. Some express a genuine causal connection, while others simply report that two facts happen to coexist. This distinction is essential for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic sciences, from fiqh rulings to kalam proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
Recognize that the author splits conditional conjunctive propositions into two categories based on whether the connection is grounded in implication (iqtida') or not.
The first is called luzumiyya, the second ittifaqiyya.
2
Examine the implicative type (luzumiyya)
Notice that it has three subtypes: antecedent causes consequent, consequent causes antecedent, or both are effects of a common cause.
For example, 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists' is antecedent cause; 'If daytime exists, then the sun is risen' is consequent cause; 'If daytime exists, then the world is illuminated' is common effect.
3
Spot mutual implication (tada'uf)
See that tada'uf is a special case where each part implies the other, like father-son relationship.
If Zayd is 'Amr's father, then 'Amr is Zayd's son, and vice versa.
4
Contrast with coincidental (ittifaqiyya)
Understand that in ittifaqiyya, the connection is merely accidental, e.g., 'If man is rational, then the donkey brays' — two unrelated truths.
There is no necessary entailment between antecedent and consequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الشرطية المتصلة
(والمتصلة) قسمان؛ لأنَّها (إمَّا ) أَنْ يكونَ الحُكم بالاتصال فيها مبنيا على الاقتضاء) وهي تُسمى: ١. (الزومِيّة): وذلِكَ إمّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المُقدّمُ عِلةً للتالي: (كقولنا: إِنْ كانتِ الشمس طالعةٌ فالنهار موجود) (٢). أو بأن يكونَ التالي علّةً للمقدَّم كَعَكْسِهِ. أو بأن يكونا مَعْلُوْلي علةٍ واحدةٍ، نحو : إِنْ كانَ النهارُ موجُوداً فالعالم مضي . كان عمرو . ومِنْهُ التضايِفُ () بينَهُما ، نحو: إِنْ كَانَ زِيدٌ أَبا عمرو ابنَه. (وإِمَّا أَنْ لا يَكونَ كذلك، بلْ يَكونُ الحكم بالأتصال بمجردِ الاتفاقِ وَتُسمى): (١) أي: ترابط وعلاقة بين المقدم والثاني، فإن بين طلوع الشمس ووجود النهار ترابطاً، أي وجود أحدهما يلزم منه وجود الآخر. (٢) فإن طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار. (٣) فوجود النهار وكون العالم مضيئاً معلولان لطلوع الشمس. (٤) أي من التلازم التضايف، فإنَّ زيداً هو أب بالإضافة لعمرو وعمرو هو ابن بالإضافة إلى زيد. (٥) أي: لا تلازم بين المقدم والتالي، بل وجدا على هاتين الصفتين، فإنهما خُلقا على انفراد ناطقية الإنسان وناهقية الحمار.
Divisions of the conditional conjunctive. (And the conjunctive) is two types; because it is either that the judgment of connection is based on implication, and it is called 1. Implicative (luzumiyya): and that is either that the antecedent is a cause of the consequent (like our saying: If the sun is rising, then daytime is present) (2). Or that the consequent is a cause of the antecedent, the reverse. Or that both are effects of a single cause, like: If daytime is present, then the world is illuminated. And from it is mutual implication (tada'uf) between them, like: If Zayd is the father of 'Amr, then 'Amr is his son. (And either it is not like that, but the judgment of connection is by mere agreement, and it is called): (1) Meaning a connection and relation between the antecedent and the second, for between sunrise and the existence of daytime there is a connection, meaning the existence of one necessitates the existence of the other. (2) For sunrise is a cause for the existence of daytime. (3) For the existence of daytime and the world being illuminated are effects of sunrise. (4) Meaning from the mutual implication is tada'uf, for Zayd is a father relative to 'Amr and 'Amr is a son relative to Zayd. (5) Meaning there is no implication between the antecedent and consequent, but they are found with these two attributes, for they were created separately: the rationality of man and the braying of the donkey.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional conjunctive propositions (الشروطية المتصلة), which are if-then statements. They are divided into two main categories: (1) Implicative (الزومية), where the connection is based on a real entailment (الاقتضاء), and (2) Coincidental (الاتفاقية), where the connection is merely by accidental agreement. The implicative type has three subtypes: (a) the antecedent is the cause of the consequent (e.g., 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists'), (b) the consequent is the cause of the antecedent (reverse of a), (c) both are effects of a common cause (e.g., 'If daytime exists, then the world is illuminated' — both caused by sunrise). Additionally, mutual implication (التضايف) is a special case where each implies the other, like fatherhood and sonship: if Zayd is 'Amr's father, then 'Amr is Zayd's son, and vice versa. The coincidental type has no real entailment; the antecedent and consequent just happen to be true together, e.g., 'If man is rational, then the donkey brays' — statements that are true individually but unrelated. The footnotes clarify the causal relationships, emphasizing that in the implicative type, one part is the cause or effect of the other or both are effects of a third. This classification is crucial for understanding conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i).

Key Terms
الشرطية المتصلة
الزومية
الاتفاقية
الاقتضاء
التضايف

Key Takeaway
Conditional conjunctive propositions are either 'implicative' (luzumiyya) with a real causal connection or 'coincidental' (ittifaqiyya) with accidental agreement; this classification is foundational for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Accidental Conditionals and the Meaning of Lack of Implication
Author defines accidental conditional and clarifies the meaning of 'lack of implication'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an accidental conditional (ittifāqiyya) and distinguish between the objective and epistemological meanings of 'lack of implication' (adam al-iqtidā').

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals connect states by coincidence, not implication; 'lack of implication' refers to the judge's ignorance of any necessary tie.

In Islamic logic, conditionals can be divided into those where the antecedent truly implies the consequent (luzūmiyya) and those where the connection is merely coincidental (ittifāqiyya). This lesson unpacks the definition and, crucially, clarifies what the logicians mean when they say 'there is no implication' in such a proposition. You will learn to spot a common misunderstanding and apply this distinction to theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accidental conditional
Consider the example: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' The two facts are both true but not causally linked. This is an accidental conditional because the connection is purely coincidental.
The example shows that truth of both parts does not imply a necessary connection.
2
Extract the definition
The author defines ittifāqiyya as a judgment of connection based solely on agreement in reality (ittifāq), without any implication (iqtidā'). The connection is not real but merely asserted due to coincidence.
The definition distinguishes it from implicative conditionals (luzūmiyya).
3
Understand 'lack of implication'
The author stresses that 'lack of implication' does not mean there is no necessary connection in reality. Rather, it means the judge (the person stating the conditional) is not aware of any such connection. This is an epistemological claim, not an ontological one.
This distinction prevents a potential objection about constant coexistence.
4
Apply the distinction to an objection
One might object: since rationality and braying always coexist as long as their complete causes persist, don't they imply each other? The author refutes this by noting that constant coexistence does not prove implication; implication requires a known causal link. The judge's lack of knowledge is key.
The objection is based on a misunderstanding of 'lack of implication' as objective non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ . (اتفاقية) كقولنا : إِنْ كانَ الإنسانُ ناطقاً فالحمارُ ناهِقٌ. فَإِنَّهُ حُكِمَ فيها بالإتصال بمجَرَّدِ الاتفاقِ بينَ ناطقيَّةِ الإنسانِ وناهقيَّةِ الحِمَارِ ؛ لأنَّهما خُلِقَا كذلك، لا أَنَّ بينهما اقتضاء). واعْلَمْ أَنَّ معنى عَدمِ الاقْتِضَاءِ: عَدَمُ عِلْمِ الحَاكِمِ بالاقتضاء " لا عدَمُهُ في نفس الأمرِ ) . فلا يَرِدُ ما يُقالُ: مِنْ أَنَّهُما لمَّا دَامَا دَامَتْ عِلَّتُهُما التامَّةُ فَامْتَنعَ انفكاك أحدِهِما عَنِ الآخَرِ. ولا نعني بالاقتضاء إلا ذَلِكَ). (۱) أي: لا تلازم بين نطق هذا مع نهيق هذا. (٢) أي: لم يحكم المتكلم بالترابط بين ناطقية الإنسان وناهقية الحمار. (۳) فالقول: إن الحمار ناهق والإنسان ناطق ما داما خُلقا على هذين الوصفين ولم نرَ أحدهما تعرى عنها فإنه تلازم، فنقول: التلازم هو ارتباط أحد الصفتين بالأخرى ولا ترابط بين نهيق الحمار وناطقية الإنسان. (٤) هذا كلام المعترض، وقد سبق الردّ عليه. (٥) هذه من القضايا الموجهة وهي نفس القضية السابقة إلا أنها غير مكيفة بكيفية الضرورة أو الدوام، وإن قيدت بأحدهما فهي القضية الموجهة، وقد أغفلها الماتن؛ لأن متنه أعده لبيان ما هو أهم من قواعد المنطق؛ لأن الموجهة قلما تستعمل في الإنتاجات فقوله وبهذا ينحل.. الخ أي: عدم الاقتضاء، أي عدم الحكم بالاقتضاء، لا عدمه في نفس الأمر. =
2. (Accidental) like our statement: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' For in it, connection is judged merely by the agreement between the rationality of man and the braying of the donkey; because they were created that way, not that there is implication between them. And know that the meaning of the lack of implication is: the lack of knowledge of the judge of implication, not its non-existence in reality. So what is said does not apply: that since they both persist, their complete cause persists, so the separation of one from the other is impossible. And we do not mean by implication except that. (1) That is: no entailment between the neighing of this and the braying of that. (2) That is: the speaker did not judge the interconnection between the rationality of man and the braying of the donkey. (3) So the statement: 'The donkey is braying and man is rational as long as they were created with these two attributes and we have not seen one of them devoid of them, then it is entailment.' We say: Entailment is the connection of one of the two attributes to the other, but there is no connection between the braying of the donkey and the rationality of man. (4) This is the speech of the objector, and the response has preceded. (5) These are of the directed propositions, and they are the same as the previous proposition except that they are not qualified with the modality of necessity or perpetuity; if they are restricted by one of them, then it is the directed proposition, and the author omitted it because his text prepared it for explaining what is more important from the rules of logic, because the directed is rarely used in productions. So his statement 'and by this is resolved' etc. means: the lack of implication, that is, the lack of judgment of implication, not its non-existence in reality.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines a type of conditional proposition called 'ittifāqiyya' (accidental conditional) and explains the crucial concept of 'iqtidā'' (implication). The author begins by giving an example: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' The connection between the antecedent and consequent here is purely coincidental—both are true because of how the creatures were created, not because one implies the other. The key point is the meaning of 'lack of implication': it does not mean that there is no necessary connection in reality; rather, it means that the judge (the person making the conditional statement) does not know of any necessary connection. This distinction is essential to avoid a potential objection: one might argue that since the two states (rationality and braying) always coexist as long as their complete causes persist, they cannot be separated, so there must be some implication. The author responds that implication here is defined relative to the judge's knowledge. The footnotes further elucidate: footnote (1) reiterates that there is no entailment between the two; (2) explains that the speaker does not judge a real interconnection; (3) addresses a possible misunderstanding where someone might claim that constant coexistence implies entailment, but the author clarifies that entailment requires a specific connection, which is absent here; (4) notes that the objection has been refuted; (5) discusses that these are 'directed propositions' (al-qadāyā al-muwajjaha) which are not qualified with necessity or perpetuity, and the original matn omitted them because they are rarely used in productive reasoning. The resolution ('by this is resolved') refers to using the same distinction about the judge's knowledge to resolve other problems.

Key Terms
اتفاقية (ittifāqiyya): A conditional proposition where the connection between antecedent and consequent is merely coincidental, not based on any necessary implication. The author stresses that the connection is judged solely by the agreement of the two states in reality, without implication.
اقتضاء (iqtidā'): Implication or necessary connection between antecedent and consequent. The author specifies that 'lack of iqtidā'' means the judge does not know of any implication, not that there is no implication in reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) That the judge does not know of any necessary connection between the antecedent and consequent.
C) That the antecedent and consequent are always false.
D) That the connection is impossible to determine.

Key Takeaway
In accidental conditionals, 'lack of implication' is an epistemological claim about the judge's knowledge, not an ontological one about reality.

Defining Perpetual and Necessary Propositions
Author illustrates perpetual and necessary propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the perpetual (dā'ima) and necessary (ḍarūriyya) propositions and identify their examples from the text.

Core Idea
Perpetual propositions assert truth as long as the subject exists; necessary propositions add explicit necessity.

In this passage, the author introduces two types of modal propositions: perpetual (dā'ima) and necessary (ḍarūriyya). By examining the examples given, you will learn the structural difference between them and see how they are used in logical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Look at the Arabic: دائمة (perpetual) and ضرورية (necessary). The author gives examples of each, both affirmative and negative.
Distinguish between the perpetual and necessary based on the presence of the word 'بالضرورة' (necessarily) in the necessary examples.
2
Examine the affirmative perpetual example
The example is 'دائماً كل حيوان إنسان' (Always, every animal is a human). Note the quantifier 'دائماً' (always) and the judgment that animality persists as long as the subject exists.
This example is false in reality but illustrates the form.
3
Examine the negative perpetual example
The example is 'دائماً لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر' (Always, no human is a stone). Here the negation is perpetual as long as the human exists.
Contrast with the necessary negative example: 'لا شيء من الحيوان بحجر بالضرورة' (No animal is necessarily a stone).
4
Compare with necessary examples
The necessary affirmative is 'كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة' (Every human is necessarily an animal). The word 'بالضرورة' marks necessity. The author notes that the necessity is known and observed by the judge.
In the perpetual, the necessity (if it exists) is not observed by the judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال الدائمة الموجبة دائماً كل حيوان إنسان حيث حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت الحيوانية ما دامت ذاتها موجودة. ومثال الدائمة السالبة دائماً لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، فإنا قد حكمنا فيها بدوام سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان ما دامت ذاته موجودة. مثال الضرورة الموجبة كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، فإنا قد حكمنا بضرورة ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان في جميع أوقات وجوده. ومثال الضرورة السالبة: لا شيء من الحيوان بحجر بالضرورة، فإنا قد حكمنا بضرورة سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان في أوقات وجوده وبعد أن عرفناهما يتبين أنهما متساويان وليست الدائمة أعم من الضرورية. جميع ومن يرى أن الدائمة أعم من الضرورية. تؤول له ذلك بمثل ما أوّلنا في القضية أن الحكم عليها بالتلازم بمو حكم الحاكم عليها لا عدمه بنفس الأمر. وهنا أن الضرورية والدائمة يحكم بهما في نفس الأمر لكنها في الدائمة ليست بمعلومة. وعلى تقدیر معلوميتها فليست ملاحظة من قبل الحاكم فلا يحكم فيها الحاكم بالضرورة. وفي الضرورية معلومة للحاكم بأن يحكم بضرورة إسناد المحمول إلى الموضوع ضرورة ومنظورة لديه. وجه كون الدائمة أعم عند البعض أنها لا يلاحظ الحاكم فيها بالضرورة، ولو كانت في الواقع فيها ضرورة. وفي الضرورة يلاحظ الضرورة إذن الدائمة أعم؛ لأنها تطلق على أفراد الضرورة الملاحظة للحاكم وعلى القضية التي لا يلاحظ فيها الحاكم بالضرورة. فعمومها وعدم مساواتها للضرورة بحسب نظرة الحاكم لا في الواقع ونفس الأمر.
Example of the affirmative perpetual: always, every animal is a human being, where we judge the perpetuity of the affirmation of animality as long as its essence exists. And example of the negative perpetual: always, no human being is a stone, for we have judged in it the perpetuity of the negation of stone from the human being as long as its essence exists. Example of the affirmative necessary: every human being is necessarily an animal, for we have judged the necessity of the affirmation of animality to the human being in all times of its existence. And example of the negative necessary: no animal is necessarily a stone, for we have judged the necessity of the negation of stone from the animal in all times of its existence. And after we have known both, it becomes clear that they are equal and that the perpetual is not more general than the necessary. All those who see that the perpetual is more general than the necessary, that is interpreted for them by what we interpreted regarding the proposition: the judgment of entailment is by the judgment of the judge, not its non-existence in reality. And here, the necessary and the perpetual are judged in reality, but in the perpetual it is not known. And assuming it is known, it is not observed by the judge, so the judge does not judge it as necessity. And in the necessary, it is known to the judge such that he judges the necessity of attributing the predicate to the subject, necessarily and observed by him. The reason why the perpetual is more general according to some is that the judge does not observe necessity in it, even if in reality there is necessity. And in the necessary, he observes necessity. Hence, the perpetual is more general; because it applies to the individuals of necessity observed by the judge and to the proposition in which the judge does not observe necessity. So its generality and its non-equality to necessity is according to the perspective of the judge, not in reality and in actual fact.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage addresses the relationship between two types of modal propositions: the 'dā'ima' (perpetual) and the 'ḍarūriyya' (necessary). The author provides examples: an affirmative perpetual proposition is 'Always, every animal is a human being' (which is false, but used as an example of the form), and a negative perpetual is 'Always, no human being is a stone'. For necessary propositions, an affirmative example is 'Every human being is necessarily an animal', and a negative example is 'No animal is necessarily a stone'. After presenting these examples, the author declares that the perpetual and necessary are equal in extension—they are not such that perpetual is more general than necessary. The author then explains the reasoning of those who claim perpetual is more general: they rely on a distinction based on the judge's awareness. The same principle used earlier (for conditionals) applies here: the judgment of necessity (or entailment) is based on the judge's ruling, not on the actual state of affairs. In reality, both perpetual and necessary propositions may involve necessity, but in perpetual propositions, the necessity is not known to the judge (or not observed), whereas in necessary propositions, the judge explicitly observes and judges the necessity. Therefore, from the judge's perspective, perpetual propositions appear to include cases where necessity is not observed, making perpetual seem more general. However, in reality (fī nafs al-amr), they are equivalent—the appearance of generality arises only from the subjective perspective of the judge. The author thus resolves the apparent conflict by distinguishing between reality and the judge's cognition.

Key Terms
دائمة
ضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a negative necessary proposition given in the passage?
A) دائماً كل حيوان إنسان
B) دائماً لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر
C) لا شيء من الحيوان بحجر بالضرورة
D) كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة

Key Takeaway
Perpetual and necessary propositions differ in form but are coextensive in reality; the examples help internalize their structure.

The Equality of Perpetual and Necessary in Reality
Author argues for the coextensiveness of perpetual and necessary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why perpetual and necessary propositions are coextensive in reality despite the appearance that perpetual is more general.

Core Idea
The apparent generality of perpetual over necessary arises only from the judge's perspective, not from actual reality.

After presenting examples, the author makes a bold claim: perpetual and necessary propositions are equal in extension. This lesson dissects the argument, focusing on the distinction between reality (nafs al-amr) and the judge's awareness. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying modal propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
The author states: 'يتبين أنهما متساويان وليست الدائمة أعم من الضرورية' (it becomes clear that they are equal and the perpetual is not more general than the necessary).
This is the conclusion of the argument.
2
Understand the opposing view
Some scholars hold that perpetual is more general. The author explains their reasoning: they focus on the judge's perspective. In perpetual propositions, the judge does not observe necessity, even if it exists in reality.
The opposing view stems from a subjective criterion.
3
Analyze the author's resolution
The author argues that the judgment of necessity (or entailment) is by the judge's ruling, not by reality. In reality (fī nafs al-amr), both types involve necessity. But in perpetual, the necessity is not known or observed by the judge; in necessary, it is.
Thus, the apparent generality of perpetual is only from the judge's perspective.
4
Conclude the equality in reality
The author concludes: 'فعمومها وعدم مساواتها للضرورة بحسب نظرة الحاكم لا في الواقع ونفس الأمر' (So its generality and non-equality to necessity is according to the perspective of the judge, not in reality and actual fact).
In reality, they are equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال الدائمة الموجبة دائماً كل حيوان إنسان حيث حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت الحيوانية ما دامت ذاتها موجودة. ومثال الدائمة السالبة دائماً لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، فإنا قد حكمنا فيها بدوام سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان ما دامت ذاته موجودة. مثال الضرورة الموجبة كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، فإنا قد حكمنا بضرورة ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان في جميع أوقات وجوده. ومثال الضرورة السالبة: لا شيء من الحيوان بحجر بالضرورة، فإنا قد حكمنا بضرورة سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان في أوقات وجوده وبعد أن عرفناهما يتبين أنهما متساويان وليست الدائمة أعم من الضرورية. جميع ومن يرى أن الدائمة أعم من الضرورية. تؤول له ذلك بمثل ما أوّلنا في القضية أن الحكم عليها بالتلازم بمو حكم الحاكم عليها لا عدمه بنفس الأمر. وهنا أن الضرورية والدائمة يحكم بهما في نفس الأمر لكنها في الدائمة ليست بمعلومة. وعلى تقدیر معلوميتها فليست ملاحظة من قبل الحاكم فلا يحكم فيها الحاكم بالضرورة. وفي الضرورية معلومة للحاكم بأن يحكم بضرورة إسناد المحمول إلى الموضوع ضرورة ومنظورة لديه. وجه كون الدائمة أعم عند البعض أنها لا يلاحظ الحاكم فيها بالضرورة، ولو كانت في الواقع فيها ضرورة. وفي الضرورة يلاحظ الضرورة إذن الدائمة أعم؛ لأنها تطلق على أفراد الضرورة الملاحظة للحاكم وعلى القضية التي لا يلاحظ فيها الحاكم بالضرورة. فعمومها وعدم مساواتها للضرورة بحسب نظرة الحاكم لا في الواقع ونفس الأمر.
Example of the affirmative perpetual: always, every animal is a human being, where we judge the perpetuity of the affirmation of animality as long as its essence exists. And example of the negative perpetual: always, no human being is a stone, for we have judged in it the perpetuity of the negation of stone from the human being as long as its essence exists. Example of the affirmative necessary: every human being is necessarily an animal, for we have judged the necessity of the affirmation of animality to the human being in all times of its existence. And example of the negative necessary: no animal is necessarily a stone, for we have judged the necessity of the negation of stone from the animal in all times of its existence. And after we have known both, it becomes clear that they are equal and that the perpetual is not more general than the necessary. All those who see that the perpetual is more general than the necessary, that is interpreted for them by what we interpreted regarding the proposition: the judgment of entailment is by the judgment of the judge, not its non-existence in reality. And here, the necessary and the perpetual are judged in reality, but in the perpetual it is not known. And assuming it is known, it is not observed by the judge, so the judge does not judge it as necessity. And in the necessary, it is known to the judge such that he judges the necessity of attributing the predicate to the subject, necessarily and observed by him. The reason why the perpetual is more general according to some is that the judge does not observe necessity in it, even if in reality there is necessity. And in the necessary, he observes necessity. Hence, the perpetual is more general; because it applies to the individuals of necessity observed by the judge and to the proposition in which the judge does not observe necessity. So its generality and its non-equality to necessity is according to the perspective of the judge, not in reality and in actual fact.

Commentary
This passage addresses the relationship between two types of modal propositions: the 'dā'ima' (perpetual) and the 'ḍarūriyya' (necessary). The author provides examples: an affirmative perpetual proposition is 'Always, every animal is a human being' (which is false, but used as an example of the form), and a negative perpetual is 'Always, no human being is a stone'. For necessary propositions, an affirmative example is 'Every human being is necessarily an animal', and a negative example is 'No animal is necessarily a stone'. After presenting these examples, the author declares that the perpetual and necessary are equal in extension—they are not such that perpetual is more general than necessary. The author then explains the reasoning of those who claim perpetual is more general: they rely on a distinction based on the judge's awareness. The same principle used earlier (for conditionals) applies here: the judgment of necessity (or entailment) is based on the judge's ruling, not on the actual state of affairs. In reality, both perpetual and necessary propositions may involve necessity, but in perpetual propositions, the necessity is not known to the judge (or not observed), whereas in necessary propositions, the judge explicitly observes and judges the necessity. Therefore, from the judge's perspective, perpetual propositions appear to include cases where necessity is not observed, making perpetual seem more general. However, in reality (fī nafs al-amr), they are equivalent—the appearance of generality arises only from the subjective perspective of the judge. The author thus resolves the apparent conflict by distinguishing between reality and the judge's cognition.

Key Takeaway
In reality, perpetual and necessary propositions are equal; their apparent difference is due to the judge's awareness, not the nature of the proposition itself.
