Perpetual vs Necessary Propositions: Definitions and Distinction
Definition and comparison of perpetual and necessary propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between perpetual (dā'ima) and necessary (ḍarūriyya) propositions and explain how the judge's perspective affects their classification.

Core Idea
Perpetuity and necessity are equivalent in reality but differ in the judge's awareness.

In modal logic, propositions are classified by the mode of predication. Two key modes are perpetuity (al-dā'ima) and necessity (al-ḍarūriyya). At first glance, they seem similar, but this passage reveals a subtle distinction rooted in the knowledge of the judge (al-ḥākim). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where modal claims are frequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Find the definitions and Arabic names for the two types: perpetual (al-dā'ima) and necessary (al-ḍarūriyya). Note that both are modal propositions that speak about the duration of predication.
Perpetual focuses on duration; necessary adds the element of necessity known to the judge.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the four examples: 'Every animal is human' always (perpetual affirmative), 'Nothing human is a stone' always (perpetual negative), 'Every human is an animal' of necessity (necessary affirmative), 'Nothing living is a stone' of necessity (necessary negative). Understand how each example illustrates the mode.
3
Distinguish the epistemic condition
Notice that the passage introduces the judge (al-ḥākim) who may or may not know the necessity. In a perpetual proposition, the judge merely asserts the duration; in a necessary proposition, he explicitly judges the necessity. This difference in awareness is key.
4
Analyze the relationship
The author states that in reality they are equivalent (متساوان). However, some deem perpetual more general if considering the judge's perspective. Understand why: perpetual includes cases where necessity is present but not observed by the judge, whereas necessary always involves observed necessity.
This is a nuanced point: the truth conditions are the same, but the mode of judgment differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال الدائمة الموجبة دائماً كل حيوان إنسان حيث حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت الحيوانية ما دامت ذاته موجودة. ومثال الدائمة السالبة دائماً لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، فإنا قد حكمنا فيها بدوام سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان ما دامت ذاته موجودة. مثال الضرورة الموجبة كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، فإنا قد حكمنا بضرورة ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان في جميع أوقات وجوده. ومثال الضرورة السالبة: لا شيء من الحيوان بحجر بالضرورة، فإنا قد حكمنا بضرورة سلب الحجرية عن الإنسان في أوقات وجوده وبعد أن عرفناهما يتبين أنهما متساويان وليست الدائمة أعم من الضرورية. ومن يرى أن الدائمة أعم من الضرورية، تؤول له ذلك بمثل ما أوّلنا في القضية أن الحكم عليها بالتلازم بمو حكم الحاكم عليها لا عدمه بنفس الأمر. وهنا أن الضرورية والدائمة يحكم بهما في نفس الأمر لكنها في الدائمة ليست بمعلومة. وعلى تقدير معلوميتها فليست ملاحظة من قبل الحاكم فلا يحكم فيها الحاكم بالضرورة. وفي الضرورية معلومة للحاكم بأن يحكم بضرورة إسناد المحمول إلى الموضوع ضرورة ومنظورة لديه. وجه كون الدائمة أعم عند البعض أنها لا يلاحظ الحاكم فيها بالضرورة، ولو كانت في الواقع فيها ضرورة. وفي الضرورة يلاحظ الضرورة إذن الدائمة أعم؛ لأنها تطلق على أفراد الضرورة الملاحظة للحاكم وعلى القضية التي لا يلاحظ فيها الحاكم بالضرورة. فعمومها وعدم مساواتها للضرورة بحسب نظرة الحاكم لا في الواقع ونفس الأمر.
Example of the perpetual affirmative: 'Every animal is human' always, by which we judge the permanence of animality as long as its essence exists. Example of the perpetual negative: 'Nothing human is a stone' always, for we have judged therein the permanence of the negation of stoneness from the human as long as its essence exists. Example of the necessary affirmative: 'Every human is an animal' of necessity, for we have judged the necessity of the affirmation of animality for the human in all times of its existence. Example of the necessary negative: 'Nothing living is a stone' of necessity, for we have judged the necessity of negating stoneness from the human in the times of its existence. And after having known both, it becomes clear that they are equivalent and the perpetual is not more general than the necessary. And whoever sees that the perpetual is more general than the necessary is led to that by analogy with what we said about the proposition: that the judgment upon it of entailment is according to the judgment of the judge on it, not its absence in the thing itself. But here, the necessary and the perpetual are judged in the thing itself, but in the perpetual it is not known. And assuming its knowledge, it is not observed by the judge, so the judge does not judge it as necessary. In the necessary, it is known to the judge because he judges the necessity of attributing the predicate to the subject of necessity and it is observed by him. The reason why the perpetual is more general according to some is that the judge does not observe necessity therein, even if in reality it contains necessity. And in the necessary, he observes necessity. Therefore the perpetual is more general, because it applies to individuals of the necessary observed by the judge and to the proposition in which the judge does not observe necessity. So its generality and lack of equivalence to the necessary is according to the judge's perspective, not in reality and the thing itself.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes two types of modal propositions: the perpetual (dā'ima) and the necessary (ḍarūriyya). The author begins by providing examples. For the perpetual affirmative: 'Every animal is human' always, meaning the predicate (animality) is affirmed as long as the subject's essence exists. For the perpetual negative: 'Nothing human is a stone' always, i.e., the negation of stoneness persists as long as the human exists. For the necessary affirmative: 'Every human is an animal' of necessity, meaning the predicate is necessarily affirmed in all times of the subject's existence. For the necessary negative: 'Nothing living is a stone' of necessity, i.e., the negation of stoneness from the human is necessary. After presenting these examples, the author states that it becomes clear that perpetual and necessary propositions are equivalent (متساوان) and that the perpetual is not more general than the necessary. However, some hold that the perpetual is more general. The author explains this difference: both types are true in reality (في نفس الأمر), but in the perpetual proposition the necessity is not known to the judge (الحاكم), whereas in the necessary proposition it is known and observed. Therefore, if one considers the judge's perspective, the perpetual can be seen as more general because it includes cases where necessity is not observed, even if it exists in reality. The author emphasizes that this generality is only relative to the judge's awareness, not in the actual state of affairs. This distinction touches on epistemology: the mode of judgment (perpetuity vs. necessity) depends on the knowledge of the judge. An example from kalam: the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' could be considered necessary if one knows the proof, or perpetual if one merely asserts it without proof. The passage clarifies that in logic, the truth of the proposition is not affected by the judge's knowledge, but the classification as perpetual or necessary depends on the mode of judgment.

الضرورية
الحاكم

Key Takeaway
Perpetual and necessary propositions are equivalent in objective truth, but differ in the mode of judgment: the perpetual does not require the judge to recognize necessity, while the necessary does.

The Three Types of Disjunctive Proposition
Author defines three types of disjunctive proposition: exclusive, exhaustive, and both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any disjunctive proposition as genuine, exclusive-only, or exhaustive-only by analyzing the opposition in truth and falsehood between its disjuncts.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions differ based on whether disjuncts can both be true, both false, or neither.

Not all 'either-or' statements work the same way. In Islamic logic, the type of disjunction depends on the relationship of opposition between the two parts. The author of al-Fawa'id al-Fanariyya distinguishes three kinds: genuine, exclusive-only, and exhaustive-only. Understanding these differences is crucial for correctly evaluating arguments that use disjunctive premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjuncts
Locate the two parts connected by 'أو' (or). They are the two possibilities being considered. For example, 'This thing is either a stone or a tree' has disjuncts 'stone' and 'tree'.
2
Check if both can be true simultaneously
Ask: Can the two disjuncts be true at the same time? If no, then the proposition is exclusive in truth (mani'at al-jam'). If yes, it is not exclusive.
In the example 'stone or tree', something cannot be both a stone and a tree, so it is exclusive in truth.
3
Check if both can be false simultaneously
Ask: Can both disjuncts be false at the same time? If no, then the proposition is exhaustive in falsehood (mani'at al-khuluw). If yes, it is not exhaustive.
In 'stone or tree', a human is neither, so both can be false, so it is not exhaustive.
4
Combine the two checks to determine the type
If exclusive in truth and exhaustive in falsehood → genuine (haqiqiyya). If exclusive in truth but not exhaustive → exclusive-only. If not exclusive but exhaustive → exhaustive-only. If neither exclusive nor exhaustive, it is not a proper disjunction.
The genuine type has opposition in both truth and falsehood; the others have opposition in only one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفوائد الفنارية
[أقسام القضية المنفصلة]
(والمُنْفَصِلة) ثلاثة أقسام:
حقيقية، ومانعة الجمعِ فقط، ومانعة الخلو فقط :
(١) لأَنَّ العِنادَ) (إِمَّا) في الصِدْقِ والكَذِبِ مَعَا تُسمى (حقيقيَّةٌ) (وهِيَ مانعةُ الجمْع والخلو معا) وهي موجبتُها .
وسالبتها برفع العنادِ في الصدقِ والكَذِب. كقولنا: ليسَ البتةَ أَنْ يَكُونَ هذا الإنسانُ كاتباً أو تركيَّا، فإنّهُما يصدِقانِ ويَكْذِبان معا.
٢ . (وإما) في الصدق فقط ) وتُسمى (مانعةَ الجَمْعِ فقط، كقولنا: هذا الشيء إِمَّا حَجَرٌ أَوْ شَجَرٌ) فإنّهما لا يصدقان)، وقد يكذِبان بأَنْ يكون إنسانا (٣).
وسالبتُها برفع العنادِ في الصدقِ فقط، نحو: ليسَ البَتَّةَ إِمَّا أَنْ يكون هذا الشيء لا شجراً ولا حجراً)، فإنهما يصدقان ولا يكذبانِ، وإلا لكانَ حَجَراً وشجَراً معا).
٣. (وإمَّا) في الكذِبِ فقط، وتُسمى (مانعة الخلو فقط، كقولنا : زيد إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ في البَحرِ وإِمَّا أَنْ لا يَعْرقَ)، فإِنَّ الكونَ في البَحرِ مع (١) أي: في الإثبات فقط.
(٢) فلا يقال عن الشيء: شجر وحجر.
(٣) أي: في النفي يجوز نفي المقدم والتالي، فنقول: هذا لا شجر ولا حجرٌ كأن يكون إنسانا.
(٤) أي: لا عناد بين المقدم والتالي حيث رفعته كلمة ليس البتة.
(٥) أي: يجوز أن يقال: هذا لا حجرٌ ولا شجر ، كأن يكون إنساناً.
(٦) أي: لا يرتفعان معا ؛ فإذا أدخلنا ليس عليهما فإن لا شجر يصير ليس لا شجر -أي: شجر ، ولا حجر يصير ليس لا حجر أي حجر، لأن نفي النفي إثبات.
أي: ترفع عدم العناد أي: حصول العناد.
Al-Fawa'id al-Fanariyya
[Sections of the Disjunctive Proposition]
The disjunctive (proposition) has three divisions:
Genuine (haqiqiyya), exclusive only (mani'at al-jam' faqat), and exhaustive only (mani'at al-khuluw faqat).
(1) Because opposition (is) either in truth and falsehood together, it is called 'genuine' (and it is exclusive and exhaustive together), and its affirmative is that. Its negative is by removing opposition in truth and falsehood, like our saying: 'It is absolutely not that this human is a writer or a Turk', for they (both) are true and false together.
2. And (opposition is) either in truth only, and it is called 'exclusive only', like our saying: 'This thing is either a stone or a tree', for they are not both true (together), and they may be both false by it being a human. Its negative is by removing opposition in truth only, like: 'It is absolutely not that this thing is neither a tree nor a stone', for they are both true and not both false, otherwise it would be a stone and a tree together.
3. And (opposition is) either in falsehood only, and it is called 'exhaustive only', like our saying: 'Zayd is either in the sea or does not sweat', for being in the sea together with (the rest of the commentary as in footnotes).
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage classifies the disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila) into three types based on the location of opposition (al-'inad) between the two disjuncts in terms of truth and falsehood. The author begins by stating that the disjunctive has three divisions: genuine (haqiqiyya), exclusive only (mani'at al-jam' faqat), and exhaustive only (mani'at al-khuluw faqat). 

First, the genuine disjunctive (haqiqiyya) is where opposition holds in both truth and falsehood; that is, the two disjuncts can neither be both true nor both false simultaneously. This is called 'exclusive and exhaustive together' (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw ma'an). Its affirmative form is exemplified by 'The number is either even or odd' because a number cannot be both even and odd, nor can it be neither. Its negative removes opposition in both truth and falsehood, as in 'It is absolutely not that this human is a writer or a Turk'—meaning it is possible for the person to be both a writer and a Turk, or neither.

Second, the exclusive only (mani'at al-jam' faqat) involves opposition in truth only: the disjuncts cannot be both true, but they can be both false. The example is 'This thing is either a stone or a tree'—it cannot be both a stone and a tree, but it can be neither (e.g., it could be a human). Its negative removes the opposition in truth only, allowing both to be true, as in saying 'It is absolutely not that this thing is neither a tree nor a stone'—meaning it could be both a tree and a stone, but that is impossible, so the example shows the logical form even if the content is unrealistic.

Third, the exhaustive only (mani'at al-khuluw faqat) involves opposition in falsehood only: the disjuncts cannot be both false, but they can be both true. The example is 'Zayd is either in the sea or does not sweat'—these could both be true if Zayd is in the sea and not sweating; but they cannot both be false (Zayd cannot be not in the sea and still sweat? Actually the example is imperfect, but the meaning is that at least one must be true). The footnotes clarify that the negative of this type removes the opposition in falsehood, allowing both to be false. The author uses marginal notes to explain technical terms and the effect of negation. This classification is fundamental in logic for understanding the behavior of disjunctive syllogisms and their contradictions.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع فقط
مانعة الخلو فقط
العناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Genuine (haqiqiyya)

C) Exhaustive only (mani'at al-khuluw faqat)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The type of disjunctive proposition is determined by whether opposition holds in truth, falsehood, or both, and each type has distinct logical properties for inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following disjunctive propositions from fiqh as genuine, exclusive-only, or exhaustive-only: (a) 'This act is either wajib or haram' (assuming it cannot be both and must be one). (b) 'This food is either halal or haram' (it cannot be both, but could be makruh?). (c) 'The prayer is either valid or invalid' (cannot be both, cannot be neither).

Types of Exclusive Disjunction: Man'at al-Jam' and Man'at al-Khuluw
Author defines two types of exclusive disjunction and their negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw based on their truth conditions and correctly identify their negations.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction forbids either joint truth or joint falsehood.

In Islamic logic, exclusive disjunctions are not all the same. Some prevent both parts from being true together, while others prevent both from being false together. This lesson unpacks these two types and shows how their negations flip the exclusivity condition. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating disjunctive syllogisms in legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the definitions: man'at al-jam' prevents conjunction (both true), man'at al-khuluw prevents emptiness (both false). Note the key difference.
2
Analyze the truth conditions
For man'at al-jam', the two parts cannot both be true, but they may both be false. For man'at al-khuluw, they cannot both be false, but they may both be true.
Memorize: jam' = together (true), khuluw = empty (false).
3
Examine the examples
The first example: 'This thing is either a stone or a tree' – it cannot be both stone and tree, but it could be a human (neither). The second: 'Zayd is either in the sea or he does not sweat' – both can be true (in sea and not sweating), but they cannot both be false (if not in sea, he sweats).
Why would he drown on land if both false? Because if not in sea and he sweats, then the second part is false, so both false implies he is not in sea and he sweats, which is contradictory to 'not sweating' being true? Actually the logic: if both false, then 'Zayd is in the sea' is false (so not in sea) and 'he does not sweat' is false (so he sweats). That is possible, but the author says 'otherwise he would drown on land' as a absurdity? The example assumes that if he is not in the sea and sweats, he would drown on land? That seems odd, but it's the given example. Focus on the logical form.
4
Understand the negation
The negation using 'laysa al-batta' removes the opposition. For man'at al-jam', negation removes the prohibition on both being true, so they become both true in some cases. For man'at al-khuluw, negation removes the prohibition on both being false, so they become both false in some cases.
The negated propositions are also disjunctive in form but now allow what was previously forbidden.
5
Apply to new cases
Test your understanding: given a disjunction like 'Either Zayd is a scholar or he is a writer', determine which type it is. Can he be both? Can he be neither? If it is man'at al-jam', then he cannot be both; if man'at al-khuluw, then he cannot be neither. Also practice negating it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وإما) في الصدق فقط ) وتسمى (مانعةَ الجمعِ فقط، كقولنا: هذا الشيء إما حجر أو شجر) فإنهما لا يصدقان)، وقد يكذبان بأن يكون إنسانا. وسالبتها برفع العنادِ في الصدقِ فقط، نحو: ليسَ البتة إما أن يكون هذا الشيء لا شجراً ولا حجراً)، فإنهما يصدقان ولا يكذبانِ، وإلا لكانَ حجراً وشجراً معا). (وإما) في الكذبِ فقط، وتسمى (مانعة الخلو فقط، كقولنا : زيد إما أن يكونَ في البحرِ وإما أن لا يعرقَ)، فإنَّ الكونَ في البحرِ مع عدمِ الغرقِ يصدقانِ ولا يكذبانِ وإلا لغرِقَ في البر. وسالبتها برفع العنادِ في الكذب فقط نحو: ليسَ البتة زيدٌ إما أن لا يكونَ في البحرِ، وإما أن يغرَقَ.
And as for that which is in truth only, it is called 'the preventer of conjunction only', like our saying: 'This thing is either a stone or a tree'; for they are not both true, and they may both be false if it is a human. And its negative is by removing the opposition in truth only, like: 'Not at all is this thing either not a stone nor a tree'; for they are both true and not both false, otherwise it would be both stone and tree together. And as for that which is in falsehood only, it is called 'the preventer of emptiness only', like our saying: 'Zayd is either in the sea or he does not sweat'; for being in the sea along with the absence of sweating are both true and not both false, otherwise he would drown on land. And its negative is by removing the opposition in falsehood only, like: 'Not at all is Zayd either not in the sea or he drowns.'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines two types of exclusive disjunctive propositions. The first is 'man'at al-jam' only', where the two parts cannot both be true (they are exclusive in truth), but they can both be false. The example given: 'This thing is either a stone or a tree' – it cannot be both stone and tree, but it could be neither (e.g., a human). Its negation, indicated by 'not at all' (laysa al-batta), removes the opposition in truth, meaning it transforms the proposition into one that allows both parts to be true together. The example shows that after negation, the two parts become both true (the thing is neither stone nor tree, but by double negation it becomes both? Actually careful: the negation of 'not stone nor tree' yields a proposition that is both true when the thing is a human, not both false). The second type is 'man'at al-khuluw only', where the two parts cannot both be false (they are exclusive in falsehood), but they can both be true. The example: 'Zayd is either in the sea or he is not sweating' – it is possible that he is in the sea and not sweating (both true), but they cannot both be false (if he is not in the sea, he must sweat; otherwise he would drown on land). Its negation removes the opposition in falsehood, converting it into a proposition that permits both parts to be false. The author emphasizes the logical behavior of these propositions and their negations, which are foundational for understanding conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
رفع العناد

C) It becomes a proposition that allows both parts to be true.
D) It becomes a proposition that prevents both parts from being false.

Key Takeaway
Exclusive disjunction in Islamic logic is not monolithic; man'at al-jam' excludes joint truth, while man'at al-khuluw excludes joint falsehood, and their negations reverse the exclusivity condition.

Conversion Rules Between Exclusive and Inclusive Disjunctions
Author derives truth-value relations between exclusive and inclusive disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth-value relation between an exclusive disjunction and an inclusive disjunction, and apply the conversion rule between them.

Core Idea
Exclusive and inclusive disjunctions are logical mirrors: one's truth implies the other's falsehood.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive propositions come in two types: man'a al-jam' (exclusive) and man'a al-khuluw (inclusive). This passage reveals a powerful conversion rule: when an exclusive disjunction is true between two things, an inclusive disjunction is true between their contradictories. Understanding this rule is essential for transforming legal arguments in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definitions
Define man'a al-jam' (exclusive: at most one true) and man'a al-khuluw (inclusive: at least one true).
2
State the truth conditions for affirmatives and negatives
If the affirmative exclusive is true, its negative is false, and the negative inclusive is true in that matter. Similarly for the inclusive affirmative.
3
Derive the conversion rule
If exclusive holds between two things, then inclusive holds between their contradictories, and vice versa.
This is a form of immediate inference.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
The ruling for an act is either wajib or haram exclusively. Therefore, between not-wajib and not-haram, inclusive disjunction holds (i.e., at least one is true).
This transformation is used in arguments about abrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عدم الكونِ في البحرِ مع الغرقِ يكذبانِ ولا يصدقان. ومِنهُ يُعْلَمُ أنَّ كلَّ مادّةٍ صدَقَ فيها موجِبَةُ مَنْعِ الجَمعِ كَذَبَ فيها سالبته، وصدَقَ فيها سالبة منع الخلوّ . وكُلَّ مادَّةٍ صدَقَ فيها مَوجَبَةٌ منعِ الخُلُوِّ كَذبَ فيها سالبتُهُ، وصدق فيها سالبَةُ منْعِ الجَمْعِ، وكذا في جانبِ سالبتهما، وأنَّ كلَّ شيْتَينِ صَدقَ بَيْنَ عَيْنَيْهما منعُ الجَمْعِ صَدَقَ بينَ نقيضهما منعُ الخُلوِّ وبالعكس).
For the non-existence of being in the sea with drowning together are false and not true. And from it is known that for every matter in which the affirmative of exclusive disjunction is true, its negative is false, and the negative of inclusive disjunction is true in it. And for every matter in which the affirmative of inclusive disjunction is true, its negative is false, and the negative of exclusive disjunction is true in it. And likewise on the side of their negatives. And that for every two things between which exclusive disjunction is true, between their contradictories inclusive disjunction is true, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage explains the truth conditions and relations between two types of disjunctive propositions: exclusive disjunction (man'a al-jam') and inclusive disjunction (man'a al-khuluw). The author starts with an example: 'the non-existence of being in the sea with drowning' — these two cannot be separated in the sense that drowning necessarily means being in the sea? Actually the example illustrates that certain pairs cannot both be false. Then he derives rules: For any domain, if the affirmative exclusive disjunction is true, its negative is false, and the inclusive disjunction's negative is true. Similarly for the affirmative inclusive. Then the key result: If between two things an exclusive disjunction holds, then between their contradictories an inclusive disjunction holds, and vice versa. This is a conversion rule useful for logical transformations. The author likely extracted this from the matn's discussion of disjunctives. Examples from fiqh: The ruling for an act is either wajib or haram (exclusive), and its contradictory (not wajib or not haram) would be inclusive (i.e., it could be both not wajib and not haram? Actually careful: If it is either wajib or haram exclusively, then the contradictory (not wajib or not haram) is inclusive. This is the logical principle behind certain transformations in juridical arguments.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع (Māni'at al-jam'): Exclusive disjunction; the proposition that asserts that at most one of the two disjuncts is true (they cannot both be true). [Note: In earlier logic, this was often called 'exclusive' but not always distinguished from inclusive.]
مانعة الخلو (Māni'at al-khuluw): Inclusive disjunction; the proposition that asserts that at least one of the two disjuncts is true (they cannot both be false).
سالبته (sālibatuhu): The negative of the affirmative disjunctive proposition; its contradictory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Man'a al-jam'
B) Man'a al-khuluw
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Exclusive disjunction between two propositions is logically equivalent to inclusive disjunction between their contradictories.

Multiple-Part Disjunctives: Classification and Examples
Author classifies multiple-part disjunctives with examples from numbers, words, elements, and universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify disjunctive propositions by the number of parts and identify corresponding examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Disjunctives can have three, four, or five parts, each drawn from a distinct science.

In this passage, the author expands on the notion that disjunctive propositions (al-munfasilāt) are not limited to two parts. He gives examples with three, four, and five parts, each taken from a different field: arithmetic, grammar, natural philosophy, and logic. Understanding this classification helps you see how logical structures map onto real categories in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of a multiple-part disjunctive
A disjunctive with more than two parts lists all mutually exclusive alternatives. For example, 'the number is either excessive, deficient, or equal' has three parts.
2
Count the number of parts in each example
For each example, count how many alternatives are given: three for numbers and words, four for elements, five for universals.
The number of parts is not random; it reflects the natural divisions in that science.
3
Recognize the science of each example
Each example comes from a different field: numbers (arithmetic/ʿilm al-ʿadad), words (grammar/nahw), elements (natural philosophy/ʿilm al-ṭabīʿa), universals (logic/ʿilm al-manṭiq).
4
Note the correspondence between number of parts and the category
The number of parts is determined by the subject matter. For instance, the five universals are exactly five, and the four elements are exactly four.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تكونُ المنفصلاتُ ذَواتِ أَجزاء ثلاثة وأكثرَ فَالثَلاثَةُ (كقولنا: العدد إما زائد أو ناقص أو مساو) والكلمة: إما اسم أو فعل أو حرفٌ. والأكثر كقولنا : العنصر إما نار أوهواء أو ماء أو أرضُ. والكلي: إما نوع أو جنس أو فصل أو خَاصِةٌ أَو عَرِضٌ عام. ومثال المتن : ليسَ معناهُ أَنْ يُنسَبَ عددٌ إِلى عَددٍ كَما ظُنَّ فالزيادة والنقصان والمساواة لا يُراد بها حينئذ معانيها اللغويّةُ . بل المراد بها معانيها الاصطلاحِيَّةُ . فإنَّ كلَّ عَددٍ يزيدُ المُجْتَمِعُ مِنْ كُسورِهِ التِسْعَةِ عليه يُسمى زائداً كاثني عشر ).
And the disjunctives may have three parts and more. The three-part case is like our saying: 'The number is either excessive or deficient or equal.' And the word: either noun or verb or particle. And the more-than-three case is like our saying: 'The element is either fire or air or water or earth.' And the universal: either species or genus or differentia or property or general accident. And the example from the text: It does not mean that a number is compared to a number as was thought, for 'excessive', 'deficient', and 'equal' are not intended here in their linguistic meanings. Rather, they are intended in their technical meanings. For every number whose sum of its nine fractions is greater than it is called excessive, like twelve.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage expands on the notion that a disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila) can have more than two parts. The author provides examples of tripartite disjunctives: numbers can be excessive, deficient, or equal (these are technical terms in mathematics); words are either noun, verb, or particle. For more than three, we have the four elements and the five universals (species, genus, differentia, property, general accident). The author then clarifies an important point: In the example of numbers, the terms 'excessive', 'deficient', and 'equal' are not to be understood in their everyday sense (e.g., 6 is greater than 5). Rather, they have technical definitions: an excessive number is one where the sum of its proper divisors (the 'nine fractions' meaning the proper divisors, such as halves, thirds, quarters, etc. up to ninths) is greater than the number itself. For example, 12 is excessive because the sum of its proper divisors (1,2,3,4,6) is 16 > 12. This shows the precision required in logical definitions. The author is likely commenting on a matn example, ensuring the student understands the correct interpretation. In Islamic logic, such examples ground abstract concepts in concrete, recognizable categories from the sciences (arithmetic, grammar, natural philosophy).

Technical Precision in Logical Examples: The Case of Numbers
Author clarifies technical meanings of excessive, deficient, and equal in the numbers example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between linguistic and technical meanings of terms in logical definitions, using the numbers example from the passage.

Core Idea
The numbers example is not about ordinary comparison but about technical number theory.

The author immediately follows the example 'the number is either excessive, deficient, or equal' with a crucial clarification: these terms have technical meanings in arithmetic, not their everyday senses. This lesson will help you understand the technical definitions and see why this precision matters in logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the numbers example
The passage gives 'العدد إما زائد أو ناقص أو مساو' (the number is either excessive, deficient, or equal). This seems like a simple comparison, but the author warns against that interpretation.
2
Read the author's warning
He says: 'ليس معناه أن يُنسب عدد إلى عدد كما ظن' (It does not mean that a number is compared to a number as was thought). So the terms are not relative comparisons.
3
Learn the technical definition of 'excessive'
The author says: 'فإن كل عدد يزيد المجتمع من كسوره التسعة عليه يسمى زائداً' (every number whose sum of its nine fractions – i.e., proper divisors – is greater than it is called excessive).
'Nine fractions' is a conventional expression for proper divisors in the tradition.
4
Apply the definition to an example
The author gives 12. Its proper divisors (1,2,3,4,6) sum to 16, which is greater than 12, so 12 is excessive.
5
Check the other two definitions
By analogy, a deficient number has sum of proper divisors less than itself (e.g., 8: 1+2+4=7<8), and a perfect number has sum equal (e.g., 6: 1+2+3=6).

Disjunction with Multiple Parts
Author gives examples of disjunctions with three or more parts across sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the number of disjuncts in a disjunctive proposition and verify mutual exclusivity across multiple alternatives.

Core Idea
A disjunction can have three, four, five or more mutually exclusive options.

In logic, a disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila) presents a set of alternatives. While two-part disjunctions are common, you will also encounter disjunctions with three or more parts, especially in classifications within Islamic sciences. This lesson examines how such multi-part disjunctions are structured and how to ensure their exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the number of disjuncts
Look at each example: 'the number is either abundant, deficient, or equal' has three; 'the word is either noun, verb, or particle' has three; 'the element is either fire, air, water, or earth' has four; 'the universal is either species, genus, differentia, property, or common accident' has five. Count them.
2
Check mutual exclusivity
For a true disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya), the alternatives must be mutually exclusive: one and only one can be true. For example, a number cannot be both abundant and deficient; a word cannot be both a noun and a verb. Verify that each set of alternatives is indeed exclusive.
The passage implies that the numerical example is a true disjunction.
3
Observe the scientific domain
Notice that the examples come from different sciences: arithmetic (number classification), grammar (word categories), natural philosophy (elements), and logic (universals). This shows the widespread use of disjunctions across Islamic disciplines.
4
Construct your own multi-part disjunction
Take a classification from an Islamic science, such as the five juridical categories of actions (wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, haram). Formulate it as a disjunction, ensuring the categories are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
Remember that in a true disjunction, all alternatives together cover the whole subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And disjunctions may have three or more parts. The three-part example: 'The number is either abundant, deficient, or equal.' And the word: either noun, verb, or particle. And more than three: 'The element (1) is either fire, air, water, or earth.' And the universal: either species, genus, differential, property, or common accident. And the example of the text: It does not mean that a number is compared to a number as was thought, for increase, decrease, and equality are not intended here in their linguistic meanings. Rather, their technical meanings are intended. So every number whose sum of its nine fractions (3) exceeds it is called abundant, like twelve. And deficient as four. And equal as six. This applies to the true disjunction. Four is a deficient number because it contains half (2) and quarter (1): 2+1=3, so it is a deficient number. Six is an equal number: it contains half (3), third (2), and sixth (1): 3+2+1=6.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines and explains disjunctive propositions that have more than two parts. The author begins by stating that disjunctives can have three or more parts, giving examples: the three-part disjunction 'the number is either abundant, deficient, or equal' and 'the word is either noun, verb, or particle'. For more than three parts, the author gives 'the element is either fire, air, water, or earth' (four parts) and 'the universal is either species, genus, differential, property, or common accident' (five parts). The author then clarifies the example of numbers: the terms 'abundant', 'deficient', and 'equal' are not used in their ordinary linguistic sense (where one number is compared to another), but in a technical sense specific to arithmetic. The technical sense is: a number is called 'abundant' (za'id) if the sum of its proper divisors (the 'nine fractions', referring to the divisors half, third, quarter, etc.) exceeds the number itself, like 12 (whose divisors sum to 15). A number is 'deficient' (naqis) if the sum is less than the number, like 4 (divisors 2 and 1 sum to 3). A number is 'equal' (musawi) if the sum equals the number, like 6 (divisors 3, 2, 1 sum to 6). The author stresses that this technical usage applies to the 'true disjunction' (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya), meaning a disjunction that is mutually exclusive and exhaustive. The passage exemplifies how logical definitions rely on technical terminology within specific sciences.

Technical Classification of Numbers: Za'id, Naqis, Musawi
Author clarifies that za'id, naqis, musawi are technical arithmetic terms based on divisor sums.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define za'id (abundant), naqis (deficient), and musawi (perfect) numbers using the sum of proper divisors, and compute the classification for any given number.

Core Idea
A number's type is determined by comparing itself to the sum of its proper divisors, not by comparison with another number.

The passage introduces a technical classification of numbers used in Islamic arithmetic. These terms appear in the disjunction example, but they have specific meanings that differ from their linguistic usage. Here we learn the precise definitions based on the sum of the number's proper divisors (the 'nine fractions').

Guided Path
1
Understand the divisor sum method
Identify the proper divisors of a number (excluding the number itself). In the text, these are called the 'nine fractions' — half, third, quarter, etc. For a number, list all its divisors from those fractions. For 12, proper divisors are 6,4,3,2,1 (sum=15).
The 'nine fractions' are a standard set: 1/2, 1/3, 1/4, 1/5, 1/6, 1/7, 1/8, 1/9, and 1/10? But the passage only mentions half, third, quarter. The key is to sum all proper divisors.
2
Apply the condition
Compare the sum of proper divisors to the number itself. If sum > number, it is za'id (abundant). If sum < number, it is naqis (deficient). If sum = number, it is musawi (perfect).
The text gives examples: 12 (sum 15 >12 → za'id), 4 (sum 3 <4 → naqis), 6 (sum 6 → musawi).
3
Test another number
Take 8. Proper divisors: 4,2,1 (sum=7). 7 < 8, so 8 is naqis. Try 28: divisors 14,7,4,2,1 (sum=28) → musawi.
This reinforces the method.
4
Distinguish from linguistic sense
Note that in ordinary language, 'za'id' means 'excessive' as compared to something else. Here it is not relative to another number but to its own divisors. The text warns against this confusion.

Compound Disjunctives and the Objection from a Single Relation
Author defines compound disjunctives and refutes an objection against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against compound disjunctives and explain why it fails according to the author.

Core Idea
A single disjunctive relation can only connect two parts, but compound disjunctives are valid because the relation is analyzable.

This lesson examines compound disjunctives with three or more parts, as defined in classical Islamic logic. The author first provides examples of manfi'a al-khulu and manfi'a al-jam' with three disjuncts, then presents a logical objection that claims such compounds are impossible because a single relation cannot connect more than two terms. By analyzing the objection and its refutation, you will learn how to defend complex logical structures in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two compound disjunctives
Locate the examples of manfi'a al-khulu and manfi'a al-jam' with three parts in the Arabic text. Note the difference: the first negates each alternative ('not A, or not B, or not C'), while the second affirms them ('A, or B, or C').
Manfi'a al-khulu requires at least one alternative true; manfi'a al-jam' requires at most one true.
2
Extract the objector's premise
The objector claims that disjunction (al-infiṣāl) is a single relation (nisba wāḥida). A single relation can only exist between two terms, not three or more. This is presented as a necessary truth.
The objector treats the disjunctive relation as a binary connective, like the relationship between two numbers.
3
Understand the objector's conclusion
From the premise, the objector concludes that no disjunctive can have more than two parts. Therefore, compound disjunctives with three parts are impossible.
The conclusion is implicit: no disjunctive is composed of more than two.
4
Analyze the author's refutation
The author shows that if the objector's reasoning were correct, it would lead to a contradiction: both manfi'a al-khulu and manfi'a al-jam' would be 'multiple' (i.e., each would be a set of multiple disjunctives), which is absurd. Thus the objection is refuted by reductio ad absurdum.
The footnotes indicate that the objection itself entails multiplicity, undermining its own claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا مانعةُ الخُلو المركبةُ مِنْ أكثر من اثنين فكقولنا: إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ هذا الشيء لا حجراً أو لا شجراً أو لا حيوانا). وأَمَّا مانعةُ الجَمع فكقولنا: إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ هذا الشيءَ شَجراً أو حَجراً أو حيوانا). فإِنْ قُلتَ: لا يتركَّبُ شيءٌ من المنفَصِلاتِ مِنْ أكثرَ مِنْ جزئيْنِ؛ لأن الانفصال نسبةٌ واحدةٌ. والنسبة الواحدة لا تُتصَوَّرُ إلا بين جزئين؛ ضرورةً أنَّ النسبة بين أمور متكثرة لا تكون واحدةً بل تكون متكثرة (۱). إذن تعددت مانعة الخلو. (۲) إذن تعددت مانعة الجمع. (۳) هذا إيراد على تعدد أجزاء المنفصلة مفاده أن النسبة وهي المعنوي الذي يربط بين المقدم والتالي كالربط بين العدد الزائد والناقص لا يكون متعدداً، والنسبة هي واحدة وليس كثيرة. والقول بتعدد أجزاء المنفصلة يلزم منه تعدد هذه النسبة، فاللازم غير صحيح، إذن الملزوم أيضا غير صحيح.
As for manfi'a al-khulu composed of more than two, like our saying: This thing is either not a stone, or not a tree, or not an animal. And as for manfi'a al-jam', like our saying: This thing is either a stone, or a tree, or an animal. If you say: No disjunctive is composed of more than two parts, because disjunction is a single relation. And a single relation cannot be conceived except between two parts, necessarily because the relation between multiple things is not one but multiple. (1) Then manfi'a al-khulu is multiple. (2) Then manfi'a al-jam' is multiple. (3) This is an objection against the multiplicity of parts of the disjunctive, its meaning being that the relation—the conceptual connector between the antecedent and consequent, like the connection between the excessive and deficient number—cannot be multiple; the relation is one, not many. And the claim of multiplicity of parts of the disjunctive necessitates multiplicity of this relation; the consequent is false, so the antecedent is also false.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage deals with compound disjunctives that have more than two parts. The author first defines two types: manfi'a al-khulu (exclusive disjunction in the sense of 'at least one is false') and manfi'a al-jam' (exclusive disjunction in the sense of 'at most one is true') with three alternatives. For example, 'This thing is either not a stone, or not a tree, or not an animal' is manfi'a al-khulu because the parts cannot all be false (the thing must be at least one of the three categories, so at least one 'not' is true). 'This thing is either a stone, or a tree, or an animal' is manfi'a al-jam' because it cannot be two simultaneously (mutual exclusivity). Then an objection is raised: a disjunction is a single relation, and a single relation can only exist between two terms, not three or more. Therefore, a disjunctive with three parts is impossible. The footnotes (1) and (2) seem to be responses: if the objector's argument holds, then manfi'a al-khulu and manfi'a al-jam' would each be multiple disjunctives, contradicting the claim. The main footnote (3) explains the objection in detail: the relation is like the connection between an excessive number and a deficient number—it is a single asymmetric relation. Multiplying the parts would multiply the relation, which is absurd. The author implies this objection is refuted (perhaps by noting that the relation is actually complex, or that the disjunction can be analyzed as a series of binary relations). The passage is dialectical: it presents a challenge and shows its consequence leads to a contradiction, thus rejecting it.

مانعة الجمع

The Binary Nature of Disjunction: Reducing Three-Part Disjunctions
Author explains reduction of three-part disjunctions to binary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any three-part disjunction into nested binary disjunctions and identify the genuine disjunctive relation.

Core Idea
Every multi-part disjunction is only apparently compound; at root it is always binary.

You may encounter disjunctions that seem to have three or more parts, like 'The act is either wajib, haram, or mubah.' But can a single logical relation truly hold among three terms? In this lesson, we will learn from Fanari's commentary that such disjunctions are only apparently compound; in reality, they reduce to a series of binary choices. This insight is crucial for precise logical analysis in legal debates and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent structure
Look at a three-part disjunction like 'Either the number is excessive, deficient, or equal.' It appears to have three parts linked by 'or'.
2
Recall the objection
A disjunction is a single relation between two things. A single relation cannot hold among three things simultaneously, so a three-part disjunction seems impossible.
This is the objection raised in the passage.
3
Understand the response
The three-part structure is only apparent. The real logical relation is binary: 'Either it is excessive or not excessive.' The 'not excessive' part then contains a further binary disjunction: 'Either deficient or equal.' So the original is a nested binary.
The passage calls this 'composition in appearance, not in reality.'
4
Practice the reduction
Take any three-part disjunction and break it into two steps: first choose between the first option and the rest (as a group), then within the rest, choose between the remaining two options.
This works for manfa'at al-khuluw, manfa'at al-jam', and even exclusive-or.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مانعة الخلو المركبة من أكثر من اثنين فكقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا الشيء لا حجراً أو لا شجراً أو لا حيوانا. وأما مانعة الجمع فكقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا الشيء شجراً أو حجراً أو حيوانا. فإن قلت: لا يتركب شيء من المنفصلات من أكثر من جزئين؛ لأن الانفصال نسبة واحدة. والنسبة الواحدة لا تتصور إلا بين جزئين؛ ضرورة أن النسبة بين أمور متكثرة لا تكون واحدة بل تكون متكثرة. قلت: المراد بتركب المنفصلات من أكثر من جزئين تركيبها بحسب الظاهر لا بحسب الحقيقة. وإلا فالانفصال الحقيقي في المثال المذكور على الحقيقية بين أن يكون العدد زائداً أو لا يكون. ثم على تقدير أن لا يكون زائداً بين كونه ناقصاً أو مساوياً. فإن قلت: فما وجه حكمهم أن الحقيقية لا تتركب من أكثر من جزئين، ومانعة الخلو والجمع يتركبان؟ الجواب: أن تركيبها فيما يرى أنها من أكثر من جزأين، والواقع في الحقيقية أنها مركبة من جزئين وهما: إذا قلنا العدد إما زائد أو ليس بزائد فالنسبة واحدة بين الزائد وبين الأضداد له المشتمل عليها. قوله: أو لا يكون: ولا يكون تتضمن الجزئين الآخرين وهما الناقص والمساوي. وهما قضية أخرى لها نسبة واحدة.
As for the disjunction of voidance [manfa'at al-khuluw] composed of more than two, like our saying: 'Either this thing is not stone, or not tree, or not animal.' As for the disjunction of aggregation [manfa'at al-jam'], like our saying: 'Either this thing is tree, or stone, or animal.' If you say: No disjunction is composed of more than two parts, because disjunction is a single relation. And a single relation is conceived only between two parts, necessarily because a relation among multiple things is not single but multiple. I say: What is meant by the composition of disjunctions from more than two parts is their composition in appearance, not in reality. Otherwise, the real disjunction in the mentioned example, in the real exclusive-or, is between whether the number is excessive or not. Then, on the assumption that it is not excessive, [it is] between its being deficient or equal. If you say: What is the basis of their judgment that the exclusive-or [haqiqiyya] is not composed of more than two parts, while the voiding and aggregating disjunctions are composed? The answer: That their composition is in what appears to be more than two parts, but the reality is that they are composed of two parts, namely: When we say 'The number is either excessive or not excessive,' the relation is single between the excessive and its opposites that it contains. His statement 'or not' [aw la yakūn] contains the other two parts, namely the deficient and the equal. And these are another proposition with a single relation.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage clarifies a key logical point about disjunctive propositions. The author begins by defining two types of compound disjunctions with three parts: manfa'at al-khuluw (where the parts cannot all be absent) and manfa'at al-jam' (where the parts cannot all be present). For example, 'Either this is not stone, or not tree, or not animal' is a voiding disjunction with three apparent parts. An objection is then raised: since disjunction is a single relation, it can only hold between two terms; a three-part disjunction would require multiple relations, which is impossible. The answer is that the three-part structure is only apparent. In reality, a three-part disjunction reduces to a binary one. For instance, 'The number is either excessive, deficient, or equal' is really 'Either it is excessive or not excessive,' and then within 'not excessive' there is a further disjunction between deficient and equal. Thus, the true disjunctive relation is always binary. A further objection asks why logicians say the exclusive-or (haqiqiyya) is never composed of more than two parts, while voiding and aggregating disjunctions appear to have more. The answer: even those are binary in reality; their appearance of multiplicity is due to nested disjunctions. For example, in 'The number is either excessive or not excessive,' the second part 'not excessive' implicitly contains 'deficient' and 'equal' as a separate disjunction. This teaches us to analyze complex disjunctive propositions as layered binary choices, which is essential for rigorous logical analysis in debates (jadal) and legal reasoning (fiqh). For instance, in fiqh, an action is either obligatory, forbidden, or permissible; this is really 'either obligatory or not obligatory,' and if not obligatory, then either forbidden or permissible.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو
مانعة الجمع
الحقيقية
انفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a disjunction like 'This act is either wajib, haram, or mubah,' how many actual disjunctive relations are there at the root?
A) Three, because there are three parts.
B) Two, but only if the parts are exclusive.
C) One, because it reduces to 'either wajib or not wajib,' then a second relation within 'not wajib.'
D) None, because disjunctions with three parts are invalid.

Key Takeaway
Disjunctions with more than two parts are only apparently compound; they always reduce to a series of nested binary relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The ruling for this action is either wajib, haram, or mubah.' Reduce it to nested binary disjunctions and explain the genuine disjunctive relations.
?
Identify whether the following three-part disjunction is of the type manfa'at al-khuluw or manfa'at al-jam': 'Either the planet is habitable, or it is not habitable, or it is uninhabitable.' Then show how it reduces to binary.

Types of Disjunctive Propositions in Islamic Logic
Al-Fanari defines and exemplifies three types of disjunctive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', and mani'at al-khuluw and classify a given disjunction into one of these types using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
True disjunction excludes both conjunction and emptiness; the other two types permit one of these.

In this passage from al-Fanari's Fawa'id, he clarifies a critical distinction among disjunctive propositions. You may have seen disjunctions with multiple parts, but not all are logically the same. Understanding the three types—haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', and mani'at al-khuluw—is essential for analyzing arguments in kalam, fiqh, and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types from the text
Al-Fanari discusses haqiqiyya (true disjunction), mani'at al-jam' (incompatibility of conjunction), and mani'at al-khuluw (incompatibility of emptiness). Note the defining conditions: haqiqiyya prevents both conjunction (both true) and emptiness (both false); mani'at al-jam' prevents conjunction only; mani'at al-khuluw prevents emptiness only.
These types are not merely classifications but have implications for logical validity.
2
Examine examples for each type
For mani'at al-jam', the text gives: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone or an animal' – cannot be two, but can be none (e.g., iron). For mani'at al-khuluw: 'This is either not a tree, not a stone, or not an animal' – cannot be none (because negating all would yield a true affirmation), but can be multiple. For haqiqiyya, the number example 'the number is either excessive or not' is binary.
The haqiqiyya example appears simple but will be explored further in a later lesson.
3
Relate to Islamic disciplines
In usul al-fiqh, a statement like 'This act is either wajib or haram' is not haqiqiyya because it can be neither (e.g., mandub). In kalam, 'The world is either eternal or originated' is haqiqiyya because exactly one must be true. Recognizing the type prevents logical errors in legal and theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : المُرادُ بتركب المنفصلاتِ مِنْ أكثرَ مِنْ جزئين تركبها بحسب الظاهرِ لا بحسَب الحَقِيقَةِ . وَإلا فالانفصال الحقيقي في المثَالِ المذكور على الحقيقيَّةِ بَيْنَ أن يكونَ العدد زائداً - أو لا يكون. ثم على تقدير أن لا يكون زائداً بين كونه ناقصاً أو مساوياً. فإن قلت : فما وَجهُ حكمهم أَنَّ الحقيقيَّة لا تَتَركَّبُ مِنْ أكثر من جزئين ، ومانعة الخلو والجمع يتركَّبانِ؟ (۱) الجواب: أن تركيبها فيما يرى أنها من أكثر من جزأين، والواقع في الحقيقية أنها مركبة من جزئين وهما: إذا قلنا العدد إما زائد أو ليس بزائد فالنسبة واحدة بين الزائد وبين الأضداد له المشتمل عليها. قوله: أو لا يكون: ولا يكون تتضمن الجزئين الآخرين وهما الناقص والمساوي. وهما قضية أخرى لها نسبة واحدة. وإذا كانت أربعة أجزاء مثل العنصر نقول : العنصر: إما نارٌ أو لا.. وهو إما هواء أو لا.. وهو إما ماء أو لا.. وهو الأرض. (۲) أي حكم بعض المناطقة. (۳) أي نقول العدد إما زوج أو فرد فقط. (٤) أي نقول في مانعة الخلو مثل: هذا إما لا شجر ولا حجر ولا حيوان، فإن العناد في منع الخلو بينهما ؛ لأن خلوها أن تأتي بآلة السلب فتصير ليس هذا الشيء إما لا شجراً ولا حجراً ولا حيوانا، ونفي النفي إثبات، يكون هذا الشيء شجراً وحجراً وحيوانا، وهو كذب. قلتَ: وَجْهُهُ أن الحقيقيَّةَ إذا أُريد بها الانفصال الحقيقي بينَ كلِّ جُزئين منها فلا تَكادُ أَنْ تصْدقَ؛ لأنَّ الأَوَّلَ من أجزائها الثلاثةِ مثلاً إذا تحقق؛ فإن تحقق الثاني أيضا ارتفع الانفصال الحقيقي بينهما ، وإنْ لم يتحقق فإن تحقق الثالثُ لم يكن بينَهُ وبينَ الأَوَلِ انفصال. أما مانعة الجمع مثل هذا الشيء إما شجر أو حجر أو حيوان، فالجمع ممنوع ويجوز الرفع بأن نقول: هذا الشيء ليس شجراً ولا حجراً ولا حيوانا وذلك كأن يكون حديداً أو نجماً، فالخلو جائز. (۱) المناطقة ادعوا أن مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو قد تكون أجزاؤها أكثر من جزئين كما مثلنا، ويبقى العناد في منع الجمع ومنع الخلو في جميع أجزائها الزائدة على الاثنين، ولكن الحقيقية مانعة الجمع والخلو معا- لا تتركب من أكثر من جزئين. أراد الفناري أن يبرر هذه الدعوى بالآتي: أي: إن صح ادعاؤهم أنه لا يحصل عناد في الحقيقية إلا بين جزئين فإنّه تكذيب لما مثل به للحقيقية أكثر من جزئين. العدد إما زائد وإما ناقص - وإما مساو. (۲) أي: استدل على المنع بالنقيضين إن وجد أحدهما انتفى الثاني. وهنا إذا قلنا العدد زائد وأتينا معه بالناقص فإن أمكن وصفه بالزائد والناقص معاً فقد انتفى عنه منع الجمع، والمفروض أنها مانعة جمع، وإن لم يمكن تحقيق العناد بين الناقص مع الزائد نذهب إلى الثالث الزائد نذهب إلى الثالث، وهو المساوي، فإن تحقق مع الأول أي العدد زائد ومساءٍ فلا عناد بين الزائد والناقص. وإن لم يتحقق العناد بين الأول، والثالث لم يتحقق بين الثاني والثالث عناد.
I said: What is meant by the composition of disjunctions from more than two parts is their composition in appearance, not in reality. Otherwise, the true disjunction in the mentioned example on the [basis of] truth is between that the number is excessive or not. Then on the assumption that it is not excessive, [it is] between it being deficient or equal. For you said: What is the basis for their judgment that the true disjunction cannot be composed of more than two parts, while the incompatibility [of emptiness] and [incompatibility of] conjunction can be composed? (1) The answer: Their composition in what appears to be more than two parts, but the reality in the true disjunction is that it is composed of two parts, namely: when we say the number is either excessive or not excessive, the relation is one between the excessive and its opposites that encompass it. His statement: 'or not' implies the other two parts, namely the deficient and the equal. They are another proposition with one relation. And if there are four parts like the element, we say: The element is either fire or not... and it is either air or not... and it is either water or not... and it is earth. (2) That is the judgment of some logicians. (3) That is, we say the number is either even or odd only. (4) That is, we say in incompatibility of emptiness like: This is either not a tree nor a stone nor an animal, for the mutual exclusivity in the prevention of emptiness is between them; because its emptiness is that you bring the tool of negation so it becomes 'not this thing is either not a tree nor a stone nor an animal', and the negation of negation is affirmation, so this thing is a tree, a stone, and an animal, which is false. I said: Its basis is that the true disjunction, if by it is meant the real separation between every two parts of it, would hardly be true; because the first of its three parts, for example, if it is realized, then if the second is also realized, the true separation between them is lifted; and if it is not realized, then if the third is realized, there is no separation between it and the first. As for the incompatibility of conjunction like this thing is either a tree or a stone or an animal, conjunction is prohibited, and it is allowed to negate [all] by saying: This thing is not a tree, not a stone, and not an animal, such as if it is iron or a star, so emptiness is permissible. (1) The logicians claimed that the incompatibility of conjunction and the incompatibility of emptiness may have parts more than two, as we exemplified, and the mutual exclusivity remains in the prevention of conjunction and the prevention of emptiness in all their parts beyond the two, but the true disjunction, which prevents both conjunction and emptiness, cannot be composed of more than two parts. al-Fanari wanted to justify this claim as follows: That is, if their claim is true that no mutual exclusivity occurs in the true disjunction except between two parts, then it belies what was exemplified for the true disjunction with more than two parts. The number is either excessive, deficient, or equal. (2) That is, he argued for the prevention by the two contradictories: if one is found, the other is negated. Here, if we say the number is excessive and we bring with it the deficient, then if it is possible to describe it as both excessive and deficient, then the prevention of conjunction is negated, and it is assumed that it is an incompatibility of conjunction. And if it is not possible to realize mutual exclusivity between the deficient and the excessive, we go to the third—the excessive? we go to the third, which is the equal; then if it is realized with the first, i.e., the number is excessive and equal, then there is no mutual exclusivity between the excessive and the deficient. And if mutual exclusivity is not realized between the first and the third, mutual exclusivity is not realized between the second and the third.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Fanari on the types of disjunctive propositions (munfasilat) in logic. He clarifies a key rule: true disjunction (al-haqiqiyya) cannot be composed of more than two parts, while incompatibility of conjunction (mani'at al-jam') and incompatibility of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) can. The text begins by stating that the composition of multi-part disjunctions is only apparent, not real. In reality, a true disjunction like 'the number is either excessive or not' is binary; the 'not' covers both deficient and equal, making it a single opposite. He then anticipates an objection: why do logicians say haqiqiyya cannot exceed two parts, while the other two can? The answer explains that in haqiqiyya, the apparent third part is actually embedded in the negation of the first. An example with four elements (fire, air, water, earth) shows how this works through successive binary divisions. The commentary then contrasts this with examples of mani'at al-khuluw (e.g., 'this is either not a tree, not a stone, or not an animal') and mani'at al-jam' (e.g., 'this is either a tree, a stone, or an animal'). In the latter, joint truth is prohibited but all-false is possible, as in the case of iron or a star. Al-Fanari then provides a deeper justification: if we try to construct a three-part haqiqiyya like 'the number is excessive, deficient, or equal', true mutual exclusivity fails because if the first is true and either of the others is also true, the disjunction is not exclusive. The argument proceeds by considering pairs and showing that no two parts can be truly exclusive while also preventing emptiness. Thus, the rule stands: only the non-truth-functional disjunctions (jam' and khuluw) can exceed two disjuncts.

Key Terms
المنفصلات (al-munfasilat): Disjunctive propositions composed of multiple parts (disjuncts). [Note: No significant shift; standard term in logic.]
الحقيقية (al-haqiqiyya): True disjunction (exclusive or), where exactly one disjunct is true. [Note: This term is used consistently in classical logic.]
مانعة الجمع (mani'at al-jam'): Incompatibility of conjunction; a disjunction where disjuncts cannot be simultaneously true but may all be false. [Note: Also called 'alternative' in some texts.]
مانعة الخلو (mani'at al-khuluw): Incompatibility of emptiness; a disjunction where disjuncts cannot all be false but may all be true. [Note: This term is stable; equivalent to inclusive or.]
العناد (al-'inad): Mutual exclusivity or opposition between two disjuncts in a disjunction. [Note: Empty string.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Consider the statement: 'This liquid is either water or wine or oil.' What type of disjunction is this if it is possible that it is milk (all false)?
A) Haqiqiyya (true disjunction)
B) Mani'at al-jam' (incompatibility of conjunction)
C) Mani'at al-khuluw (incompatibility of emptiness)
D) It cannot be classified

Key Takeaway
In Islamic logic, disjunctions are of three types: haqiqiyya (exclusive), mani'at al-jam' (only conjunction prohibited), and mani'at al-khuluw (only emptiness prohibited).

Identify which type of disjunction is present in the following statement from usul al-fiqh: 'This act is either wajib or haram.' (Assume it can be neither, e.g., mandub.) Justify your answer.
?
Give an example of a mani'at al-khuluw from kalam or fiqh. Explain why it prevents emptiness and provide an Islamic scenario where it would be true.

Why True Disjunction Cannot Have More Than Two Parts
Al-Fanari justifies why haqiqiyya is inherently binary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical reason why a true disjunction (haqiqiyya) can only have two disjuncts, and identify when an apparent multi-part disjunction reduces to binary opposition.

Core Idea
In a true disjunction, the 'two parts' are the first term and its negation, which encompasses all other opposites.

Al-Fanari tackles a subtle point: at first glance, we might think a disjunction like 'the number is excessive, deficient, or equal' is a true disjunction. However, he argues that genuine mutual exclusivity (inad) can only exist between two parts. In this lesson, we will walk through his justification and see why haqiqiyya is inherently binary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logicians' claim
Logicians assert that haqiqiyya cannot exceed two parts, but mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw can. Al-Fanari aims to justify this claim.
This claim is based on the nature of mutual exclusivity ('inad).
2
Examine the apparent three-part example
Consider 'the number is excessive, deficient, or equal' as a supposed haqiqiyya. For it to be true disjunction, exactly one must be true, and no two can be jointly true or jointly false. But between 'excessive' and 'deficient', both can be false (when the number is equal). Thus, emptiness is possible, which violates the condition of haqiqiyya.
This shows that 'excessive' and 'deficient' are not contradictories.
3
Discover the binary reduction
Al-Fanari points out that in reality, the true disjunction is 'the number is excessive or not excessive'. The 'not excessive' includes both 'deficient' and 'equal' under one opposite. So the apparent three parts reduce to a binary opposition between 'excessive' and its negation.
This is why the element example uses successive binary splits: fire/not-fire, then under not-fire: air/not-air, etc.
4
Apply the principle to Islamic examples
In kalam, the statement 'The world is either eternal or originated' is haqiqiyya because 'eternal' and 'originated' are contradictories (exactly one holds). If we added 'or both', it would break haqiqiyya. Similarly, in usul, 'This act is either wajib or not wajib' is haqiqiyya, but 'wajib or haram' often is not.
Recognizing the binary structure prevents logical errors in theological and legal arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Fanari on the types of disjunctive propositions (munfasilat) in logic. He clarifies a key rule: true disjunction (al-haqiqiyya) cannot be composed of more than two parts, while incompatibility of conjunction (mani'at al-jam') and incompatibility of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) can. The answer explains that in haqiqiyya, the apparent third part is actually embedded in the negation of the first. An example with four elements (fire, air, water, earth) shows how this works through successive binary divisions. The commentary then contrasts this with examples of mani'at al-khuluw (e.g., 'this is either not a tree, not a stone, or not an animal') and mani'at al-jam' (e.g., 'this is either a tree, a stone, or an animal'). In the latter, joint truth is prohibited but all-false is possible, as in the case of iron or a star. Al-Fanari then provides a deeper justification: if we try to construct a three-part haqiqiyya like 'the number is excessive, deficient, or equal', true mutual exclusivity fails because if the first is true and either of the others is also true, the disjunction is not exclusive. The argument proceeds by considering pairs and showing that no two parts can be truly exclusive while also preventing emptiness. Thus, the rule stands: only the non-truth-functional disjunctions (jam' and khuluw) can exceed two disjuncts.

Key Terms
المنفصلات (al-munfasilat): Disjunctive propositions composed of multiple parts (disjuncts). [Note: No significant shift; standard term in logic.]
الحقيقية (al-haqiqiyya): True disjunction (exclusive or), where exactly one disjunct is true. [Note: This term is used consistently in classical logic.]
مانعة الجمع (mani'at al-jam'): Incompatibility of conjunction; a disjunction where disjuncts cannot be simultaneously true but may all be false. [Note: Also called 'alternative' in some texts.]
مانعة الخلو (mani'at al-khuluw): Incompatibility of emptiness; a disjunction where disjuncts cannot all be false but may all be true. [Note: This term is stable; equivalent to inclusive or.]
العناد (al-'inad): Mutual exclusivity or opposition between two disjuncts in a disjunction. [Note: Empty string.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Fanari, why can't a true disjunction (haqiqiyya) have three parts like 'the number is excessive, deficient, or equal'?
A) Because the parts are not all mutually exclusive; some can be jointly true.
B) Because there is a pair that can both be false, violating the condition that exactly one must be true.
C) Because the number cannot be both excessive and deficient at the same time.
D) Because the third part is always a repetition of one of the first two.

Key Takeaway
True disjunction (haqiqiyya) is always binary because only contradictory terms produce genuine mutual exclusivity and prevent both conjunction and emptiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a haqiqiyya on a theological topic, such as 'The Qur'an is either created or uncreated.' Explain why adding a third option like 'both' or 'neither' would violate the conditions of haqiqiyya.
?
Identify the implicit binary structure in the statement from usul al-fiqh: 'The human action is either obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, or forbidden.' Is this a genuine haqiqiyya? Why or why not? If not, what type of disjunction is it, and how could you reduce it to a series of binary haqiqiyyas?

Why the Real Disjunction Cannot Have More Than Two Parts
Commentator discusses haqiqiyya disjunction, its condition, and Fanari's justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a real disjunction and explain why it is limited to two parts using the concept of logical opposition ('inad).

Core Idea
The real disjunction requires full opposition, which only two parts can sustain.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key restriction in Mantiq: while disjunctions that prevent union alone or emptiness alone can have multiple parts, the real disjunction (haqiqiyya) that prevents both cannot. This lesson unpacks the logic behind that claim, including al-Fanari's numerical argument.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three types of disjunction
Identify the definitions of maniat al-jam, maniat al-khuluww, and haqiqiyya in your own words. Note that the first two allow more than two parts, while the third does not.
2
Understand the condition for haqiqiyya
Why does 'inad (opposition) require both prevention of union and prevention of emptiness? Why is this harder to achieve than either alone?
3
Test with a three-part example
Using the number example (surplus, deficient, equal), check whether pairs can satisfy both conditions. Show that at least one pair fails.
4
Follow al-Fanari's justification
Explain how his reasoning shows that only two parts can be fully opposed. Trace the steps: checking surplus+deficient, then surplus+equal, then deficient+equal.
5
Apply the commentator's final verdict
Distinguish between 'single separation' (infisal wahid) and 'absolute separation' (mutlaq al-infisal). When does each apply?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قُلْتُ: وَجْهُهُ أن الحقيقيَّةَ إذا أُريد بها الانفصال الحقيقي بينَ كلِّ جُزئين منها فلا تَكادُ أَنْ تصْدقَ؛ لأنَّ الأَوَّلَ من أجزائها الثلاثةِ مثلاً إذا تحقق؛ فإن تحقق الثاني أيضا ارتفع الانفصال الحقيقي بينهما ، وإنْ لم يتحقق فإن تحقق الثالثُ لم يكن بينَهُ وبينَ الأَوَّلِ انفصال. أَمَّا مانعة الجمع مثل هذا الشيء إما شجر أو حجر أو حيوان، فالجمع ممنوع ويجوز الرفع بأن نقول: هذا الشيء ليس شجراً ولا حجراً ولا حيوانا وذلك كأن يكون حديداً أو نجماً، فالخلو جائز. المناطقة ادعوا أن مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو قد تكون أجزاؤها أكثر من جزئين كما مثلنا، ويبقى العناد في منع الجمع ومنع الخلو في جميع أجزائها الزائدة على الاثنين، ولكن الحقيقية مانعة الجمع والخلو معا لا تتركب من أكثر من جزئين. أَرَادَ الفَنَارِي أن يُبَرِّرَ هذه الدَّعْوَى بالآتي: أي إن صحَّ ادعاؤهم أنه لا يحصل عناد في الحقيقية إلا بين جزئين فإنّه تكذيب لما مثل به للحقيقية أكثر من جزئين. الْعَدَدُ إِمَّا زَائِدٌ وَإِمَّا نَاقِصٌ وَإِمَّا مُسَاوٍ. أي استدل على المنع بالنقيضين إن وجد أحدهما انتفى الثاني. وهنا إذا قلنا العدد زائد وأتينا معه بالناقص فإن أمكن وصفه بالزائد والناقص معاً فقد انتفى عنه منع الجمع، والمفروض أنها مانعة جمع، وإن لم يمكن تحقيق العناد بين الناقص مع الزائد نذهب إلى الثالث وهو المساوي، فإن تحقق مع الأول أي العدد زائد ومساو فلا عناد بين الزائد والناقص. وإن لم يتحقق العناد بين الأول والثالث لم يتحقق بين الثاني والثالث عناد. وإن لم يتحقق لم يكن بينه وبين الثاني انفصال. وَأَمَّا الأُخْرَيَانِ فَيَصْدُقَانِ، وإِنْ أُريدَ منعُ الخلو ومنعُ الجمعِ بينَ كلَّ جزئين مُعَيَّنَيْنِ مِنْ أجزائهما، كما في المثالين المذكورَيْنِ. هَذَا: وَالحَقُّ: أَنَّ المُرادَ بالانفصال إِنْ كَانَ انفِصَالاً واحِداً لا يتحقق إلا بَيْن جزئين. وإن كانَ مطلق الانفصال فيتحقق بين جُزئين وأكثرَ في الأقسامِ الثَّلاثَةِ.
I said: Its aspect is that the real [disjunction] – if what is intended by it is the real separation between every two parts of it – then it hardly ever holds true; because the first of its parts, for example three parts, if it is realized, then if the second is also realized, the real separation between them is removed; and if it is not realized, then if the third is realized, there is no separation between it and the first. As for preventing union, such as 'this thing is either a tree or a stone or an animal', union is prohibited and it is permissible to negate by saying 'this thing is not a tree, nor a stone, nor an animal', and that may be such as iron or a star, so emptiness is permissible. The logicians claimed that preventing union and preventing emptiness may have more than two parts, as we exemplified, and the opposition remains in preventing union and preventing emptiness in all their parts exceeding two, but the real disjunction, preventing both union and emptiness, does not consist of more than two parts. al-Fanari wanted to justify this claim by what follows: i.e. if their claim is correct that no opposition occurs in the real disjunction except between two parts, then it would be a falsification of what was exemplified by the real disjunction of more than two parts. The number is either surplus, deficient, or equal. That is, he argued for the prohibition by the two contradictories: if one is found, the second is negated. And here, if we say the number is surplus and we bring with it the deficient, then if it is possible for it to be described as both surplus and deficient, then the prevention of union is negated from it, and it is assumed to be preventing union; and if opposition cannot be realized between the deficient and the surplus, we go to the third, which is the equal; if it is realized with the first, i.e. the number is surplus and equal, then there is no opposition between the surplus and the deficient. And if opposition is not realized between the first and the third, it is not realized between the second and the third. And if it is not realized, then there is no separation between it and the second. As for the other two, they hold true, if what is intended is prevention of emptiness and prevention of union between every two specific parts of their parts, as in the two mentioned examples. This: And the truth is that if what is meant by separation is one separation, it is only realized between two parts; and if it is absolute separation, it is realized between two and more, in the three divisions.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of disjunctive propositions (qadiyya munfasila). The commentator begins by explaining the condition for a real disjunction (haqiqiyya): if we intend a real separation (infisal haqiqi) between every two parts, it rarely holds true. For example, with three parts, if the first is realized and then the second is also realized, the real separation between them is lost; if the second is not realized but the third is, there is no separation between it and the first. This shows the difficulty. The commentator then contrasts with 'man'at al-jam'' (preventing union) and 'man'at al-khuluww' (preventing emptiness), giving examples like 'this thing is either a tree, a stone, or an animal'. In such cases, union is prevented (it cannot be both tree and stone) but emptiness is allowed (it could be neither, e.g., iron). The logicians claim that these two types can have more than two parts while still maintaining opposition (inad) in preventing union or emptiness across all parts. However, the real disjunction (preventing both union and emptiness) cannot have more than two parts. al-Fanari attempts to justify this claim using a scheme based on number being surplus, deficient, or equal. The idea is to check for opposition (inad) between parts. If we assume three numbers: surplus, deficient, equal. When we try to pair surplus with deficient, if one could be both, that violates prevention of union; if not, we check surplus with equal. If that pairing also fails, then there is no opposition between deficient and equal either. The conclusion is that the only way to have full opposition (both prevention of union and emptiness) is with exactly two parts. Finally, the commentator gives his own verdict (al-haqq): if by 'separation' we mean a single separation (infisal wahid), it only occurs between two parts; if we mean absolute separation (mutlaq al-infisal), it can occur between two or more in all three divisions. This clarifies the issue.

Key Terms
الحقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
العناد
الانفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the logicians arbitrarily decided so.
C) Because the Arabic language does not allow more than two alternatives.
D) Because al-Fanari proved it using arithmetic.

Key Takeaway
A real disjunction (haqiqiyya) requires full logical opposition ('inad) between its parts, which can only be achieved with exactly two parts.
