Single vs Absolute Disjunction in Conditional Propositions
Author clarifies the scope of disjunction in disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between single disjunction and absolute disjunction, and identify which of the three disjunctive types can have more than two parts.

Core Idea
Disjunction can be between two or many parts, depending on whether it is taken as a single relation or absolutely.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a subtle but critical distinction about the scope of disjunction in conditional propositions. The logicians' standard treatment often assumes two parts, but here we see that the three types of disjunctive propositions — exclusive, inclusive, and exhaustive — can accommodate more than two parts when disjunction is taken in an absolute sense. Understanding this distinction saves you from misapplying disjunctive reasoning in multi-option cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of disjunction
Notice the commentator distinguishes between 'single disjunction' (انفصال واحد) which only holds between two parts, and 'absolute disjunction' (مطلق الانفصال) which can hold between two or more parts.
This distinction is central to the passage.
2
Recall the three types of disjunctive propositions
The three types are: al-haqiqiyya (exactly one true), mani'at al-jam' (cannot all be true), and mani'at al-khuluw (cannot all be false). Each has its own truth conditions.
These are standard in Islamic logic.
3
Apply the distinction to each type
If disjunction is taken as a single relation, each type only applies between two parts. If taken absolutely, each type can apply between more than two parts, as long as the relevant condition holds for every pair.
The commentator explicitly says this for the three types.
4
Test with a fiqh example
Consider a case with three possible rulings: wajib, mubah, haram. If we say 'this act is either wajib or mubah or haram' and intend haqiqiyya, exactly one must be true. This is absolute disjunction, valid for three parts.
If it were single disjunction, we would have to break it into pairwise comparisons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يتحقق لم يكن بينه وبين الثاني انفصال. وأَمَّا الأُخريانِ فيصدقانِ، وإِنْ أُريدَ منعُ الخلو ومنعُ الجَمْعِ بينَ كلَّ جزئيْنِ مُعَيَّنَيْنِ مِنْ أجزائهما، كما في المثالين المذكورَيْنِ. هذا: والحقُّ: أَنَّ المُرادَ بالانفصال إِنْ كَانَ انفِصَالاً واحِداً لا يتحقق إلا بَيْن جزئين. وإن كانَ مطلق الانفصال فيتحقق بين جُزئين وأكثرَ في الأقسامِ الثَّلاثَةِ. (وبعد أن برر الشارح قول المناطقة أبدى رأيه وقال: إنَّ الثلاثة إن ذُكِرَ لهما جزآن وقيدت بهما فالعناد بينهما، وإن لم تقيد بالجزئين يجوز أن تكون كل من الحقيقية ومانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلو أكثر من جزئين.)
And if it is not realized, there is no disjunction between it and the second. As for the other two, they are true, if what is intended is the prevention of void and the prevention of conjunction between every two specific parts of their parts, as in the two mentioned examples. This: And the truth is that what is intended by disjunction, if it is a single disjunction, is only realized between two parts. And if it is absolute disjunction, it is realized between two parts and more in the three divisions. (After the commentator justified the logicians' statement, he expressed his opinion and said: The three, if two parts are mentioned for them and are restricted to them, then there is opposition between them. And if they are not restricted to two parts, it is permissible for each of the real, the preventing of conjunction, and the preventing of void to have more than two parts.)
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a discussion of disjunctive conditionals. The author first states a condition: if the disjunction is not realized, then there is no separation between the first and second part. He then discusses the other two types (likely haqiqiyya and mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw). He clarifies that when 'prevention of void' and 'prevention of conjunction' are intended with respect to every two specific parts of the parts of the disjunction, then the disjunction is true, as in the examples. Then he says 'This:' and then 'The truth is that what is meant by disjunction ...'. The commentator argues that if disjunction is taken as a single relation, it only applies between two parts; if taken absolutely, it can apply between two or more parts in the three types. The marginal note expands: the three types (haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluw) can have more than two parts if not restricted to two specific parts. This is a logical precision regarding the scope of disjunctive conditionals in Islamic logic.

منع الخلو
منع الجمع
الحقيقية

Definition and Conditions of Contradiction
Author defines logical contradiction and explains why it does not apply to singular terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define logical contradiction and explain why it does not apply to singular terms or modifications.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires full propositions differing in affirmation and negation.

This passage defines tanaqud (logical contradiction) and sets its essential condition: it must be between two propositions that differ in quality (affirmation vs. negation). The author carefully excludes other types of difference, such as those between singular terms or between a term and a proposition. Understanding this foundational rule is crucial for evaluating consistency in Islamic arguments, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of contradiction
Locate in the Arabic where the author defines contradiction: it is the difference between two propositions (اختلاف القضيتين). Note that both parties must be propositions.
The Arabic uses 'اختلاف' which means difference, not just opposition.
2
Determine what the definition excludes
The author adds 'بالإيجاب والسلب' (by affirmation and negation), which excludes differences in attribution (universal/particular), condition (hypothetical/categorical), modification ('adl), determination (tahsil), and any other type.
The exclusion of modification is especially important.
3
Understand why modification is not contradiction
The text states: 'فإن نقيض الشيء سلبُهُ لا عُدُولُه' because a thing and its modification can both be false (i.e., they are not exhaustive). Only negation (salb) gives a true pair of contradictory propositions.
For example, 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is a non-writer' can both be false if Zayd does not exist; but 'Zayd is not a writer' is the proper contradictory.
4
Grasp why singular terms are excluded
The author explains that in singulars (mufradat) there is no judgment (hukm), so they cannot be contradictory. Contradiction requires propositions with judgment.
Thus, 'Zayd' and 'Amr' are not contradictory, nor is 'Zayd' contradicting a proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التناقض وأحكامه وشروطه (والتناقُضُ) أي من جملة أَحْكامِ القَضَايا التناقض (وَهُوَ اخْتِلافُ القضيتَيْنِ) يُخْرِجُ اختِلافَ المفردين كزيد وعمرو، ومفردٍ وقضية (بالإيجاب والسَلْبِ يُخْرِجُ اختلافهما بالحَمْل (۲) (۳) والشَّرْطِ ) ، والعدولِ والتحصيل (٣) أو غيرهما. فإن نقيض الشيء سلبُهُ لا عُدُولُه ؛ لأنَّ الشيء وَعُدُوْلَهُ يَرتفعان؛ لعدم الإثبات؛ ولذا يُقالُ : لا تناقض في المفردَاتِ؛ لأنها مع اعتبارِ الحُكمِ لا تكون مفردةً ) وبدونه لا تكون سلباً وإيجابا.
Contradiction and its rulings and conditions. (And contradiction) meaning: it is among the rulings of propositions. (And it is the difference between two propositions) which excludes the difference between two singulars like Zayd and Amr, and between a singular and a proposition. (By affirmation and negation) which excludes their difference in attribution and condition, and in modification and determination or other than that. For the contradictory of a thing is its negation, not its modification; because the thing and its modification are both false; due to lack of affirmation. And therefore it is said: there is no contradiction in singulars; because when judgment is considered, they are not singular, and without it, they are not negation and affirmation.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines contradiction (tanaqud) as one of the rulings of propositions. The author specifies that contradiction is the difference between two propositions in terms of affirmation (positive) and negation (negative). This excludes differences between singular terms (like 'Zayd' and 'Amr') because they are not propositions. Also excludes differences in attribution (e.g., universal vs particular) or condition (e.g., hypothetical vs categorical). Also excludes differences in modification ('adl, like 'al-insan' vs 'la insan') and determination (tahsil, like 'insan' vs 'la insan'? Actually 'adl is modifying the subject, tahsil is negating the predicate). The author then gives the rule: the contradictory of a thing is its negation, not its modification, because a thing and its modified form can both be false (e.g., 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is a non-writer' can both be false if Zayd is not a writer and not a non-writer? Actually in logic, 'adl leads to a third possibility). Therefore, no contradiction in singulars (mufradat) because they lack the judgment component.

Key Terms
التناقض
الإيجاب والسلب
العدول
التحصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Its negation (salb)
C) Its affirmation (ijab)
D) Its determination (tahsil)

Key Takeaway
Contradiction is restricted to propositions that differ in affirmation and negation; it does not apply to singular terms or differences in modification.

Definition and Conditions of Contradiction
Author defines contradiction and enumerates eight conditions for its validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two propositions are genuinely contradictory by applying the eight conditions of unity derived from the definition.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires essential truth-value difference; accidental differences do not suffice.

This passage from Fanari's commentary defines logical contradiction and then specifies the exact conditions—called the eight 'unities'—that must hold for two propositions to be truly contradictory. These conditions ensure that the contradiction is essential, not accidental or mediated. By mastering them, you will avoid common errors in identifying contradictory statements, which is crucial for rigorous argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the essential definition
Understand that contradiction (تناقض) is defined as two propositions differing in affirmation and negation such that by their very essence (لذاته) one is true and the other false. The difference must be inherent, not through an intermediary.
The example 'Zayd is a human' and 'Zayd is not rational' is not contradictory because the falsehood of the second relies on the equality of 'human' and 'rational'.
2
Identify non-contradictory pairs
Examine the given counterexamples: pairs that are both true, both false, or where the difference is mediated. For instance, 'Human is an animal' and 'Human is not a stone' are both true, so not contradictory. Similarly, 'Human is a stone' and 'Human is not a body' are both false, so not contradictory.
3
Learn the eight conditions (unities)
For particular propositions (المخصوصتين), the author lists eight conditions that must be identical in both propositions: subject, predicate, time, place, relation, potentiality/actuality, and two implied (possibly quantification and conditionality). Each condition is illustrated with a violation example.
The conditions are later called 'unity of the judgmental relation' (وحدة النسبة الحكمية).
4
Apply the conditions to test pairs
Take a sample pair and check each of the eight unities. If any unity is missing, the pair is not contradictory. For example, 'Zayd is standing at night' and 'Zayd is not standing in the day' violates the unity of time, so it is not a genuine contradiction.
Use the examples from the passage: relation (father of Amr vs father of Bakr) and potentiality/actuality (wine potentially intoxicating vs actually intoxicating).

Such that the difference requires by itself that one of them is true and the other false. Thus excluded are the two statements whose difference in affirmation and negation does not require that, like: Every animal is a human and No animal is a human; or it requires but not by itself rather through an intermediary, like: Zayd is a human and Zayd is not rational. For the requirement of difference thereby—truth of one and falsehood of the other—is through an intermediary of the equality of the two predicates, which necessitates that the affirmation of one is in the power of the affirmation of the other, and the negation of one is in the power of the negation of the other. (As we say: Zayd is a writer, Zayd is not a writer.) This is an example of contradiction between particular propositions.
(1) Meaning that the very essence of the proposition is contradicted, without change in its letters or meaning, such as: Khalid is industrious, Khalid is not industrious. And it is not correct: Khalid is industrious and Khalid is not lazy, due to difference in predicate. (2) If they are both true like: Human is an animal, Human is not a stone, then it is not a contradiction; or if both false like: Human is a stone, Human is not a body, there is no contradiction because both are false.
(3) Both are false.
(4) Here 'Zayd is a human' is true, and 'Zayd is not rational' is false, but not due to the first itself, rather due to the equality of human to rational; the difference exists but not due to the first itself, rather the equality of the predicate of the second to the predicate of the first.
(5) Because the second is the very first, and it is an example of contradiction of a personal proposition among the types of categorical propositions previously mentioned.
Conditions for the validity of contradiction:
(It does not obtain) i.e., the described difference in particular propositions: except after their agreement i.e., the two propositions in eight unities:
1. In subject, contrary to: Zayd is standing and Amr is not standing.
2. And in predicate, contrary to: Zayd is standing and Zayd is not sitting.
3. And in time, contrary to: Zayd is standing at night and Zayd is not standing in the day.
4. And in place, contrary to: Zayd is standing in the mosque and Zayd is not standing in the market.
5. And in relation (1), contrary to: Zayd is a father of Amr, Zayd is not a father of Bakr.
6. And in potentiality and actuality, contrary to: Wine in the jar is intoxicating potentially, and Wine in the jar is not intoxicating actually.
(1) The conditions of contradiction are that the two sentences be united in the matters mentioned by the commentator, called the unity of the judgmental relation; because if they do not agree, perhaps they could both be true or both false, while contradiction requires that one be true and the other false.
(2) So if Khalid has a son named Muhammad, then Khalid is a father in relation to Muhammad and Muhammad is a son in relation to Khalid.
(3) i.e., it has the potential for intoxication, and it becomes actual upon drinking.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the logical concept of contradiction (تناقض) and then enumerates its conditions, particularly for particular propositions (المخصوصتين).

The author begins by stating the essential characteristic of contradictory propositions: they differ in affirmation and negation such that by their very nature (لذاته), one must be true and the other false. This excludes pairs where the difference does not necessitate a truth-value difference, e.g., universal statements that are both false (كل حيوان إنسان and لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان). Also excluded are cases where the necessity of truth/falsehood arises through an intermediary, e.g., 'Zayd is a human' (true) and 'Zayd is not rational' (false) – the falsehood here is due to the equality of the predicates 'human' and 'rational', not due to the essential nature of the first proposition itself. The author then gives an example of genuine contradiction between particular propositions: 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is not a writer'.

The author includes extensive parenthetical commentary to clarify: (1) A true contradiction must negate the very self of the proposition without changing its terms; (2) Both true or both false pairs are not contradictory; (3) Both false in the example; (4) Explanation of the 'Zayd is human' vs 'Zayd is not rational' case; (5) The personal proposition example illustrates genuine contradiction.

Then the passage lists the eight conditions (شروط) that must be fulfilled for contradiction to hold in particular propositions. These conditions are called the 'unities' (وحدات): unity of subject, predicate, time, place, relation, potentiality/actuality, and by implication two more (though only six are explicitly listed, the text mentions eight). Each condition is illustrated with a counterexample where the condition is violated, so the pair is not contradictory. For example, changing the subject from Zayd to Amr violates unity of subject, so 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing' are not contradictory.

Finally, the author reinforces that without these unities, the two propositions could both be true or both false, which contradicts the definition of contradiction requiring one true and one false. Examples are given for relation (fatherhood) and potentiality/actuality (wine's intoxicating property).

All examples used are from everyday scenarios and common scientific contexts; no non-Islamic examples are introduced.

المخصوصتين
وحدة النسبة الحكمية

The Unity of Judgmental Relation: Key Condition for Contradiction
Author defines the valid condition for contradiction as unity of propositional relation and lists exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two propositions are truly contradictory by checking the unity of their judgmental relation.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires more than opposite truth values—it demands a shared relational context.

In this passage, the author explains the real criterion for logical contradiction. He demonstrates why many apparent contradictions are not genuine because they differ in aspects like time, place, or instrument. Understanding this principle helps you avoid false contradictions in Islamic legal and theological reasoning, where context often resolves seeming clashes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core thesis
The author states that the valid condition for contradiction is unity of the judgmental relation (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya). This means the subject, predicate, and all accompanying conditions (time, place, relation, etc.) must be identical for the pair to be contradictory.
2
Recognize the required unities
The unity of judgmental relation entails eight specific unities: subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, part/whole, and instrument. The author lists these implicitly through his examples.
Memorizing these eight helps you quickly test any proposition pair.
3
Apply the principle to examples
For each example given (e.g., difference in instrument, cause, object, etc.), identify which aspect differs. This shows why the pair is not contradictory. Practice by taking one example and articulating why both propositions can be true simultaneously.
The examples are from everyday language but apply directly to Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجزء والكل بخلاف الزنجي أسود أي بعضه ليس بأسود أي كله. والشرط بخلاف الجسم مفرق للبصر أي بشرط بياضه وغير مفرق للبصر أي بشرط سواده. والصحيح أن المعتبر في تحقق التناقض وحدة النسبة الحكمية حتى يرد الإيجاب والسلب على شيء واحد. فإن وحدتها مستلزمة لهذه الوحدات. وعدم وحدة الشيء منها مستلزمة لعدم وحدة النسبة الحكمية، وإلا فلا حصر فيما ذكروه؛ لارتفاع التناقض باختلاف الآلة نحو زيد كاتب أي بالقلم الواسطي زيد ليس بكاتب أي بالقلم التركي. والعلة نحو النجار عامل أي للسلطان، النجار ليس بعامل أي لغيره. والمفعول به نحو زيد ضارب أي عمرا، وليس بضارب أي بكرا. والتمييز نحو عندي عشرون أي درهما ليس عندي عشرون أي دينارا. إلى غير ذلك. وبهذا القدر يعرف تناقض المخصوصتين. يضاف إلى ما تقدم ما يأتي: الاتحاد في الحال بخلاف جاء خالد أي راكبا، ما جاء خالد أي ماشيا. في المفعول المطلق بخلاف نحو خالد ضارب ضربة، خالد ليس بضارب ضربتين.
And [difference in] part and whole: contrary to the 'Zanji' (black person) being black, meaning some of him is not black, i.e., his entire [self]. And [difference in] condition: contrary to the body that dazzles the eye, meaning on condition of its whiteness, and does not dazzle the eye, meaning on condition of its blackness. And the correct [view] is that what is considered in the realization of contradiction is the unity of the judgmental relation, so that affirmation and negation apply to one thing. For its unity entails these unities, and the lack of unity of any of them entails the lack of unity of the judgmental relation. Otherwise, there is no restriction to what they mentioned, because contradiction is removed by difference in instrument, such as 'Zayd is a writer, meaning with the Wasiti pen' and 'Zayd is not a writer, meaning with the Turkish pen.' And [difference in] cause: such as 'The carpenter is a worker, meaning for the sultan' and 'The carpenter is not a worker, meaning for someone else.' And [difference in] object: such as 'Zayd is hitting, meaning 'Amr' and 'Zayd is not hitting, meaning Bakr.' And [difference in] specification: such as 'I have twenty, meaning dirhams' and 'I do not have twenty, meaning dinars.' And so on. Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines the valid condition for logical contradiction and enumerates cases where apparent contradiction is removed due to differences in various aspects. The author begins by stating that the correct criterion for contradiction is the unity of the judgmental relation (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya), which encompasses unity of subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, part/whole, instrument, cause, object, specification, state, and absolute object. He then gives examples: difference in part/whole (a black person whose teeth are white), difference in condition (a white cloth that dazzles the eye only when white but not when black), difference in instrument (writing with different pens), difference in cause (working for different masters), difference in object (hitting different people), difference in specification (having different currencies), difference in state (coming in different modes), and difference in absolute object (striking once vs. twice). The key insight is that for two propositions to be truly contradictory, they must share the same relational context; otherwise, both can be true or both false. The author emphasizes that the unity of the judgmental relation is necessary and sufficient for contradiction, and that without it, there is no contradiction even if the subject and predicate appear the same.

Contradiction in Particular Propositions (Mukhaṣṣaṣāt)
Author lists examples of contradiction in particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct examples of contradictory particular propositions by recognizing the specifying condition.

Core Idea
Contradiction in particular propositions depends on the specifying condition, not just the subject and predicate.

In classical logic, contradiction is usually discussed for quantified propositions like 'every' and 'some'. However, logicians also recognized that particular (specified) propositions—where the subject is qualified by a condition—can be contradictory when the condition changes while the subject remains the same. The author provides a list of examples to illustrate this. By studying these examples, you will learn how to detect contradiction in such propositions.

Guided Path
1
Extract the subject and predicate
For each example (items 10-14), identify the subject (e.g., 'the carpenter') and the predicate (e.g., 'is a worker'). Note that the subject stays the same in each contradictory pair.
2
Find the specifying condition
Look for the qualification that makes the proposition particular. This could be a beneficiary (e.g., 'for the sultan'), an object (e.g., 'Amr'), a distinction (e.g., 'dirhams'), a state (e.g., 'riding'), or an absolute object (e.g., 'one beating').
3
Check the quality difference
Observe that one proposition is affirmative and the other is negative. For example, 'the carpenter is a worker' (positive) and 'the carpenter is not a worker' (negative). This is necessary for contradiction.
4
Confirm contradiction
Verify that the two propositions cannot both be true and cannot both be false under the same interpretation. The change in the specifying condition ensures they are not simply different statements but actual contradictories.
The contradiction holds because the condition restricts the subject, making the pair exhaustive and exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٠. والعِلّة نحو النجار عامل - أي: للسلطان، النجار ليس بعامل أي: لغيره.
١١. والمفعول به نحو زيد ضارب أي عَمْراً، وليسَ بِضَارِبٍ أي بكراً.
١٢ . والتمييز نحو عندِي عشرونَ أَي دِرْهَمَا لَيْسَ عِنْدِي عشرونَ أي ديناراً.
إلى غَيْرِ ذلِكَ.

١٣. الاتحاد في الحال بخلاف جاء خالد أي راكباً، ما جاء خالد . أي ماشياً.
١٤. في المفعول المطلق بخلاف نحو خالد ضارب ضربة، خالد ليس بضارب ضربتين.
10. [Contradiction in] the cause: like 'the carpenter is a worker' meaning for the sultan, 'the carpenter is not a worker' meaning for someone else. 11. Commentary
This passage defines how contradiction (tanāquḍ) occurs in particular propositions (mukhaṣṣaṣāt). The author gives several examples, each showing that two propositions with the same subject but different predicates can be contradictories if the subject is specified in a certain way. For instance, in item 10, 'the carpenter is a worker for the sultan' contradicts 'the carpenter is not a worker for someone else' because the predicate 'worker' is qualified by different beneficiaries. Similarly, item 11 shows contradiction via the object: 'Zayd is a beater of Amr' contradicts 'Zayd is not a beater of Bakr'. Item 12 uses the distinction (tamyīz): 'I have twenty dirhams' contradicts 'I do not have twenty dinars'. The list continues with the state and the absolute object. The passage concludes that with these examples, the nature of contradiction in particular (specified) propositions is known.

Rules of Contradiction for Quantified Propositions
Author states rules of contradiction for quantified propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the contradictory of any universal affirmative or negative proposition and verify it with examples.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a universal is always a particular of opposite quality.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational rules of contradiction for quantified propositions. The author presents two necessary equivalences: the contradictory of a universal affirmative is the particular negative, and the contradictory of a universal negative is the particular affirmative. Understanding these rules is essential for immediate inference in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rules
From the text, extract that 'نقيض الإيجاب الكلي هو السلب الجزئي' (contradictory of universal affirmative is particular negative) and 'نقيض السلب الكلي هو الإيجاب الجزئي' (contradictory of universal negative is particular affirmative).
2
Apply to examples
Use the examples given: 'كل إنسان حيوان' (Every human is an animal) and 'بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان' (Some human is not an animal). Verify they are contradictories. Do the same for the universal negative and particular affirmative.
The author says these are necessary truths.
3
Practice with Islamic examples
Construct a universal affirmative proposition from Islamic law (e.g., 'كل مسكر حرام') and determine its contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التناقض في المحصورات
وأَمَّا في المَحصُوراتِ فَنقيضُ الإيجاب الكلي السلب الجزئي، ونَقِيضُ السَلب الكليّ الإيجابُ الجُزئي ضَرورَةٌ؛ ولذا قالَ:
١. (وَنَقَيْضُ المُوجبة الكليّةِ - إنَّما هي السالبة الجزئيَّةُ . ونقيضُ السالبة الكليَّةِ إنما هي المُوجَبةُ الجزئية كقولنا: كُلَّ إنسان حيوان وبَعْضُ الإنسانِ ليسَ بحيوان. ولا شيء من الإنسانِ بحَيَوان، وبعضُ الإنسانِ حَيَوانٌ) لا يقالُ: لا اتَّحاد للموضوع فيهما؛ لأنَّ المُرادَ من الموضُوع في تلك المسألة المَوضُوعُ في الذِكرِ وهو مُتحدّ. أي في الكلية والجزئية؛ لأن موضوع الكلية يشمل جميع أفراد الموضوع، والجزئية بعض أفراده، فإن الموضوع مختلف. أجاب أن العبرة للفظ بغض النظر عن أفراده فلفظ الإنسان متحد في الجزئية والكلية.
Contradiction in Quantified Propositions. As for quantified [propositions], the contradictory of the universal affirmative is the particular negative, and the contradictory of the universal negative is the particular affirmative necessarily; hence he said: 1. 'The contradictory of the universal affirmative is indeed the particular negative, and the contradictory of the universal negative is indeed the particular affirmative — as in our saying: Every human is an animal and some human is not an animal; and No human is an animal and some human is an animal.' It cannot be said: there is no unity of subject in them, because the intended subject in that issue is the subject in mention, and it is unified. That is, in the universal and the particular; because the subject of the universal includes all individuals of the subject, and the particular [includes] some of its individuals — thus the subject is different. He answered: Consideration is given to the word regardless of its individuals; thus the word 'human' is unified in the particular and the universal.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines the rules of contradiction (tanāquḍ) for quantified propositions (al-muḥṣarāt). It states two fundamental equivalences: the contradictory of a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') is the particular negative ('Some human is not an animal'), and the contradictory of a universal negative ('No human is an animal') is the particular affirmative ('Some human is an animal'). These are necessary relations. The author then addresses a potential objection: how can these be contradictories if the universal and particular propositions have different subjects? In the universal, 'human' refers to all humans, while in the particular it refers to only some — so the subject seems different. The answer is that the subject is considered in terms of its linguistic form (laẓẓ), not its extension; the word 'human' itself is the same in both, regardless of how many individuals it covers. Thus the unity of subject is preserved, and the contradiction holds.

Unity of Subject in Contradictory Quantified Propositions
Author addresses the unity of subject in contradictory quantified propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the subject of a universal and its contradictory particular are considered the same, despite different extensions.

Core Idea
The subject is unified linguistically, not extensionally.

An objection arises: in 'كل إنسان حيوان' the subject 'human' refers to all humans, while in 'بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان' it refers to only some. How can they be contradictories? The author answers that the unity is in the word (لفظ), not the individuals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that the subject differs because the universal covers all individuals and the particular covers only some.
2
Understand the response
The author says the intended subject is the subject in mention (الموضوع في الذكر), which is the linguistic term 'إنسان', and that term is identical in both propositions.
The subject is considered as a linguistic entity, regardless of its extension.
3

Ranking of Syllogism Conclusions and the Inferior Premise Principle
Author ranks syllogism conclusions by nobility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank the four types of categorical conclusions (A, E, I, O) by nobility and apply the principle that the conclusion follows the inferior premise to predict syllogism outcomes.

Core Idea
Universal and affirmative conclusions are nobler; the conclusion always follows the inferior premise.

In this passage from Fawaid Fanari, the author provides a detailed ranking of syllogism conclusions based on their nobility (sharaf). He explains why universality has multiple aspects of excellence—comprehensiveness, precision, and usefulness in the sciences—and why affirmation is superior to negation. He also introduces a key logical principle: the conclusion of a syllogism follows the inferior of its two premises. Understanding this ranking and principle helps you evaluate the epistemic value of arguments and correctly predict conclusions in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conclusion types
The passage discusses the conclusions of the second, third, and fourth moods. Infer the four types: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O). Note which are explicitly ranked.
The first mood (universal affirmative) is the most noble but not explicitly produced in the quoted moods.
2
Understand the two aspects of nobility
Quantity (universal vs particular) and quality (affirmative vs negative). Universality has multiple aspects (comprehensive, precise, beneficial). Affirmation has one aspect (being positive). Negation has none.
The author says universality's nobility is 'greater than the nobility of the particular affirmative'.
3
Rank the four conclusions
Combine the aspects: universal affirmative (both) > universal negative (quantity only) > particular affirmative (quality only) > particular negative (none).
The passage explicitly states universal negative is nobler than particular affirmative, confirming the ranking.
4
Apply the inferior premise principle
The conclusion takes the less noble attribute from each pair: if one premise is universal and the other particular, the conclusion is particular; if one is affirmative and the other negative, the conclusion is negative.
The author states 'the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises'.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Use a Fiqh syllogism: major premise 'Every intoxicant is haram' (A), minor premise 'This drink is intoxicating' (I). The conclusion is 'This drink is haram' (I) because the minor premise is particular.
The principle prevents unwarranted universal conclusions from mixed premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني ينتج السالبة الكلية وهي أشرفُ من الموجبة الجزئية؛ لأنَّ شرفَ الكلية لكونه من وجوه متعددَةٍ، كَكَوْنِهِ شَامِلاً، ومَضْبُوطاً، ونافِعًا في العلوم، أزيد من شَرَفِ الموجَبَةِ الجزئية. والثالثُ - يُنتِجُ المُوَجَبَةَ الجزئية وهي أَشْرَفُ من السالبة الجزئيَّةِ؛ لأَنَّ فِيهِ شَرَفاً واحِدَاً - وهُوَ الإيجاب (٢). وليسَ في نَتِيجَةِ الرابع شيءٌ من الشَّرَفَيْنِ". (١) أسباب كون الكلية أشرف من الجزئية. (۲) الشرف في الإيجاب هنا وهو أشرف من السلب. (۳) لأن النتيجة فيها السلب والجزئية. وقد سبق أن وضحت أن الشريفة هي الكلية والموجبة والخسيسة هي الجزئية والسالبة. والنتيجة تتبع أخس المقدمتين.
And the second produces the universal negative, and it is nobler than the particular affirmative; because the nobility of universality, due to it being from multiple aspects, such as being comprehensive, precise, and beneficial in the sciences, is greater than the nobility of the particular affirmative. And the third produces the particular affirmative, and it is nobler than the particular negative; because in it there is one nobility – which is affirmation (2). And there is not in the conclusion of the fourth any of the two nobilities. (1) Reasons for universality being nobler than particularity. (2) The nobility is in affirmation here, and it is nobler than negation. (3) Because the conclusion in it is negation and particularity. And it has already been clarified that the noble ones are the universal and the affirmative, and the base ones are the particular and the negative. And the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage from Fawaid Fanari discusses the nobility (sharaf) of the four types of categorical propositions that can appear as conclusions in syllogisms. The author states that the second mood (producing universal negative) yields a conclusion nobler than the third (producing particular affirmative). The reason is that universality has multiple aspects of excellence: it is comprehensive, precise, and beneficial in the sciences. The third mood produces particular affirmative, which is nobler than particular negative because affirmation is a single aspect of nobility. The fourth mood's conclusion (particular negative) has no nobility at all because it combines particularity and negation. The author then clarifies that the universal and affirmative are considered noble, while the particular and negative are base. Finally, a general principle is stated: the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises. For example, in a syllogism with a universal affirmative major and a particular affirmative minor, the conclusion will be particular affirmative, not universal. This principle is crucial for determining the quality and quantity of conclusions. In Islamic logic, this ranking helps evaluate the epistemic value of syllogistic arguments.

The Six Certain Premises: Classification Criteria
Author enumerates and classifies the six types of certain premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given certain premise into one of the six types using the criteria of sense and middle term.

Core Idea
Certainty in premises comes in six kinds, distinguished by how the mind relies on sense and logical intermediates.

In the Islamic logical tradition, a demonstration (burhan) must rest on certain premises (yaqiniyyat). Scholars like al-Fanari classify these into six types based on two key questions: Does the mind need sensory input? Does it need a middle term? By learning this classification, you gain the ability to identify the epistemological foundation of any premise used in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first main division
The passage divides certain premises into those that require no sense assistance and those that do. This is the highest-level split.
Sense assistance refers to any use of external or internal senses.
2
Analyze the 'no sense' branch
Within the first branch, look for reliance on a middle term (wasat). Awwaliyyat require no middle term; qadaya qiyasatuha ma‘aha require one but it is inherent.
Example of awwaliyyat: 'The whole is greater than the part.'
3
Analyze the 'with sense' branch
This branch splits based on whether certainty after sensation depends on something else. Mahsusat are immediate; the sixth type (e.g., mutawatirat) depends on additional conditions like recurrent transmission.
The text cuts off on the sixth type, but it typically involves hearing.
4
Subdivide mahsusat
Within mahsusat, separate by sense type: external senses give mushahadat (observed), internal senses give wijdaniyyat (experienced).
Internal sense examples: hunger, fear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام البرهان اليقينيات) (واليقينيات ستة أقسام): لأن حكم العقل بها إما بلا استعانة من الحس أو معها، والأول إن لم يتوقف على وسط حاضر في الذهن فهو: الأوليات (وتسمى البديهيات أو الضروريات تدرك طبيعة بدون دليل). وإن توقف: فهو قضايا قياساتها معها. والثاني: إما أن لا يتوقف اليقين به بعد الإحساس على شيء واحد أو يتوقف. فالأول المحسوسات، فالإحساس إن كان بالحس الظاهر فهو: المشاهدات. وإن كان بالحس الباطن فهو: الوجدانيات (الوجدانيات مثل: الجوع والعطش والخوف والغضب). وإن توقف فالحس إما حس السمع وهو: (النص مقطوع)
(Sections of demonstration: certainties) (And the certainties are six sections): because the mind's judgment of them is either without assistance from sense or with it. The first: if it does not depend on a middle term present in the mind, then it is: primary premises (and they are called self-evident or necessary, perceived naturally without proof). If it depends, then it is: premises whose syllogisms are with them. The second: either the certainty regarding it after sensation does not depend on a single thing or it depends. The first: sensibles. Then the sensation: if it is by the external sense, then it is: observed premises. If it is by the internal sense, then it is: experiential premises (experiential premises like: hunger, thirst, fear, anger). And if it depends, then the sense is either the sense of hearing, and it is: (text cut off)
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage classifies the six types of certain premises (yaqiniyyat) that serve as the basis for demonstration (burhan). The classification is based on two criteria: the role of sense (hiss) and the need for a middle term (wasat). The first main division is between premises that require no sense assistance and those that do. Among those without sense: if they do not depend on a middle term present in the mind, they are primary premises (awwaliyyat), also called self-evident (badihiyyat) or necessary (daruriyyat). If they do depend on a middle term, they are premises whose syllogisms are inherent with them (qadaya qiyasatuha ma‘aha), meaning the conclusion is so closely tied to the premises that they are almost self-evident, like 'two is a number' or 'the whole is greater than the part'. The second main division involves sense assistance. Here, if certainty is achieved immediately after sensation without depending on any other thing, these are sensibles (mahsusat). Within sensibles: if the sensation is via external senses (sight, hearing, etc.), they are observed premises (mushahadat). If via internal senses (hunger, thirst, etc.), they are experiential premises (wijdaniyyat). If certainty after sensation depends on something else, then the sense is hearing, leading to the sixth type (likely mutawatirat, recurrent reports), but the text is cut off. Thus the six types are: awwaliyyat, qadaya qiyasatuha ma‘aha, mahsusat (which include mushahadat and wijdaniyyat as subdivisions), and the sixth involving hearing. The passage provides the key structure of the classification of yaqiniyyat in the tradition.

The Six Types of Certain Premises (Yaqiniyyat)
Author classifies certain premises into six types with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given certain premise into one of the six yaqiniyyat by applying the decision tree from the passage.

Core Idea
Certain premises are divided by whether they rely on sense, intellect, or both.

In demonstrative proof (burhan), not all premises are equal. Some are self-evident, others depend on the senses or on repeated experience. The author provides a systematic classification of six certain premises (yaqiniyyat) that form the foundation of certain knowledge. By understanding this classification, you will be able to evaluate the epistemic basis of any premise used in Islamic theological or legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
First dichotomy: intellect alone vs. with sense
The author splits certain premises into two main categories: those that rely only on the intellect without sensory assistance, and those that require sense perception.
2
Intellect-only branch: check for middle term
Within the intellect-only category, if the intellect judges without needing a middle term, it is awwaliyat (self-evident). If a middle term is needed but present in the mind, it is qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha.
3
Sense branch: check if certainty depends on additional conditions
For premises involving sense, first see if certainty after sensation requires something extra. If not, they are simply mahsusat, split into external (mushahadat) and internal (wijdaniyyat) observations.
4
Additional conditions: hearing, repetition, or intuition
If additional conditions are needed: if the sense is hearing, it's mutawatirat (mass transmission). If it requires repetition of observations, it's mujarrabat. If it requires intuition (hads), it's hadsiyyat.
5
Apply the decision tree to new examples
Take a premise like 'The world is temporally originated' (based on observation of change). Which branch? It involves senses (observation of change) and requires repetition? Actually, it's mujarrabat if based on repeated observation, or awwaliyat if argued from the impossibility of a thing causing itself? Practice with examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام البرهان اليقينيات) (واليقينيات ستة أقسام): لأَنَّ  ُحكْمَ العَقل بها إِمَّا بلا اسْتعَانَةٍ من الحِس أَوْ معها، والأَوَّلُ إنْ لَمْ يتوقف على وَسَط حاضِرٍ فِي الذَّهْنِ فهوَ: الأوليات) وإن توقف: .. فَهوَ قضايا قياسَاتُها مَعَها. والثاني: إِمَّا أَنْ لا يَتَوقَّفَ اليَقِينُ بِهِ بَعدَ الإِحْساسِ على شَيءٍ واحد أو يتوقف. فالأَوَّلُ المَحْسوسات، فالإحْساسُ إِنْ كَانَ بالحِسِّ الظَّاهِرِ (۲) فهو :٣. المُشاهَدَاتُ. وإنْ كانَ بالحِس الباطِنِ فَهُوَ الوُجْدَانِيَّاتُ . (۳) وإن توقف فالحسُّ إِمَّا حِسُّ السَّمْع وهُوَ :٤. المتَواتِراتُ فإنّها تتوقَّفُ على حُكْمِ العَقْلِ بِامْتَناعِ تَواطِئ المُخْبِرِينِ على الكَذِبِ (1) أو غيرُه فإِنْ تَوقَّفَ عَلَى تَكرارِ المُشَاهَدَاتِ ه. فالمُجَرَّبات و إِنْ توقَّفَ على الحِدْسِ)٦. فالحدسيات وهذا وَجْهُ الضَّبْطِ لا الحَصْر العَقْلِيُّ ، وإلى تَعْدَادِهَا أشار بقوله: ١. (أَحَدُها أَوَّلِيَاتٌ؛ كقولنا: الواحد نصفُ الاثنين، والكلُّ أعظَمُ من الجزء).
Divisions of demonstrative certainties: The certainties are six divisions. Because the judgment of the intellect regarding them is either without assistance from sense or with it. The first, if it does not depend on a middle term present in the mind, then it is: the primary propositions. And if it depends, then it is propositions whose syllogisms are with them. The second: either the certainty after sensation does not depend on a single thing or depends. The first: the sensed things. Sensation, if it is with the external sense, then it is: the observations. And if with the internal sense, then it is the found things. And if it depends, then the sense is either the sense of hearing, and it is: the mutawatirat, for they depend on the intellect's judgment of the impossibility of the informants conspiring upon falsehood; or other than it. So if it depends on repetition of observations, then it is the experienced things. And if it depends on intuition, then it is the intuitive things. And this is a method of organization, not a rational exhaustive enumeration. And to their enumeration he alluded by his saying: 'One of them is primary propositions, like our statement: one is half of two, and the whole is greater than the part.'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage classifies the six types of certain premises (yaqiniyyat) used in demonstrative proof (burhan). The author begins by dividing judgments of the intellect into those made without assistance from the senses and those made with it. For the first category, if the intellect does not need a middle term (i.e., an intermediate concept) present in the mind, then the premises are called awwaliyat (primary or self-evident propositions). If they do require a middle term, they are qadaya qiyasatuha ma'aha (propositions with their syllogisms built in). For the second category (judgments made with sense assistance), the author further divides based on whether certainty after sensation requires something else or not. If no further condition is needed, these are mahsusat (sensed things), which split into mushahedat (observations via external senses) and wijdaniyyat (found or internal sensations like hunger or fear). If certainty does require something additional, then the sense involved may be hearing, leading to mutawatirat (massively transmitted reports), which rely on the rational judgment that it is impossible for all transmitters to agree upon a falsehood. If the requirement is repetition of observations, these are mujarrabat (empirical or experienced things); if it is intuition (hads), these are hadsiyyat (intuitive things). The author concludes by stating that this is a method of organization (dabt) rather than a rational exhaustive enumeration (hasr 'aqli), and he cites the example often given for awwaliyat: 'one is half of two' and 'the whole is greater than the part.' Understanding this classification is crucial for recognizing the epistemological foundations of demonstrative arguments in Islamic logic.

C) al-Awwaliyat (self-evident propositions)
D) al-Mushahadat (observations)

Key Takeaway
Certain premises are organized by their dependence on intellect and senses, with six distinct types that serve as the building blocks of demonstrative proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following premise into one of the six yaqiniyyat: 'The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) performed the miraj, as reported by a large number of companions in a way that precludes collusion.' Explain your reasoning using the decision tree.
?
Identify which type of certain premise is used in the statement: 'Every human is mortal, based on repeated observation of humans dying throughout history.'
